THE RED PAGE.
”m-yOW .. . the Scotch have been criticised,
X and the Irish ; and it comes to the Eng-
-1 1 lishinan’s turn. But the Englishman
won’t mind. Not because he is un-
touched ; but because he does n’t care. And that
not-caring is a mark of the essential English
superiority—in some things. Each of the three
Island Races has its good points and its bad ones ;
and each will welcome praise and defy blame.
But, when the Scot is criticised, however justly,
many Scots grow sullen; when the Irishman is
criticised, some Irishmen grow enraged. And
the Englishman ? He despises criticism ; he feels
himself above it; all arrows are blunted by his
armour of pride or indifference. Let the galled
jade wince : his withers are unwrung.
When you deal with the English type, you need
not take account of humourless individuals. When
you deal with the Scotch and Irish types you
must be careful. The want of humour exposes
itself as really-a want of logical faculty. Humour
is essentially a perception of incongruity, and the
faculty that detects incongruity is the faculty of
logical classification. A while ago it was denied
that Burns was the greatest poet that ever lived.
The average Englishman, told that Shakespeare
was not the greatest poet that ever lived, simply
shrugs his shoulders. But Burns! . . . the jade
winces— i.e., admits the need of defence i.e.,
admits his own doubt of the validity of the defence.
“ The Irish are the most savage of quasi-civilised
races.” And a humourless Irishman winces.
Now, mind you, that allegation is perfectly con-
sistent with other well-known allegations that
the Irish are among the bravest of races, that
their isle is the greenest, their women the fairest
—and so on, all reasonable, and all beside the
question. Blit humourless people cannot under-
stand that expressio unius is n o t ajfirmatio altirius,
unless that other js a precise logical opposite.
When we say that the big race-class A includes
the little race-features a, b, c, mark that we say
nothing at all about race-features d, e, / and the
rest. Yet one continually meets, in many phases,
the fallacy which takes for granted that if a, a
supposed bad feature, is included in a class, there-
fore d, a supposed good feature, is not included.
Another example, for clearer understanding of
the obvious. The Bulletin was formerly con-
sidered by some narrow people “a scandalous
paper.” (“Formerly” —because opinion has ap-
parently changed. In Sydney Telegraph of 28th
February, for instance, the Palace Theatre ad-
vertised at the head of its list of complimentary
press notices—
Bulletin says: “One commendable feature of ‘The
Wrong Mr. Wright’ is the fact that all its fun is CLBAIS
and WHOLESOME from the beginning- to the end of the
piece.”
—making The Bulletin, you observe, a criterion
of what is “clea,n and wholesome.”) When
Hebblethwaite’s book of verses, breathing simple
devotion to God and published by The Bulletin,
reached some narrow people, their astonishment
was great. Evep the two or three IMaoriland
writers under solemn league and covenant never
to offer verses to The Bulletin —because it was
“such a scandalous paper —found it necessary
to revise their impressions. From letters and
comments it appeared that into their narrow
minds had at last filtered a conception of Breadth
I Breadth which includes “piety” and “im-
” alike as manifestations of the Human, each
iv of appreciation from the standpoint of
alism. or of literature. These humourless,
Sal critics had been arguing that because The
tin 'included h, i, j , therefore it could not
>ly include <c,‘y, z.

war, in fact,’ “ has been a tale' of shame and

lat is a stimulating opening. Then it is de-

is dead ; that old-fashioned English sport is

s upon only one stop —the bathotic stop.
b learnt to sing songs of Araby in one squalid
every morning, and he has since been able to

rculation.” An effective chapter deals with
le Employed Person.”
e English are a nation of employed persons,
rever you go, from Berwick to Land’s End, you will

big and abiding fact in the average Englishman s

he happy. The notion of working for himself
,1s him ... For a full century the rich mer-
its, enterprising manufacturers, colliery-owners, mill-

s, have been preaching and fomenting this doctrine
very means in their power. To their aid in spreading

Churches. If a man will not work, neither shall he
Servants, obey your masters. Punctuality is the
of business. Be faithful over a few things. Begin

g employed which permeates the beautiful.'Spirit of
English competitor. You will meet hoary and bald-
led Englishmen who will tell you with a quaver that
have been in the employment of one and the same
le, man and boy, for over half a century, sir ! Some-
the Englishman tells you this with a look of pride,
rather expects you to regard him as a sort of marvel,
aver occurs to him that he is really bragging of his
ineptitude—to use Mr. Crosland’s favorite abstrac-
, his own lack of enterprise. The number of Scots
i have been in the employment of one house for forty
■s, least of all the number of Scots who brag about it,

Tie English army and navy are in a bad way,
English churches serve pap to listless women
1 children; the House of Commons is a dull
emblage of mediocrities ; —and the poets !
fhen England was at her highest, when the best in her
i having full play, she produced poets. Eight down
3 the Victorian Era she went on producing them,
in she took to the Slock Exchange, and an ostentatious
of life, and the supply of poets fell off. If we except
Swinburne, who does not belong rightfully to this
sent time, t here is not a poet of any parts exercising
function in England to-day. Furthermore, any book-
er will tell you that the demand for poetry-books by
v writers has practically ceased to exist.
J* J*
Kipling ?
le is still young, and, happily, among the living ; but
cannot be denied that as poet he has outlived his
utation. . . . The bulk of his metrical work is just
>ut as dead and forgotten as the coster-songs of
iter-year. He has not even made a wit; nobody
3tes him, nobody believes in him as a poetical master,
sody wants to hear any more of him. His imitators
ve all gone back to the imitation of better men. If a
>y of verses have a flavour of Kipling about it nowadays,
tors drop it as they would drop a hot ooal. So much
the poet of empire, the poet of the people, the
trical patron of Thomas Atkins, Esq.
Another poet of empire—Mr. W. E. Henley—has fared
i-v little better. “ What can I do for England?” is, I
lieve, still in request among the makers of a certain
,ss of anthology ; but English poetry in the bulk is just
e same as if Mr, Henley had never been. Even the
lderdash about “ m.v indomitable soul ” has fallen out
the itsm loquendi of the young men’s .Christian
sociations and the young men's debating societies.
The Song of the Sword” is sung no longer, “For
igland’s . Sake ” has gone the way of ail truculent war--
ietry, and out of “ Hawthorn arid Lavender ” perhaps a
uple of lyrics remain. Mr. Henley attacked Burns
when Burns had been a century dead. Who will con-
sider it worth while to attack Mr. Henley in, say, the
year 2002. . ,
Possibly the real, true English poet who will in due
course put on the laurel of Mr. Austin, is Mr. Stephen
Phillips. Yet Mr. Stephen Phillips is a purveyor of
metrical notions for the stage, and in his last Ifreat work,
“Ulysses,” I find'him writing- . . . exactly the kmd of
poetry one requires for the cavertt scene of a Mew Year s
pantomime. ...a.’
Possiuiy, again, the real, true English poet is Mr.
William Watson, with his tiresome miimcry of Words-
worth and his high-and-dry style of lyrical architecture.
Mr. Watson is believed to have done great things, but
his rdle now appears to be one of austere silence , he is
what the old writers would have termed a costive poet.
And if his “ Collected Poems ” are to be the end of him,
his end will not be long deferred. Or possibly the one
and only poet our England of to-day would wish to boast
is Mr. Arthur Symons. Mr. Symons writes just the kind
of poetry one might expect of a versifier who in early
youth had loved a cigarette-smoking ballet-girl, and
could never bring himself to repress his passion. . . . Mo
doubt, if the English continue to descend-the moral
Avernus at their present' rate of speed, Mr. Symons will
become, by sheer process of time,-the representative poet
of the nation. ■ „ . ....
For the rest, they aft mean well, and they all aim high ,
but one is afraid that nothing will come of them. There
are Francis Thompson, and Laurence Housman, and
Henry Newbolt, and Laurence Binyon, and F. B. Money-
Coutts, and Arthur Christopher Benson, and \ ictor
Plarr —amiable performers all, but each a standing ex-
ample of poetical shortcoming. Perhaps one ought not-*
to mention Mr. John Davidson and Mr. W. B. Yeats,
because Mr. Davidson is a Scot, and Mr. Yeats, putatively
at least, an Irishman. In some respects these twain
may he considered the pick of the basket, lam con-
strained to admit, however, that neither of them has as
yet fulfilled his earlier promise.
As to fiction —
The high priest and the high priestess who serve to the
right and left of the altar of six-shillingism are as every
one knows, Mr. Hall Caine and Miss Marie Corelli. Each
of them wears a golden ephod, with a breastplate of
jewels arranged to spell out the magic figures One
Hundred Thousand. All the other priests of the Taber-
nacle lobk with awe and envy upon these two, because
the other priests’ breastplates have hard work to spell
out fifty thousand, and some of them do not even achieve
one thousand five hundred. Burnt offerings of Caine and
Corelli therefore fill the place with savour. A pair of
sorrier writers never was on sea or land. Everybody
knows it, nobody denies it, and nobody seems sad
about it.
A chapter on “ Suburbanism ” is good and true,
though of course not the whole truth ; the
Englishman’s drink, food, law and order,
education, and recreation are satirised with
vigour i aud we have an anecdote of the recent
visit of’the King of Portugal. On being asked
what had impressed him most in England he
replied at once, “The roast beef.” “Nothing
else, sir?” enquired his interlocutor. “Yes,”
said the monarch, “ the boiled beef. Even the
Englishman
feels in his hones that something has gone wrong with
him. It may be a slight matter and not heyOnd repair
but there it is. He cannot put his hand on his heart and
say “ I am the fine, substantial, sturdy, truth-speaking,
incorruptible, magnanimous, genial Englishman of half a
century ago'” The flv has crept into the ointment of
his virtue, and the fragrance of it no longer remains.
His attitude at the present moment is the attitude of the
anxious man who perceives that life is a little too much
for him, and keeps on saying, We shall have to buck
up!”
Then .. . the appeal ; .to “ the Colonies
Recognising his need of props and stays and abdominal
belts as it were, the Beloved [Englishman] has latterly
taken to remembering the Colonies. He is now of
opinion that he and his sturdy‘children over-seas should
be “ knit together in bonds of closer unity,” “ to present
an unbroken front to the world,” “ should share the
burdens and glories of Empire,” and so on and so forth.
*Phe Colonies —good bodies ! —saw it all at once. They
had been accustomed to be snubbed and neglected and
left out of count, and they had forgotten to whom they
belonged In his hour of need the Beloved cried, “ ’Elp !
I said I didn’t want you, but I do—l do!” and the
Colonies sent to his aid, at a dollar a day per head, the
prettiest lot of freebooters and undesirable characters
they found themselves able to muster. Later, they sent
several landau loads of premiers and politicians, who
were fed and flattered to their hearts’ content, and went
home no doubt greatly impressed with the English roast
and boiled beef. These gentlemen made speeches in
return for their dinners ; they were- allowed to visit the
Colonial Office and kiss the band of Mr. Chamberlain ,
they saw Peter Robinson s and the tuppenny tube : and
the bonds of Empire have been knit closer ever since.
Not to put too fine a point upon it, the Englishman’s
I attempt to buttress himself up out of the Colonies has
proved a ghastly failure. The scheme fell flat, The
English may want the Colonies, but the Colonies do not
want the English—at any rate on bonds of unity lines.
The banner of Imperialism w hich has waved so gloriously
durino- the past lustrum will have to be furled aqd put
away."’ The great Imperial idea declines to work ; it has
been brought on the political stage half a century too
late. At best it was a fetch, and it has failed, The All-
Beloved will have to find some other way out.
And that, at all events, is one judgment that
Australia pan endorse.

S.T.S. : Tennyson provides a parallel to those Bulletin
verses (ifA/3) about the husband who fought a prize-
fight “ for Dolly and the Kid ”
Thy face across his fancy comes
And gives the battle to his hands :
A moment, while the trumpets blow,
He sees his brood'about thy knee;
The next. Wee fire he meets the foe,
And strikes him dead for thine 'tfid thee !
A French soldier told Henry Kingsley in 1870 that he
could stand any Prussians except the Uhlans ; they were
mostly veterans with families, and were so keen on
getting home again safe that when they came to close
grips they fought like devils.
Wife and weans have been always a fine stimulus
to combat. Fitzsimmons tells how, during his
fight with Corbett (and his wife at the ring-side),
‘‘ I said to myself then and there, ‘lt shall never
be the lot of that woman to be the wife of a
defeated husband.’” But indeed the motive is
pre-historic. The cave-man doubtless fought for
the cave-wife and (probably) the cave-progeny;
and there are traces of the conflict all the way
down the ages. Is the motive pre-anthropic ?
the bull that fights for the heifer: does he fight
also for the calf ? Or is it not the case that Mrs.
Lion defends the cubs, and Mr. defends Mrs., or
his possession of Mrs. ? Don’t remember noting
a case of animal fatherhood (purely) inspiring to
combat.

course not.

Memorandum—
Hubert Church’s just-published book of verses, The
West Wind (No. V of The Bulletin Booklets) was an-
nounced for publication some time ago; but issue has
been delayed by various misadventures. The price is
2s. 6 d.; and two hundred and fifty copies only are printed
for sale. The Bulletin Booklets include work by Boderic
Quinn, Louise Mack, James Hebblethwaite, and Hubert
Church ; and the verses of “ Danton ” will shortly be
added to the series. Some of the sweetest lyrics written
in Australasia are to be found in these dainty booklets,
which now represent quite a substantial poetical perform-
ance.

The more this Christianity changes, the more it is tie
same thing. The apostie Paul, in a time of doubt, a day
of tribulation, did not rely upon God : he appealed to
Caesar. And when N.S.W. churches, in time of droughti
seek a day of humiliation, do they appeal to God? No ;
always to See, sir!
Not when I saw the low, sandy shore and the familiar
straggle of the trees, nor when the harbour disclosed
known headland after headland, bay after well-remem-
bered bay, nor when lit eyes welcomed me upon the
wharf and the faces of the houses took friendly shape,
but when I walked from the ferry-boat at Milson’s Point,
and the charity-harmonium broke into its old monotonous
chant, its worn and dolorous wailing, then first and at
last I knew I was at Home !•

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the Beginning.
Tn the beginning, God made things
Perfect in harmony, apt and fit.
Next came the Devil, on subtle wings,
Scoffed at the scheme, and then tangled it.
So our theology says, and I,
Skipping the difficult spots, agree , , ,
Surely the Devil’s success went nigh
To the prodigious, if God made me 1
God took an exquisite soul, some clay
Fit for work of the finest kind ;
God put a heart that was blithe and gay
Under control of an excellent mind.
Down in his woikshop Satan had got*
All the mean qualities on a shelf:
He mixed ’em up, and poured all the lot
Into God’s man . . . I’m not proud of myself !
T,
F.M.
In the Grey.
I’m out of love with Love, and all
My soul that sang, decays in curses.
There is no worse thing can befall:
I’m out of love with love, and all
Things else scarce count. Unmusical,
Bitter and barren now my verse is.
I m out of love—with Love and all!
My soul that sang, decays in curses.
TAB.
FRANK MORTON.
A Garden.
’Twas a garden of deep shade,
With many a quiet nook,
Where the summer breeze oft strayid
And the wild acacia shook.
There was always a wind In the trees
That shaded the garden sweet,
Ai d sheltered the frail heartsease
From the summer’s tyrant hi at.
It fanned all the drooping flowers
With a subtle fiagrance rare
It had caught from the dewy showers,
And breathed on the sun-dried air.
When a wild-eyed, wondering child,
Often I’d careless stepped
Where the n ignonette ran wild
And the honeysuckle crept;
Where the heavy*headed flowers,
Slow i olding above my head,
Dreamed a. d slept through the hours
In the brilliant poppy-bed.
On the outskirt used to stand
Where the blooms were sparse and few,
A straight-thewed gum-tree band,
With the sea-wind murmuring through.
Dear garden ! where the bright,
Long days of Liberty,
Gilded with Love’s delight,
Wove round my love and me.
The trees are gone from the rise
By the side of the quiet sea;
Vanished the dear, dark eyes,
And my garden of memory.
SPIX.
The Dreamer.
The white-robed angels once a spirit led
Before the Lord of all men quick and dead.
“ W 1 you done with Life ?” he asked the
SouL
“Master, I have done naught,” the Spirit said.
“ I dreamt of Perfect Love, of Life pure, true,
And strove in Song to give my dreams anew
To men who had forgot Gain is not all:
They passed me by, some listened, they were few.
“At last I woke, too late, my life had flown.
Father, have mercy ! I my deep sin own.”
Then answer came, “What men call Life is
dream—
Your dreams of Perfect Love are Life alone.”
N.S.W.
W. M. MAILLER.
Success.
Oh, long and dark the stairs I trod
With trembling feet to find my God,
Gaining a foothold bit by bit,
Then slipping back and losing it;
Never progressing, striving still
With weakening grasp and fainting will,
Bleeding to climb to my God, while he
Serenely smiled, unnoting me.
Then came a darker time when I
Loosened my hold and fell thereby.
Down to the lowest steps my fall,
As if I had not climbed at all;
And while I lay despairing there
I heard a footfall on the stair,
In the same path where I, dismayed,
Faltered and fell and lay afraid ;
And lo ! when Hope had ceased to be
My God came down the stairs to me.
N.s.vv.
MAGGIE SULLIVAN.

WE PUBLISH EVIDENCE LIKE THIS.
The Case of Mr. A. LYES.
(FOR THE “STANDARD.”)
The requirements of the Australian popu-
lace can only be arrived at by reference to
the statistics which are compiled at various
periods, and as evidence is necessary for the
purpose of gauging popular opinion, it may
here be interesting to listen to Mr. Albert
Lyes, of No. 7 Frazer-street, Leichhardt
“ When living in the North Coast dis-
trict,” said Mr. Lyes, “ I experienced a
vast amount of trouble with my liver and
kidneys, the consequence being that I was
put to a lot of inconvenience owing to the
acute pains across the small of my back.
As I had heavy work to do at the time, my
afflictions came as a great hardship, mere so
if there was any lifting to bs done, when my
back plains were intensified to a degree that
I could scarcely endure.”
Reporter: “ Were you much affected in
any other part ? ”
Mr. Lyes : “ It would be quicker for me
to tell you where I was not affected, for I
had aches and pains all over me. There
was a cold, painful sensation in the spine
which felt as if somebody had shot a ball of
ice along my back, and that horrible shoot-
ing pain extended right to my eyes, which
also gave me the impression of being
swollen, I was sluggish, drowsy, and had
no energy whatever, the only thing I felt
fit for being to lie down—it did not matter
where. slt is a serious matter for a married
man to get into that condition, but I held
out as long as I could, and then I had
to give in, for all the grit was taken out
o(,me. Being without any appetite, which
had been on the wane for a long time, the
chances of stopping a further leakage of my
vitality were very remote, and as I could not
sleep at all well at nights, there was no
doubt that I was in a very grave state. With
my tongue covered with a nasty substance
and a vile taste in my mouth, there is
uothing remarkable in the fact that I could
not enjoy any food, and in the natural
course of events I got a long way thinner
than I ought to have done. I was tor-
mented with very sevex-e headaches which
made me feel so gloomy and wretched that
there seemed to be nothing in the world
worth living for, and with my weakness
daily increasing, my limbs ached so persis-
tently that I could not even take a walk in
comfort. Palpitation of the heart and dizzi-
ness always followed any exertion, my
heart being particularly bad. It used to
make a thumping sound like the noise from
a horse’s hoof heard in the distance, and
many an anxious hour have I passed in
thinking what might be wrong with that
organ. Like most people who suffer like
this for the first tame, I consulted a
doctor, who said that my liver and kidneys
were diseased. He wrote a prescription,
which I had made up, and said I would
have to keep as quiet as possible, and not
exert myself any more than was absolutely
necessary.'
Reporter: “How did you feel after
taking his medicine ? ”
Mr. Lyes : *• Just about the same as
before, and when I saw it was only so much
waste to take any more I dropped it off.
The oppression in my chest and the flatu-
lence in my st omach were still there, and
the urine l-emained of a cloudy and scanty
nature, so what was the use of throwing
any more money away I thought? Besides,
my nervous system was completely out of
order, as any kind of noise had a startling
effect which kept my nerves at extreme
tension, as if they had been suddenly un-
hinged, and then, again, I felt agitated and
irritable at the slightest provocation. The
long-continuance of these sufferings had
made me very morose and low-spirited, for
there were others to think of besides my-
self; but, thank goodness! that trying
ordeal is all over now, and it is not likely
to be repeated, because I discovered a
remedy which cured my ailments then, and
I am sure it could do so again if ever I
needed it.”
Reporter: “ May I learn what the remedy
was ? ”
Mr. Lyes : “ I have no desire to keep the
matter secret, because I might do other
poor sufferers a good turn l«y letting you
know. It was Clements Tonic—a medicine
that I had been reading about—that made
a new man of me, and I always regard it as
my only friend amongst all medicines. I
had only taken a couple of bottles of
Clements Tonic when I felt ever so much
better. In fact, the change was so great
that I could scarcely believe it myself,
especially as I had lost confidence in
physics generally. But Clements Tonic
asserted itself as something uncommonly
good by rapidly giving me a desire for food,
and in the matter of making me sleep well
I thought it a most marvellous medicine.
The health-giving properties of Clements
Tonic were not confined to those improve-
ments, as it amended the defects in my
internal organs which had gone against the
digestion of my food, and by those means
the pains in my chest and shoulders were
relieved. The fermentation of my food
was done away with in like manner, so that
I was getting the full benefit of my meals,
and how well I knew it! for the return of
my strength was quite noticeable. I no
longer desired to lie about in a state of
careless languor, for Clements Tonic gave
me the energy and ambition to do some
work, which was becoming easier to per-
form each day, because the pains in my
back were less troublesome as time moved
on. My heart gave me no occasion to
worry myself, as it was beating with a
regular pulsation, and with the departure
of my headaches my sight became so clear
that I could sit down and read for hours
without the least inconvenience. & I was
greatly pleased with Clements Tonic for
the effect it had on my nervous system,
for it invigorated my nerves so thoroughly
that they became perfectly steady, while
su_h a thing as becoming excited or annoyed
over trifling incidents did not happen. A
good indication to go by as to the efficacy of
Clements lonic was the entire removal of
my deranged kidney symptoms, and it was
then that I was altogether free from pain.”
Reporter: “Did you stop taking your
medicine immediately that happened ? ”
Mr. Lyes : “ Not exactly, for I wanted
to be quite sure that all disease was effaced,
so I took another couple of bottles of
Clements. lonic, and when they were
finished it was not necessary to take any
moie, as I was as strong and thoroughly
lestored as I could wish to be. It is several
years ago since Clements Tonic put an end
to my sufferings, yet my work has never
since interfered with my health—a fact
which shows that Clements Tonic made me
as sound as a bell; and if these statements
are likely to be perused by others, you may
publish them as you like."

SATURDAY, MARCH 14, 1903.
Attorney-General Wise, Whitewashes
The doleful process of whitewashing this
country’s fallen financial state still goes on.
Hardly a day passes now without the informa-
tion that some Australian politician has thrown
a new light on the subject, or invented a new
variation of the old light, or has made a new
and astounding calculation about the value
of “ our magnificent natural resources,” or
has broken out in a new curse against
everybody who disapproves of the loan
boom policy. They are very sad and un-
convincing strugglers against the inevit-
able, but still they do their best to put a
good face on a cheerless situation. The
latest, and one of the most hopeless efforts is
that of N.S.W. Attorney-General Wise, who
has been demonstrating in England that
New South Wales is, after all, not much
more heavily in debt, in proportion to popu-
lation, than is the United Kingdom, if the
value of the United Kingdom's railways is
included in the calculation. And if the process
of including things had been carried a little
further, and the value of Britain’s shipping,
and its private lands, and the cost price of
the Tower of London, and the value of the
property destroyed in the civil wars of King
Stephen’s time, had also been added to the
British debt, there is very little doubt that
B. R. Wise could have proved that Britain
owes a great deal more per inhabitant than
New South Wales does. The situation, as
B. R. Wise puts it, is somewhat as follows :
U. Kingdom. N. S. Wales.
Government debt . ■ £775,000,000 £81,000,000*
Railway companies .. 1,050,000,000 300,000
Municipal debts . • 350,000,000 3,000,000
Water companies .. 60,000,000 200,000
£2,235,000,000 £84,500,000
Population .. .. 42,000,000 1,405,000
Per head .. .. £53/4/- £6O
It looks a nice, convincing argument, and
but for the never-to-be-sufficiently-regretted
fact that Mr. Wise forgot to add on to the
British debt the cost price of the Tower of
London and the breakages done at the land-
ing of Julius Caesar it would have been
even more convincing.
And, after all, -the whole argument is
almost the poorest, thinnest little sophistry
ever invented. It is unworthy to be told by
a rational politician to a rational audience,
and when such arguments are laid before a
meeting of London financiers they can only
cause derision. A country that hasn’t a
better delusion to offer than that had
better buy a pick and take to honest labor.
The difference between the situation of
Britain and that of N.S.W. lies in the fact
that Britain owes itself £53 per inhabitant
while New South Wales owes somebody else
£6O per inhabitant—not reckoning private
foreign debts, which raise the figures to
far above that sum. Britain owns its
railways, while Australia’s railways are
really owned by Bull, the mortgagee. The
interest on the 2235 millions, which represent
Britain’s public and municipal debt and its
railway and water capital, is paid locally,
and spent locally, and is subject to British
taxation. The interest on N.S.W.’s liability
of millions is mostly paid abroad, is.
spent abroad, and is exempt from N.S.W.
taxation. England is subject to the local
capitalist; Australia is the bond slave of the
absentee. If Britain’s public debt was held
in France, and its municipal debts in the
United States, and its railways were owned
in Germany, and its water supply companies
belonged to Dutchmen, and all these had to.
be exempted from taxation in order to pacify
the French and Yankee and German and
Dutch owners, the British and the Australian
situations would be parallel. As things
stand there is no resemblance between them
whatsoever.
• • • •
Europe, in the bad old Middle Ages, and
Latin America to a much later date, were
overshadowed by a power which owned
a stupendous proportion of the country’s
wealth, and which refused to pay taxes,
whereby a crushing burden wasthrown on the
remainder of the community. That power
was the Church. Australia is rearing a similar
curse ; but it isn’t the Church —it is Cohen.
Australia’s foreign State bond-holder has the
same exemption from Australian taxation
that the Church used to have in France,
Spain, Italy, and Mexico, and he is growing
to be just as overshadowing. And whereas
the Church, at all events, spent most of its
revenues locally, Cohen spends his else-
where. Australia is now, in proportion to
population, the great absentee - owned
country of the earth. Its alleged policy of
State socialism is a sham. It has less real
State socialism now than England possesses.
In England the railways, water-works, public
liabilities, &c., are at least mainly owned by
Englishmen. In Australia, railways, tele-
graphs, water-supply, culverts, bridges,
roads, harbor works, &c., &c., are owned
by foreigners, the Government being merely
the agent, factor, bailiff, or deputy of the
foreign owner. The British railway share-
holder put up the money to build his own
railways*; the Australian railway shareholder
(i.e., the general taxpayer) has consistently
refused to do so, and regards it as a ridiculous
insult that he should be expected to do so.
Yet he fondly imagines that he owns the
property he didn’t pay for. There is no
grander ideal than State ownership of all
public works; if N.S.W. had no debt, then
uvery family of five would own about £250
worth of wealth as its permanent and in-
alienable share of the State’s assets ; and it is
difficult to imagine anything better than that
as a beginning of “ Socialism in our Time.”
And just because the Labor party doesn’t
make the total cessation of borrowing and
the repayment of the existing debt the first
plank of its political platform, it either
knows nothing or cares nothing for social-
ism in our time, or in anybody else’s time.
.
The British policy of the private owner-
ship of land, railways, waterworks, &c., has
led to a horrible inequality between the rich
and the poor in England. The Australian
policy of private ownership of railways,
roads, bridges, harbours, telegraphs, &c., by
the British money-lender, with the State as
his intermediary and rent-collecting agent,
is leading to the same inequality here, only
the poor are in Australia and the rich mono-
polist is in England, and, being out of sight,
he attracts little or no attention. There is
no evil in the way of monopoly and social
inequality with which England is cursed that
Australia is not toiling to reproduce—with
the absentee curse as an additional drawback.
Australia has, for many years, been selling
its whole future for a little extra ease and a
little less trouble in the present. Now, in
its time of slump, it hasn’t produced one
eminent politician who is prepared to urge
the people to reform, though it has produced
dozens who are toiling to prove that there is
no need of reform. And among this noble
and disorganised army of whitewashers, the
saddest and most disappointing specimen is
B. R. Wise, who holds that, because Britain
owes £53 per head to itself, it is quite right
that we should owe £6O per head to some-
body else ; and that, because Britain owns
its railways, and its own public liabilities,
and is solvent, therefore N.S.W., which
doesn't own its railways or its public liabili-
ties, must be solvent also.
• * f *
Australia is in one or other of two posi-
tions. If it can’t go on without foreign bor-
rowing then it is insolvent, and its insol-
vency will become apparent just as soon
as the loan money stops coming in. If it
can get on without it, then it is still solvent,
but is cursed with a gang of cowardly and
incompetent politicians, and is the home of
a shiftless and incompetent people. In the
former case, it can’t meet its liabilities ; in
the latter it can, but whether, after all the
years of easy-going drift, it will brace up
and take the trouble to do so is another
matter. In a country where the debt is held
locally, there is a large bond-holding class
whose vote always goes for paying up
honestly at any cost, and when, as in Britain,
the bond-holding class is also the governing
-class, public solvency is especially assured.
In a country where the debt is mos y
abroad there is no such class and no such
incentive to public honesty. Attorney-
General Wise has volubly assured the British
public that we have in no way derogated
from the honesty of our British fore-
fathers, and in that respect he may
possibly speak the truth. Like our British
forefathers we are honest when it clearly
pays to be so, and at other times we are
doubtful. There is hardly a treaty which
Britain has made that it didn’t break just as
.soon as it found that by so doing the gain
would be its own and the loss would be the
other fellow’s. But if it broke faith with its
public creditor the ruling class would stand
most of the loss and have the disgrace as
well, so on that point British faith is now
founded on a rock. (While the King was
the governing power, however, and repudia-
tion meant his profit and somebody elses
loss, the British public debt was repudiated
pretty regularly.) If Australia ever decided
that it was cheaper to compound with its
creditors than to face its liabilities it would
be just in the same situation as Britain
occupied when it broke its solemn treaty
with the Orange Free State and stole the
Diamond Fields ; it would have the profit
and the loss would be another person’s share.
Under these circumstances even Mr. Wise s
assurance counts for less than might appear
on the surface. Australia has already found
that its public men can’t be trusted to deal
honestly with a sinking fund or a civil service
superannuation fund when there is a very
slight temptation to be otherwise. How
they will pan out in larger matters when a
larger temptation comes along only time can
tell.
Some Aspects of the Tariff Issue.
Mr. E. W. Owe’Sullivan, N.S.W. Minister for
Works, writes as follows: —
Under the above heading your Feb. 28th issue con-
tained certain statements concerning my attitude towards
Protection, which are both incorrect and unfair to me.
I never dealt with any tender for 75,000 barrels of cement
for the new reservoir in connection with the Sydney
Water Supply, but I did decide one for 50,000 barrels, the
whole of which went to N.S.W. manufacturers. I never
first proposed to purchase the whole of this supply of
50,000 barrels from the foreigner. With regard to the
successful tenders, no indignant representation by the
Chamber of Manufacturers was ever laid before me, nor
was there any threat of a public agitation. How could
there be when the local manufacturers got the tender ?
I have had, however, threats from the foreigner. There
has, therefore, been no betrayal of Protection by me, but
there has been a want of accuracy in your informant,
which shows that he knows but little of the subject upon
which he grossly misled you. As a matter of fact, I have
on many occasions helped local manufacturers by giving
them orders, and this, I think, they will be willing to
admit. With regard to Protection and democracy, The
Bulletin need be under no doubt as to my loyalty to
either. An 18 years’ record in Parliament, and more than
three years’ administration as Minister for Works, ought
to afford many proofs on these points.
• • » •
The Bulletin’s information was from a member
of the Chamber of Manufacturers, and was
endorsed by a responsible officer of that body. It
was accordingly used without very close scrutiny.
Mr. Owe’Sullivan’s denial has led to a searching
and complete investigation into the whole question
—and thatshowsthat Mr. Owe’Sullivan canrightly
contradict this paper on certain inaccuracies of
detail, while on the actual facts The Bulletin
hardly blamed the Owe’Sullivan enough. That
mistake will now be rectified. When the Common-
wealth tariff on cement was imposed in October,
1901, with the object of allowing Australian
manufacturers to pay decent wages to their
workmen, and yet compete with the foreign
article produced at wage-rates equal to only one-
third of the Australian level, the N.S.W. Works
Department, then (and still) under the control of
Mr. Owe’Sullivan, sought to go behind the
intentions of the Australian Parliament by calling
for tenders for cement in bond. In bond the
German article could undercut the local, though
in the open market of Sydney the_ local could
undercut the duty-paid foreign article. These
tactics lasted some 12 months, during which
time local manufacturers were given no chance of
competing with theforeigners. The while, however,
the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners, with a much
clearer idea of public duty, called for tenders
for their supplies on a tariff-paid basis, and
accordingly were supplied exclusively by local
manufacturers. Local manufacturers protested
repeatedly to Mr. Owe’Sullivan, but without
avail. He was asked, by way of gentle satire, if
the foreign producers, to whom the actions of his
department practically confined the patronage of
the Government in cement contracts, would be re-
quired to pay the minimum wage enforced on local
Government contractors, and he replied that he
had no means of enforcing a minimum wage in
Germany.
The N.S.W. Works Department and the
Owe’Sullivan were, doubtless, influenced by
these considerations: —
(1) The N.S.W. Govt, was, at the time, contending
that no duty could be collected by the Australian Govt,
on goods imported by a State Govt. In the event of that
contention being upheld, the whole of the duty paid by it
on its cement imports would be returned.
2. In any event, the Braddon clause ensured the return
of three-fourths of the duty paid ; and, since the Aus-
tralian Government was not at the time using its full
one-fourth of the total customs, virtually the whole duty
would come back.
3. The duty would be paid in the first instance out of
borrowed nionej'—the cement being used on works con-
structed out of loan funds ; the return, whether in part
or in whole, of the duty Would be engulphed in the con-
solidated revenue and be another proof “ of the elasticity
of the State’s finances,” &c., &c
These considerations—as regards Nos. 1 and 2
at least—are probably defensible on Freetrade
grounds ; and No. 3, whilst in the light of private
commercial methods absurdly reckless and dis-
honest, is quite in keeping with the established
traditions of Australian public finance. They show
as objectionable only when examined in the light of
a Protectionist and National ideal; and then they
appear as quite inadequate excuses for the thrift-
less and heartless system of keeping the local
workman out of a job so as to get it done at a
nominally cheaper rate by an alien. But that was
the policy of the N.S.W. Works Department
until, finally, representatives of local industry
brought the matter under the notice of Premier
See. Then, at a conference between them, the
Premier and Minister Owe’Sullivan, the system
of giving unfair preference to the foreigner was
ended, for the while at any rate. Contracts since
then—including that .for the Prospect dam—have
gone to local manufacturers.
As the protest and negotiations were kept
private, ana as there was, after their happy ter-
mination, no reason for any party to feel aggrieved
and to complain publicly, it is not surprising that
the current account of the matter should have
become a little confused in details, whilst not in
any way exaggerating the root of the grievance.
The Bulletin is sorry to have reproduced in
print any of the confusion ; more sorry that the
details makes the case worse for Minister of
Works Owe’Sullivan, for, as the Owe’Sullivan
points out, he hat done good work for Protection
and democracy in the past, has helped to cultivate
a healthy, national feeling. But it would seem as
if, of late, borrowing had dulled the edge of his
democratic husbandry.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Slander of “Joey” Carrathers.
Thl following is taken from the Sydney Tele-
graph’s report of the recent speech of “Joey”
Carruthers, when he orated at Paddington
(N.S.W.) on the subject of “Our Misgoverned
Country ”:
The Federal and State Governments had bandied
backwards and forwards their responsibilities in the
matter of suspending the fodder duties. They had trifled
with the people while the great staple industry of the
State was being ruined. One consequence—there was
less work for and less revenue from the Government
railways; another consequence—the Government could
not get its rents from its tenants on the land. Then the
wheat crop failed. Here again the Federal Government
came to the rescue by taxing breadstuffs to the tune of
£250,000 1 They had fattened on the poverty and troubles
of the needy.
The above may be, to some extent, the misre-
presentation of a hurried reporter, and may not
be exactly what the orator said. But if it is what
he said, then “Joey ” Carruthers was guilty of a
mean and shameful political lie, and a grossly
wicked slander, and he doesn’t understand the
meaning of fair play. The Federal Government
didn’t “ fatten on the food duties it collected
in N.S.W. It is no fatter than if there had
been no food duties at all. It isn’t a penny
richer for the collection of these duties, for
as it has never taken anything like the full 25 per
cent, of the customs and excise revenue to which
it is entitled, it would have had just as much re-
venue as it has now if there had been no food
duties. Instead of “fattening” on the N.S.W.
food duties, the economical Treasurer Tup.ner
carefully returned the money to N.S.W., to be
available for the supply of relief to the drought-
stricken settlers. Of course if the N.S.W.
Government thought fit to refund the duties to
those who paid them, then N.S.W. would be
importing its food absolutely free —but it didn’t
feel so inclined. The Federal Government
couldn’t abolish the food duties, for its act would
have had to apply to the whole Commonwealth,
and the other States (it maybe well, just here, to
inform “Joey” Carruthers that N.S.W. isn’t
the whole Commonwealth) objected. And
N.S.W., which could have wholly abolished these
duties as regards its own territory, merely at
the cost of losing the revenue they supplied,
refused to do so. It was wise in so acting. The
drought reduced the revenue from many sources.
It caused a loud demand for increased expenditure
in relief, and the money for relief must come from
somewhere. To levy a small tax on the food of
useless and comparatively affluent city non-pro-
ducers like Carruthers (for the imported food
was mostly used in the city) was as good a way as
any to get over the difficulty. Certainly it was
better at the moment than putting an extra tax
on the drought-stricken farmer’s land just when
his last horse had died and his family was clothed
in bagging. But, however that may be, the
Federal Government did not “fatten” in any
sense on the N.S.W. food duties. The re-
mark, if it is correctly reported, only goes
to show how much the parish-minded poli
tician hates the idea of Australian unity,
and how far he will go in disregard of the
truth in order to malign and misrepresent the
Australian Government. The poor, inferior,
tenth-rate parish pump politician in office charges
the Australian Government with all his failures.
The same man, who expects to be in office, charges
his prospective failures to the Australian Govern
ment in advance. If it supplies enough revenue
to provide the means of relieving the drought
ruined settlers it is a cruel tyrant levying “oppres-
sive taxation if it doesn’t levy “oppressive taxa
tion,” then it is an unfeeling institution which
won’t supply the means to relieve the drought
cursed selector. And when the parochial poli
tician comes to a bad hitch in his oration he
always curses the Australian Government in a
genera] way till he can think what to say next-
just as he might curse the Chinaman or the Pope.
♦
Kingston Speaks.
When Customs Minister Kingston faced an
Adelaide audience, last week, the reception given
to him was that which always awaits the Man in
politics. Men —real men—are rare in Parliamen-
tary life, the general rule of which seems to be
progress by tortuous intrigue, by corrupt
favoritism, by palsied conciliation of enemies,
rather than by straightforward and manly advo-
cacy of principle. But it is strange that this
rarity should continue whilst there is such frequent
proof that there is no surer path to the hearts of
the people than that of the courageous man who
goes straight. The shuffling, clowning, con-
ciliating, dodging politician has his small
or great triumphs, according as he can
shuffle to the best advantage; but when the
public man appears who is defiant of all those
arts, who neither seeks friends nor compromises,
who is as absolute in principle as politics (neces
sarily tinged with some expediency) allow, the
other sort shrivels before him. The French
Premier, who last year went to the polls pledged
to an uncompromising hostility to the anti-
Republican conspiracies of both clerics and
Chauvinists, courted destruction in the minds of
timorous shufflers ; he ranged against him church
and army, the two greatest forces in the State.
Yet he won. because the people, appealed to
bravely, answered bravely. Kingston, hounded
down almost unanimously by the Australian daily
press, ranging against himself that large section
of the wealthy lower orders which has no desire
to be more honest than is absolutely necessary—
yet finds his way to the hearts of the people, who
respect him for his his sincerity,
his incorruptible honesty, for tne very gruffness
of his challenge to dishonest “ respectability.” His
meeting at Adelaide far exceeded in its enthusiasm
and in its mass all recent demonstrations, and put
completely in the shade the comic entertainment
recently contributed by G. H. Reid. Even the
bitterly hostile Freetrade daily press has to admit
that. And it was solely a tribute to honesty and
courage'in politics, for Adelaide, misled by its news-
papers and by a weak-kneed generation of poli-
ticians, has lately weakened considerably on its
Federal enthusiasm; and finds many occasions,
through its local Jenkins Iscariot, to belittle the


Federal Government. Mr. Kingston waited long,
patiently and silently under such a flood of unjust
censure as has seldom been poured on a public
man. Now that he has broken the silence, the
glowing pride with which his hearers received his
words is as fine a vindication, as complete an
encouragement, as a public man could wish for.

The Victorian Railway Muddle.
Premier Irvine is about to put in practice Ms
great idea that the remedy for the disorganisation
of the Victorian railways lies in giving the
management the same unlimited powers of sack-
ing men without appeal that private employers
enjoy. And in one sense Irvine is quite justified.
The Victorian railways are crowded with incom-
petent men who hold their positions by political
influence, and peremptory powers of “sack,” if
the powers belong to the right kind of man, would
probably shift the great majority of them. The
promotion of many a good man is blocked because
immediately above him there is an incompetent
drunk with what the Americans describe as a
“pull.” But, on the other hand, there is an
aspect of the case which Irvine overlooks.
The private employer seldom sacks a really
valuable man merely because of a personal
prejudice, or because of the man’s religion, or be-
cause he doesn’t grovel sufficiently—the fact that
the loss of the valuable man will be his (the em-
ployer’s) personal loss is a restraining influence,
and even a very incompetent private employer
gets an occasional glimpse of his own interest.
The mere ganger or overseer can let his prejudices
run riot, for the loss of a valuable man, discharged
for insufficient abasement, or for not belonging to
the right brand of superstition, isn’t his loss. The
new system will work well enough—much better
than the old one in fact —if it is intelligently ad-
ministered, and with that end in view it cer-
tainly must not be administered by Bent. Mean-
while a new Canadian Chief Commissioner has
been engaged, and that individual is on his way
to Victoria to take charge. The papers state
that the poor wretch “thoroughly understands
the position with regard to the Victorian railways
and the task which he has before him.” The
Bulletin is prepared to bet a considerable
sum that he doesn’t. When he finds himself
in charge of 3000 miles of rotten railway which
hasn’t been decently repaired for ten years, with
350 good locomotives and 150 cripples, with the
rolling-stock dry-rotted, a staff that isn’t large
enough to clean the engines, a Government that
refuses to pay for repairs and which demands that
he shall earn interest on lines that are closed,
and make lines pay where there is practically no
population through the persistent blocking of
settlement by the big western landlords; when
he finds that the trains have given up trying to be
less than two hours late, and the express is
habitually hauled by a goods engine; when he
finds himself expected to produce vastly better
results out of the railways alone than his native
Canada can show with stupendous land-grants, to
help out the railway revenue ; when he finds him-
self ordered about by crassly ignorant politicians,
saddled with hopeless new lines, and expected
to carry starving stock gratis; and when he
makes the acquaintance of Bent and chaos —the
new manager will probably be a very much
astonished man. Unless the Irvine Government
sent him Bent’s photograph and a full list of
Bent’s language, his knowledge of what lies
before him is very limited indeed.
The Haw, Officah, and Haw, Gentleman.
The notion of the Screwmaker of England, that
Imperialism in its rampant and aggressive form
can be largely supported by levying on “ the
colonies ’’ for men and money is evidently not
shared, so far as concerns the men (unless they
keep in the ranks), by “ the gentlemen of
England”—or, at any rate, that section of
the gentlemen which has been left by its
ancestors with sufficient energy to play polo
and join the army. During the S’African
war very many of the * ‘ colonial ’ recruits who
showed special skill in warfare were secured per-
manently for the service of the Empire by the
grant of com missions in the British army. These
men seemed likely to make good professional
soldiers —the kind who would make, a living out
of the army and help to maintain in reasonable
efficiency the fighting force of a nation, which
seems to have seen its best military days. The
authorities contrived with some wisdom, from
their point of view, but the gentlemen of England
have since upset the little scheme pretty effectu-
ally, and thus removed a very real danger to Aus-
tralian nationalism. Jf the adventurous young
manhood of the Commonwealth could find a good
outlet for its energies in the British army, doubt-
less the military profession would call very many
away. But the officer and gentleman in the
British army stands in the way. He takes up
towards the “ mere colonial ” an attitude.of offen-
sive snobbery, refuses to associate with him,
tries to drive him out of the service by raising
the regimental expenses, and speaks civilly to him
only when suggesting that he should take his
slovenly unhandsome plebianism out of the way
by seeking employment abroad. According to
liondon Daily Mail :
Whenever the War Office issues a circular asking for
“jungle and swamp” volunteers the notice is thrust
before the attention of the colonial officer, and to his fit-
ness for the post, he is told, attention will be specially
drawn. The ranker is to have all the drudgery of the
Empire’s outposts with the prospect of the Sandhurst
man superseding him when the guns begin to shoot.
Here are some of the employments which colonial officers
are going in for, in order to free themselves from an un-
pleasant situation :—Egyptian Army, South African Con-
stabulary, West African Field Force, Colonial Forces,
Chinese Regiment, King’s African Rifles, West India
Regiment.
The “colonial officer,” in short, is Urged to keep
to the kennel and to the kitchen, and leave the
drawing-room to the “officah —haw —and gentle-
man.” This snobbery is probably a bad thing for
Britain, but it’s certainly a good, thing for Aus-
tralia. Then, again, the disgusting fact has now
come to light that the flogging of subordinate
officers by their superiors is largely practised in
the British army, the flogging being.ordered by a
secret and self-appointed court-martial.. Even if
the Australian officer wasn’t promptly driven out of
the service by the caddish and blastiferous
“gentlemen of England” he would. probably
object to stay on these terms and conditions, l his
difficulty requires to be looked into before there is
any further discussion about the advisability of
Australia contributing men to the British army
and navy. When Chamberlain and co. can t
prevent their muddled and ill-regulated forces
from illegally throwing out the Australians who
are already in them, what is the good of Chamber-
lain and co. demanding that more Australians
shall come in ?
General Hutton Again.
General Hutton has been keeping so quiet lately
that memory of his blunders was almost fading
away. Those two very awkward matters await-
ing decision—the Drayton Grange scandal and
the Bush veldt scapegoats scandal—have doubtless
provided reasons why the Imperial officer who
represents British army interests in Australia was
glad to let the public forget his existence for a
while. But General Hutton simply cannot keep
out of trouble, for any length of time, and he has
now plunged into difficulties again with an absurd
attempt to amend the King’s regulations as
regards thepresence of soldiers at public demon-
strations. The regulation in force in Britain on
this point reads:
76. Officers and Volunteers of a Corps or Regiment are
not, individually or collectively, to attend political meet-
ings or join in public political discussions or demonstra-
tions in uniform. Bands of Corps or Regiments are not
to appear in uniform for any purpose without the consent
of the Commanding Officers of their Corps or Regiments.
That has been found to work satisfactorily enough
in Britain. But at someone’s foolish instigation,
General Hutton (Commonwealth Gazette pro-
clamation, 27/2/’O3) applies an amended regulation
to Australia’s citizen soldiery.
Officers and soldiers are forbidden to institute or, when
in uniform, attend any meeting, demonstration, or pro-
cession for any religious or political purpose.
This regulation also applies to Bands of Regiments or
Corps.
The absurdity of this is easily seen. It would
prevent Major Jawbone Nkild from instituting
any meeting, demonstration or procession, and
drive out of public life all volunteer officers.
Practically, that, perhaps, would not be an un-
mixed evil, but it is probably not intended, and
clumsiness, not design, is responsible for the
wording of the regulation. Further, that well-
known military institution, “church parade,”
is clearly abolished—which is also probably by
inadvertence rather than by design. Summarised,
the new prohibitions placed upon the citizen
soldiery are :
1. Denial of the right to have any share in instituting
any meeting, demonstration or procession for any
religious or political purpose. 2. Denial of the right to
attend church or to take part in any march to church
when in uniform.
And The Bulletin doesn’t think that Major-
General Hutton really means all that. Rather it
imagines that he has been trying to amend the
King’s regulations with a reckless ignorance of
the King’s English.
“Our Misgoverned Country.”
Joseph . Carruthers, Leader of the N.S.W.
Opposition, is conducting, with all the mild
ferocity of a sheep, a series of meetings which are
supposed to represent the uprising of New South
Wales against the misdeeds of the O’See Ministry.
These misdeeds are many and glaring, but the
spectacle of “ J oey ” Carruthers rising to protest
against them is calculated to make the buried dead
rise up and laugh hilariously in their grave-clothes.
New South Wales remembers Joseph only too
well. He was the colleague of George Reid, and
part-owner of the Dry Dog; the defender of
Retd’s deficits and the man who helped to deny
that there were any ; and the apologist for that
unclean political swindle when Reid manufactured
his one big surplus by not charging to the
year ended 30th June the half-year’s interest
on the public debt which was paid over about the
16th June, but described the money as cash on
hand at the end of the month. It remembers him
as the man who strenuously supported Reid when
he abandoned Dibbs’s policy of economy, and ran
up the borrowings from £1,330,000 to over
.£2,000,000 a year; and as the voluble person who
assured the public that when George Reid
charged, in the year which ended on 30th June,
1899, £833,000 of ordinary and wholly unproduc-
tive expenditure to loans, that the proceeding was
quite honest and honorable. It distinctly recol-
lects Joe also, as a man who was glad to hold
office, with a minority behind him, as long as he
could hold it, being kept in power by what he
now denounces as the unconstitutional process of
buying the Labor vote by concessions. New
South Wales remembers its Joseph for these
matters in detail, and it also recollects him
generally as a politically unclean person who was
partly responsible for the dirtiest and most sordid
period of latter-day N.S.W. history. It might
overlook even all this if there was any sign that
Joseph Carruthers had repented, but there
isn’t. It finds in him the same old, voluble, dap-
trappy Joe, with a platitude in his right hand,
and a myth in his left, and a big, resonant poly-
syllable in his mouth. And this is the programme
he offers: —
1. Responsible Government to be re-established and
maintained in its integrity.
2. General legislation tending to the common good,
and not based on class interests.
3. Ministerial administration to be just, firm, and
impartial. . . .
4. Reduction of members to 84 in Lower and 42 in
Upper House.
5. Financial reform; living within our means; loan
expenditure to be confined to reproductive permanent
works ; a sinking fund to be established.
6. Public Service appointments to be made only on
recommendation of Public Service Board, and to be free
from political patronage. ...... ..
7. Local municipal government to be established in the
shape of a Municipal Act for towns, and local govern-
ment for country districts, with special arrangements for
unsettled districts.
8. Public Works policy to be progressive yet prudent,
water conservation being kept in the front.
9. The application of a Closer Settlement Act.
The first three items are merely the sort of thing
that anybody can find at the top of any page in
any copy-book. The reform of the Upper House is
the policy on which Joseph’s old Ministry fought
two elections, and it came back each time with
a big majority—and left the Upper House unre-
formed. As regards Local Government, Joseph’s
Cabinet advocated it for five years, and did
nothing. And Joseph helped to assure the coun-
try for five years that his old Deficit Ministry was
living within its means; and he belonged to a
Government that spent over £BOO,OOO a year of
loan money for revenue purposes, and assured the
country that the loan expenditure ivas being
“ confined to reproductive permanent works ” ; so
his assurance that he is going to say the same
things over again is only Bacon’s promissory note
endorsed by. Pork. If the shifty Joseph Car-
ruthers will come down to details, and say
exactly how much per cent, his proposed sinking-
fund will be ; exactly what works he is going to
call “ permanent and reproductive ” ; how large a
deficit he will call “living within our means”;
and what are his views on the complete cessation
of borrowing, then he might be a sufficiently
tangible Joe to be worth listening to.

The King as a Ru’er.
The Daily Mail states that since King Edward’s
accession to the throne he has commanded the attendance
of Ministers 123 times ; that he advised'them upon the
Colonial Conference, and many other subjects, evidently
intending to exercise no inconsiderable influence upon
the policy of the Empire.— Cable item,
If it is true—the Daily Mail’s statement may
not be absolutely accurate, though it is reinforced
by other evidence-that King Edward is deter-
mined to “exercise no inconsiderable influence
upon the policy of the Empire,” there ma p be a
good deal of troub'e ahead for that Empire.
The last time that a British King exercised
no inconsiderable influence, etc.”, was when
George 111. reigned, and the American colonies
were lost, and hundreds of millions of pounds were
spent in the effort to convert the French nation
from a belief in Republicanism. Since the
disasters which that reactionary monarch
inflicted upon Britain, the sovereign has been a
mere rybber-stamp registering the decrees of
Parliament. The flattery of courtiers might have
suggested otherwise, but each sovereign who
reigned in England, including Victoria, was, so
far as ruling was concerned, a nonentity. Queen
Victoria might hate Gladstone, who “spoke to
her as if she were a public meeting,” and revere
Disraeli, the vulgar Jew with the velveteen
breeches and the oiled ringlet and the genius for
flattery, but she could not hasten by one day the
dismissal of one or the summoning of the other.
The monarch, excluded from Cabinet meetings,
forbidden to influence Parliament directly, has
become under the British system the mere gilded
spout through which flows the voice of Parliament.
That is the working compromise which the British
people—a people withagenius for compromises and
no very keen sense of the ridiculous—has made
between republicanism and monarchism. The
monarch is preserved as a splendid mummy,
without power, but with all the reverence
accorded to Supreme Power, and Parliament
rules without hindrance. King Edward, if the
reports be true, wishes to break the terms of that
compromise, to arrogate to himself real power,
and to become a ruling as well as a reigning
monarch. Doubtless the idea has been put into
his head by the new school of pushful ex-radical
politicians—the men who once professed to be too
republican to shake hands with a king or a king’s
son, and who now wish to prove the sincerity
of their change of heart in the manner
natural to renegades. But if he has any lucid
moments of common sense, King Edward will
put the dangerous suggestion away irrevocably.
For even if the British people would tolerate a
King-ruler, the innovation would fail, for the
British system, whilst killing 1 the King-power, has
probably also killed the King-capacity and pro-
duced a breed of monarehs capable of laying
■foundation-stones and setting the fashion in vests
with good nature—capable of that and little more.
It would be as absurd to expect from Edward or
his son George any capacity for government as to
look for “ new-woman ” ideas in a harem. If he
is wise, Edward will “lay to bis book” —to his
foundation-stones and waistcoats—and so nob
endanger his generous salary.
When the Australian Navigation Bill is before
Parliament attention should be given to the
matter of securing an adequate number of able
seamen for all ocean-going vessels. Mr. Belcher,
Secretary of the Maoriland Seamen’s Union,
gives in a Maoriland paper figures as to the
ridiculously small number of able seaman re-
quired under the present law :
Mararoa, 1381 tons, licensed to carry 368 passengers,
compelled by law to take only eight able seamen.
Takapuna, 1036 tons, licensed to carry 163 passengers ;
compelled to take five able seamen.
Waikari, 1901 tons, licensed for 316 passengers ; must
carry nine able seamen.
If only the legal complement of able seamen were
carried, in some cases there would not be one able
seaman per boat in case of accident, and in such a
crisis it is only the able seaman who can be de-
pended upon ; the steward or “galley slush” is
mostly useless, or nearly so. The Union S.S. Co.,
as it happens, does not take advantage of the law
to so ridiculously under-man its steamers ; because
it is a wise co. with valuable vessels and a reputa-
tion that is worth preserving. But experience
shows that some shipping companies will take no
precautions which are not absolutely forced upon
them by the law; and it is for those companies
that a Navigation Act is wanted.
Victorian Treasurer Shiels, of the Rueful
Countenance, has made a second violent attack on
the Old Age Pensions Act. Shiels seems deter-
mined to wreck the Victorian Act, and, with that
object, he has enlisted the services of the polire-
sergeants throughout the State. The unthinking
police officers, like most public servants, are
opposed to the Old Age Pensions Act be-
cause they have been told by an inhuman Tory
crowd that it is responsible for the retrenchment
policy of the Government, which includes adding
five years to the term a policeman must serve
before he can get a pension. Yet Shiels has
sent round to them for reports on the habits and
circumstances of the pensioners in each district.
In doing that he is going at the back of the courts
which granted pensions, and also going outside
the law. Further, he is guilty of the meanest
trick that has ever marred the administration of
any Victorian Treasurer, because he parades these
probably prejudiced repoits before the public as
impartial, conclusions drawn from facts. All
this trickery and inhumanity on the part of Shiels
and others, who want to down the old-age pen-
sioners, spring from fear of a land-tax. Shiels,
by the way, is largely interested, in one way and
another, in Western District estates, so his
prejudices are most likely to go with the anti-
land-tax crowd.
Readers of recent Melb. Argus “ The Land Our
Mainstay” articles would fancy that the Western
district of Victoria was blessed with a race of
land-holding philanthropists of the first order.
The special reporter has drawn a really beautiful
picture of the generous landlord and the happy,
contented tenant. But during the c’oser settle-
ment discussion in Vie. Parliament, t’other night,
one of the members of that region, R. F. Toutcher,
showed a vastly different (and much truer) aspect
of the case. He told of toiling tenants, crushed
under extortionate rents, galled by restric-
tions that must be loathsome to any in whom the
spirit of free men still exists, and with no hope of
ever becoming independent citizens. And he. is
undoubtedly right. It is easy to see the motive
of the Argus’ articles—to delay as much as pos-
sible, by booming the squire and tenant system,
what must eventually come in Victoria —compul-
sory resumption and a State tenant system.
Amongst other senseless slush ladled out by the
special reporter is the assertion that the land-
letting squatters are “pioneers” and “experi-
mentalists ” in the use of manures and scientific
methods of farming, whereas in reality they are
simply hard, unfeeling business men driving a
cruelly hard bargain for their own benefit. It
will take more than the fulsome flattery of the
Argus groveller to make the rack-renting land-
lord a public benefactor

Launceston (Tas.) Examiner, which always lies
heroically on behalf. of the Retrograde Party,
strongly urges Victoria to adopt that part of the
Factories Commission’s report Which advocates
the abolition of the old Minimum Wage Law, and
not to adopt the other part, which recommends the
establishment, as a substitute, of Arbitration
Courts with power to fix wages. As regards the
Minimum Wage this fine large hunky lie is really
thrilling :
After the experience of the disastrous results of the
determinations of the wages boards—the utter stagnation
of trade, closing of factories, dearth of employme t,
strangling of enterprise and emigration by thousanus oi
artisans—with scarcely one compensating advantage, ine
abolition of the wages boards will come as a reprieve trom
industrial annihilation.
Under the Minimum Wage law the number of
employees in Victorian manufactures increased
from 50,397 in 1896 to 66,529 in 1901; and the
number of factories increased in the same period
from 2809 to 3249. This is the Examiner’s
“closing of factories, dearth of employment,
strangling of enterprise ” and all the other lies !
At the time of writing, there is no definite know*
ledge as to what steps Victorian Premier Irvine
proposes to take in regard to his Reform Bill.
He must do something definite now—either back
•down definitely or deal with the obstructive
Council definitely—for the House of Landlords
has a second time knocked all the reform pro-
posals out of his Reform Bill and thrown it back
at him with emphatic contempt. Obviously the
House of Landlords does not think that Irvine is
really in earnest; if it did, it would not run the
risk of destruction which any “ Upper House ”
incurs when it opposes a resolute Government,
backed up by a majority of the popularly-elected
branch of the Legislature. The point in doubt is
whether Irvine’s intentions can be best judged
by the public from his public speeches or by the
Landlords from their private acquaintance with
the gentleman. The Bulletin, with all its pro-
found distrust of the average politician, feels
•compelled to think that Irvine must stand by his
public utterances. They have been so clear and
emphatic as to defy the most ingenious mind to
explain them away; and the only method of
going back on them is by a direct admission of
falsehood and cowardice. Of course that is
possible—anything is possible to the average poli-
tician. Still the position seems to indicate that
something is going to happen to the Victorian
Upper House.
Agar Wynne, M.L.C. (Vic.), achieved notoriety
last week. His horse, Great Scot, won the Aus-
tralian Cup; his house at St. Kilda was entered
by a burglar; his life was threatened by an alleged
madman ; and he made a Tory speech in the
House of Rich Landlords on the Reform question
which completely right-about-faced on his own
utterances of a year before in favor of the Peacock
Reform Bill, when he was a salaried Minister.
When a Democratic Ministry is in power there is
always one good Democrat in the Vic. Council —
the salaried Minister. He was generally a bitter
Tory before he got the salary, and he is a violent
Tory after the salary expires, but while the salary
lasts he is a good enough Democrat. If 25 salaried
Ministers could be appointed in the Vic. Council
almost anything might happen. It is that body’s
amenableness to a salary which makes The
Bulletin suggest that the Council would vote its
own abolition if the Bill provided that the whole
48 would go out with £IOOO a year life pension—
and their abolition would be cheap at the price.

“F”: Yesterday I heard a South Sea Island
missionary, an old, solid, square-headed High-
lander, who has spent over 30 years with Tommy
Tanna, say to a big audience what, as a White
Australian, sent a thrill of pride and satisfaction
through me. Said the big, serious, old man, with
a huge right hand raised to Heaven :
I want you to mark carefully what lam saying. There
is nothing that this new Commonwealth Parliament can
do that will redound more to its honor and glory than the
passing of the Act which is terminating the South Sea
labor traffic. If the name of this first Federal Parliament
lives by anything it has done, it will be by this noble and
humane Act. I speak of what I know, and I say that the
abolition of this traffic is a thing to thank God for.
The speaker pointed out that the natives of the
New Hebrides were a decaying race, and that the
labor traffic was simply a pouring out of the life-
blood of the islands. And so unhealthy to white
life are these islands that, unlike the case of the
Australian aborigines, the black man’s loss will
not be the white man’s gain. (I put half-a-crown
in the collection on the strength of a White Aus-
tralia.) And here is another informative item to
the same point. Dr. Annand, of Santo Island,
New Hebrides, writing as late as Dec. last, says :
We learned that, at North Malekula, an English
recruiter had been shot dead by the bush people. . . .
His death was not unexpected by some, as he was
“ wanted ” by the natives in several places in the group.
No one need be surprised to hear of one or two more of
his class meeting with the same retribution. In fact it is
the only way the natives have of protecting themselves.
They are kidnapped by white men, and their evidence is
not accepted in the investigations. We have been dis-
tinctly told by our Commissioner that no charge against
a white men can be sustained unless under oath of a
white man or a Christian teacher, neither of whom is
likely to be present when an outrage is committed.
Once again—Three cheers for a White Australia !

The Age is trying to awaken V ictoria to the fact
that the over-crowding and antiquated treatment
of lunatics in the State represents ultimately a
very poor economy. Victoria has, seemingly,
about the worst lunatic system in Australia, with
10,924 under treatment, and a percentage of
cures of only 28T6 (the N.S.W. percentage of
cures is 34 23, the Westralian 40'92). The patients
are over-crowded, persons only slightly deranged
being herded with hopeless and violent maniacs,
and the medical treatment is not the best pro-
curable. The consequence is a serious waste of
public money. Sound economy would suggest
treating the lunatic in the manner most likely
to ensure cure, scr £hat he may remain
an unproductive and costly maniac for the
shortest possible space of time. The foolish
parsimony of the Victorian Government saves a
few pounds on the first year’s treatment at the
price of having to keep a confirmed oase for years
afterwards. Practically every Australian Govern-
ment errs in the same way in its treatment of
lunatics, consumptives, etc. The sensible and
truly economical system is to spare no money or
pains to cure such folk ; the general system is to
box them up in an asylum, restrict curative effort
from motives of parsimony, and thus establish
them as permanent burdens on th® community.
This statement, supplied by a Mackay (North
Queensland) sugar-grower, is actually quoted in
the leader columns of Brisbane Courier —since it
fell out with Philp and his nigger-labor Ministry :
We are much better off than with the colored labor.
In the Mackay district the farmers who employed only
white labor have drawn about £15,000 in rebate, and it
has entirely changed the aspect «f affairs for nany of
them. The storekeepers can tell you that the result of
the season’s work has changed many bad accounts into
good ones. But there are other points which should be
mentioned. With the higher-priced labor we have to go
in for better cultivation ; we could not go on in the old
happy-go-lucky style. Then there is the necessity for
economising which farmers did not observe before. With
colored labor the hands were kept on practically all the
year round, and were paid twice a year, the mills guaran-
teeing the wages. That was not economical. Now the
work is chiefly contract, and the men have to be paid
when it is done. When the wages were guaranteed the
sum was deducted from the price of the cane purchased,
and the farmers often did not handle the money. Now
differently situated, and the whole tends to
economy.
