The voice of Dolores breathed forth a
farewell season in Sydney Town Hall from Satur-
day till Tuesday last, and, as usual, the heights
and depths of vocal lore were ransacked for gems
to make that last appearance especially brilliant,
so that it may glitter in the memory till the
wonderful little singer shall turn Australian wards
again. Mdlle. is now (unless the advertisement
prevaricated) on her way to Europe via America,
and it is whispered that she may not return to
Australia any more. If so, Australia is indeed
unfortunate, for Dolores is a national insti-
tution. Since Melba burst upon us with public
receptions, midnight bookings, gruesome shark
stories, thousand-guinea Thompson guarantees,
and all the rest of the pomp and circumstance
of gaudy, shrieking advertisement (and gave
four songs for a guinea) we have learned things.
We have learned somewhat of the true value of
the dainty little French lady who gave us so much
for our money, who sang with such human feeling,
and who ranged through all the compositions of
all the composers, living and dead —from Mas-
cagni back tnrough the ages to that nameless,
grim old Abbe who composed the wild dour Chant
of the Cross, which Geoffrey de Bouillon’s fanatios
howled as they tramped up the Mount of Olives
over heaps of dead. But The Bulletin hopes
that Dolores will come back again.
** » »
“ When the Lamps are Lighted ’ was re-
vived at Sydney Cri. last Saturday. It is a
curious farrago, part melodrama, part operatic
comedy, part everything or anything. Most
of the characters change their names oc-
casionally, while Johnny Sheridan and Miss
Celia Mavis further complicate matters by
disguising themselves as five or six other
persons respectively. This they do on the
slightest provocation, mostly with the intention
of helping Paul Wayland out of his difficulties.
Paul, who is well acted by Thomas Curran, has been
wrongfully accused of one crime, and in trying
to make things even with the villain is accused
of another. The characters as a rule act more
naturally in the operatic part than in the dramatic,
Miss Celia Mavis being rather an exception. Miss
Heba Barlow is not cut out for the gentle heroine
type of character; she nods her head in a manner
characteristic of the pert housemaid rather
than the cultivated daughter of society. Also
her walk 'would better suit slavey Semolina,
who, by the way, is well acted by Miss
Tilly Dunbar. Miss Mabel Hardinge-Maltbv, as
a lady whose sin has found her out, is a moderate
success, and little Frankie Rafferty, who
used to pervade the stage in a most natural
manner when enacted by Sheridan Junior, on this
occasion seems to remain mostly at the wings,
chiefly, it is presumed, because he is enacted by
someone else. A good deal of fair scenery is
shown, including a few slums, a “den” or two,
and the Thames Embankment, whereon the
costers frolic in a manner that is calculated to
interfere with traffic. These remarks don’t tend
in any large way to explain what the drama is
about. The writer, as a matter of fact, has given
up trying to decide what it is about.
W* * *
“Sweet Nell of Old Drury” will beguile
the bad King and the bkd King’s dog to-morrow
(Friday) for the last time. “Zaza,” in fiveactsand
two “z’s,” will follow at Sydney Royal on Saturday.
“Zaza” is French, and there are hopes that it is
wicked. The reports of its appearance in Mel-
bourne set forth that it was wicked in a certain
picturesque sense when it appeared there, but
didn’t set forth so clearly as they might have done
how wicked it was. Sydney has hopes that it is
wickeder —in a certain picturesque sense—than
these Melbourne notices seemed to imply. There-
fore, Sydney is making remarkably good time
down the street to buy its ticket. And while
doing so, Sydney desires to express a hope that
the Melbourne press will be more explicit next
time.
»* » *
“The Wrong Mr. Wright” continues to
pursue a gay but not really sinful career at Sydney
Palace, and with the cooler weather the play is
acted with more vim and the audience is able to
enjoy it with less restraint. It was not unalloyed
pleasure to go to a farce when, after a hearty
laugh, you felt as if you had fallen into a pool of
warm water. But the dog days seem to have
mercifully passed away from Sydney for a while.
** * *
Sutfeon Vane’s drama, the “ Cotton King,”
after a short stay at Sydney Lyceum, gives place
this Saturday to “The World,” which will be a
change for the better. The “Cotton King”
doesn’t rise anywhere above commonplace melo-
drama, and the various “sensations” don’t excite
enthusiasm somehow or other. The Mill Hands’
Picnic is one bright spot, but it doesn’t spread
into Acts 1., 111., and IV. “ The World,” so far
as writer remembers, boasts of a raft, an infernal
machine, and several interesting murders. A
really interesting murder has been lacking at the
Lyceum for some little time now.
*# m m
Programme at Sydney Tivoli brightened
up considerably this week. The new game of
“Push Ball” is a variant of “net-ball”—the idea
of which came from America—and provides some
interest, partly due to the game itself, largely to
the incidental display of female figures. Push-
ball played by men wouldn’t draw worth a cent.
John Coleman’s popularity continues, and the
Concertina King proves that he is a musician of
resource, with frequent changes of programme.
An innovation at the Tivoli, which seems
likely to attract some support, is “an illustrated
dramatic recital ”by John Cosgrove. The recital
is the sort of tripe which G. R. Sims wrote in his
worst moments, before he took to comparative
canity and an interest in hair restorers, and the
illustrations are almost bad enough for the yarn
But Mr. Cosgrove recites well, and with les*
atrocious material would do better —and probably
please his audienoe less. For the recital “ goes ”
very well as it is. Those remarkable gymnasts,
the Schenks, were announced to reappear on
Wednesday, the lltb.
** m m
“ Man’s Enemy,” new to Melbourne
Royal last Saturday, seems much the same
Andersonian melodrama as usual in the first two
acts, after which it takes a drunk and disorderly
turn for the better. The third act has a rather
convincing smell of beer and spirits, and the
people who don’t get injured or arrested in the
vicinity of a small pub are subsequently let loose
in a doss-house where some of them sleep the sleep
of the unrighteous for fourpence, and the others
throw one another out of the window. A fourth
act is likewise human and murderous, but un-
fortunately the principal villain has a vision
after killing Miss Duggan with a poker, and
this vision, which serves as an illustrated post-
script to the beer-stained narrative, shows Robt.
Inman in the act of getting married to a respect-
able young woman who is supported by 16 brides-
maids of various sizes. The wedding tableau
would have been an error in dramatic tactics
under any conditions. The 16 assorted brides-
maids amount to a lamentable joke. Considering
that it was Inman’s first wife whom the villain
had slain with the fire-iron he might have shown
better taste in visioning the widower’s second
nuptials.
• * * •
Miss Duggan was a bad woman when she
married Inman—at the outset of the drama. She
had a bogus brother Tom, who lured the bride-
groom on to brandy whilst she returned to her
native gambling hell to smoke a cigarette. She
was even wicked enough to enter the holy bonds
in a low-necked dress. Probably she smoked a
cigarette in the vestry. Anyway, she showed no
natural love and affection for Inman when he was
getting ruined by Thomas the Tempter. Further-
more, she helped to put the drink-fiend
on his track again after he had become
sober through extreme poverty. Miss Duggan and
Thomas hired a man named Bill to intoxicate the
stone-broke penitent and throw sulphuric acid
over the girl who loved him, but Bill cast the
vitriol on the wrong female and came to a bad end.
Also Inman got saved from the Downward Path
by the effects of his delirium tremens, and the
bogus brother slew Miss Duggan as aforesaid.
He was quite sober when he did the deed. With
the awful exception of Bill, none of the lurid
sinners in “Man’s Enemy” were addicted to
liquor. They simply went wrong on temperance
principles, and left their punishment to Provi-
dence. One young man who embezzled £lOO
in the first act got no punishment whatever. He
afterwards became a clergyman, that he might
avoid temptation. It is practically impossible for
a clerk in holy orders to rob his employer.
• • • •
Miss Duggan, as the bare-necked bride
from the gambling hell, shows a tendency to im-
prove her elocution. Inman is a satisfactory
feature of the performance, likewise Carey, and
sundry casual inebriates; and Willard, as a
foreign gentleman of dishonorable character, who
lives and dies in one scene only. The orthodox
comic relief, which falls to Bert Bailey’s lot, is a
painful success.
*• * # *■
The imposing musical tumultuousness of
“The Fortune Teller,” likewise its scenery, girls,
uniforms and simple humors, are carrying that
alleged Hungarian comic opera to a successful
issue at Melbourne Princess’s. A Hungarian
patriot complains in the Argus that “ The For-
tune Teller’’ is not a truthful representation of
places, flags, people and things in his beloved
country, but Melbourne seems disposed to put up
with such errors as are hurting his Hungarian
feelings. Granting that Madame Slapoffski, as
Musette the gipsy, is identical with another girl
who couldn’t possibly wear the same com-
plexion, the fact remains that Musette breaks
a plate on the skull of an aged dancing
master. And when an actress smashes a plate
on a fellow-creature’s head she strikes public
criticism dumb. She is entitled to be a white
gipsy if she chooses. She seizes approbation by
the forelock, like a low comedian who rolls down
a flight of stairs. Next to the Musette breakage
of the crockery the most brilliant comedy incident
in the Hungarian opera is perpetrated by a fleet-
ing Hebrew who jumps into a fish-pond to save a
diamond necklace from drowning. Apparently
he sticks in the mud head downwards, and dies a
hero’s death with his legs pointing to the blue
firmament; so the surviving humorists shake a
quantity of hidden treasure from the boots of the
departed.
** * *
“ The Geisha ” revival, at Her Majesty’s,
holds the floor until Monday, the 16th, and mean-
while the tuneful fable of old Japan passes with-
out protest from any local subject of Britain’s
yellow ally, although it looks nearly as libellous
as “The Mikado.” On Tuesday, which is the
Feast of St. Patrick, manager Williamson will
introduce to Australian notice a more or less Irish
comic opera entitled “The Emerald Isle.” The
late Arthur Sullivan signed his honorable name
to the music of “ The Emerald Isle,” and Captain
Basil Hood wrote the book, and J.C.W., who has
never known Sullivan to misfire on this side of
the equator, reckons that the new opera will
probably go off with a bang.
** * »
At the Bijou, the Woods-Williamson Co.
is reviving “ The Gates of Bondage ” for a second
time in Melbourne. This co.’s first production of
“ The Gates ” at the old Alexandra reminded the
present scribe that it was very little different from
“ Ben-My-Chree,” a previous adaptation of “ The
Deemster,” by Wilson Barrett and Hall Caine
And because “Ben-My-Chree” had unfolded its
heavy theatrical tale before “The Gates” came
to hand, nobody wanted the reminder in question.
An excellent stabbing match between two young
men lightens the gloom of this intensely provincial
drama, but for the rest it has a tiresome, tearful
plot of the kind that interests actors only. The
“ powerful” situations have no air of reality about
them, and even if the mutual distresses of Alf.
Boothman and his son, Alf. Woods, were at ali
convincing, it would grow horribly monotonous all
the same. “-Boy Jim,” adapted from Conan
Doyles pugilistic novel, “Rodney Stone,” will
come like balm in Gilead when “ The Gates ” are
shut.
The programme at Melb. Opera House
includes Gracie Emmett and 00. in a new faroe
(not so amusing as the farce that went before),
Seely and West (who play various musical instru-
ments, especially the cornet), Ernest F itts, a
couple of aotive and intelligent female infants
(both named Eileen), and Jacques Inaudi, the
calculator Small Jacques, with the gigantic
head for figures, has outlived all suspicion of
fraud, and is now seriously accepted for just what
he is worth as an entertainer. Some nights
he falls flat for want of questioners, but
as a rule he is called upon to supply cube roots and
fifth quantities and things to solemn-looking
citizens, who have come prepared to gloat
over his discomfiture. Up to now Inaudi has
always vanquished the enemy, but there are times
when the little man’s genius for arithmetic seems
very tired. When they are bowing to the audience
he looks at his comparatively giant colleague as
though he wished they could change places for a
week. It is so hard to be Inaudi and so easy to
be the stout beeswaxed person who chalks the
figures on the board.
** * *
At the Adelaide Tivoli, Fred. Poplar is
appearing as the intoxicated gentleman whose
turn with a dummy friend is already well-known
at the same house as a cinematograph show.
Kitty Wager is also doing well. The Schenk
Brothers have moved on with their balancing
feats, and in their place come Pope and Sayles,
the genuinely black comedians who, in the Tivoli’s
early days, occupied the corner-cliairs until Ade-
laide came to regard them as permanent citizens.
From the Royal the pantomime has passed away,
after two crowded weeks of the Fan Ballet, the
Phantom Guards, and various quaint electric
effects. The great Hayseed family, in town for
the Show, has flocked to Fitzgeralds’ Circus, with
Parrel and the tea-cup cycle whizz.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
“Warn everyone not to touch Arltunga ; bom-
bastic booming; grossly exaggerated.” This message
has been sent to The Bulletin direct from the field, and
is from one of the most experienced explorers and
successful prospectors in Australia. The Bulletin in-
tended to have something to say about the insanity of
the boom which is prevailing in Adelaide and other
centres, but sufficient for the day is the explicit condem-
natory telegram thereof. It must be pointed out, how.
ever, that all the excitement is founded on the highly,
colored report of one Dr. Chewings. It may be pertinent
to ask is this the Dr. Chewings who figured so conspicu-
ously in the early days of Coolgardie, and wrote glowing
reports on all sorts and conditions of alleged mines,
notably the General Gordon ?

The figures for Westralia are always more or less doubt-
ful until the Government Statistician’s monthly report
comes along, there being a perpetual uncertainty as to
whether State revenue only, or State and Federal re-
venue conjointly, is mentioned in the telegraphic
intelligence. There is reason to believe, however,
that the above statement of the case is correct.
The aggregate result for the five States is, therefore, a
doleful decrease of £120,090. And the situation is not
improved by the fact that the only two States which
show a material increase are also the two which are keep-
ing up a bogus prosperity by lavish and insensate bor-
rowing. New South Wales, but for the increased revenue
arising from the Federal tariff, would show an awful dron
of £212,000. y
So far as the financial year has gone, the posi-
tion is shown hereunder. The Westralian figures are
given only to the end of January, that being the date up
to which the Statistician’s monthly returns extend They
might be brought up to date by adding the telegraphed
figures for February to the Statistician’s returns, but
experience has shown that these and the official figures
somehow seldom quite agree :

The City Mutual Ltfe Assurance Society
(Sydney) has this year done a most excellent and com-
mendable thing—it has wholly written off that always
dubious asset, “ Deferred Instalments of Annual Pre-
miums.” In the following st: tement, in order to put the
year 1902 on the same footing a- its predecessors, this
asset has been disregarded throughout:

The year Just concluded shows the largest revenue in the
society’s record, though the increase on 1901 is very small
by comparison with some previous increases. The accu-
mulated funds are now £238,396, all represented, so far as
can be judged, by genuine assets, the “ Deferred Instal
ments of Annual Premiums ” having, as already men-
tioned, been completely written off.
• • • *
Another good point in the case is that there is
a considerable decrease in expenses, though the rate is
still absurdly heavy. The record is as follows :
Expenses compared with Total Income.
1893 £27 15 11 per cent. 1898 £22 10 11 per cent.
1894 35 9 6 „ 1899 21 18 4 „
1895 29 8 3 „ 1900 29 311 „
1896 25 10 0 „ 1901 25 16 2 „
1897 22 18 9 „ 1902 23 3 2 „
The improvement ought to be larger than it is ; still it if
a very material improvement, and, the prodigal having
returned to some extent, the case calls for a partial slaying
of the fatted calf in strict moderation. The revenue on
which these calculations are based consists of interest,
consideration for annuities, and premiums, less return
premiums and any sums added to the “ Deferred
Premium ” item, or plus any diminution of the “ Deferred
Premium " item.
The interest earnings are above the average,
representing £5 9s. 4d. per cent, per annum on the
average funds for the year. The amounts written off
assets are considerable, though it is well, in such a
matter, to err on the safe side. Allowing for the con-
siderable reduction in expenses, the high interest-rate
earned, the fact that reasonable progress is being made
at building up the funds, and, above all, for the fact that
“Deferred Instalments of Annual Premiums" have
wholly disappeared from among the assets, 1902 must be
regarded as rather a favorable year for the City Mutual
Life Assurance Society.

“F.C.”: Anent the coming land values tax.
Ran across a block of 500 acres scrub land within 14 miles
of the G.P.0., Melb., just rented, with the 6-roomed
house and stable thereon, by a friend of mine, at 15s. a
week from the Bank. Seems cheap, but the
hank was glad to let it. Friend makes a living out of
cows, poultry, a pig or two, and taking cattle and horses
on agristment. It is very poor country, but none the less
good fruit-growing land, and dozens of orchardists
would be only too glad to buy 20 and 30-acre blocks, but
—the bank wants £lO an acre, no less.

* * * *
Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of 10jin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*
• « * *
It is not generally known that in the treatment
of ore as much ol the valuable minerals is often lost in
the tailings as is saved by the appliances. This is due
in most instances to the inefficient machines used and
the difficulty in saving the fines and slimes. The best
machine to treat the latter classes are Luhrig Vanners.
These machines are in successful daily use in the principal
gold, galena, copper and tin mines in Australia and
Tasmania, proving their superiority over other so-called
slime machines. Messrs W. & J. Lempriere, 506 Little
Collins-street, Melbourne, are the general agents, and full
particulars can be obtained from them.*
• * • *
A. Kozmmsky, 106 Queen-st., Melbourne,
having returned from England, is now prepared to
purchase paying mines, and mining properties (not
merely surface shows), situated in any part of
Australasia requiring further capital for development and
machinery. Only genuine and legitimate ventures,
capable of bearing the strictest examinations will be
entertained.*
• • * •
The Wilfley Concentrator is probably the most
successful piece of mining machinery ever introduced.
In 1897 it was first brought out as a novelty, and in 1902
is regarded as an absolute necessity in the plant of every
mine requiring to concentrate its ores. Over 5000 of the
machines have been sold and upwards of 600 mines have
it in use all over the world, while in the Commonwealth
of Australia above 500 machines are now working on 120
mines. Thirty million tons of pre are being treated
annually by the above tables ; probably more than equal
to the combined ore tonnage of all other makes of con-
centrators. The Wilfley is the only concentrating table
in use at the Great Anaconda Copper Mines, where 431
tables are now at work. Particulars and catalogues can
be obtained on application to the sole Australian
Manufacturers, The Austral Otis Engineering Coy.,
South Melbourne.*

City Leader, July, 1902, writes re the Colonial
Mutual Life Assurance Society-Limited “ The Directors
with their characteristic foresight always place security
before interest. Notwithstanding "the difficulty in
obtaining remunerative rates the interest rate has slightly
increased as compared with the previous year.”

There is apparently more than nuts in Brazil.
The latest cable received on the subject states that the
Trans-Pacific dredge, in a run of seven days, obtained
60oz. gold. Work is now being delayed by floods. As
the Trans-Pacific Co. hold over 100 miles of river beds, if
much more of it pans out at the same rate, there should
be great joy and some dividends in North Bananaland,
where most of the Trans-Pacific shares are held.

The Mort’s Dock and Engineering Co. has
£50,000 of preference capital which carries a cumulative
6 per cent, dividend. This dividend was not paid at
June, 1902, though what possible end the management
expected to gain by postponing it, seeing that it must be
paid some day, it is hard to tell. Then at December,
1902, the management decided to pay up the arrears of
preferential share dividend, which was inevitable in any
ease ; to pay the equally inevitable 6 per cent, preference
dividend for the current half-year ; and to give the ordi-
nary shareholders 6 per cent. As the earnings didn’t
nearly cover the £4500 required for the ordinary dividend
the wisdom of this proceeding seems very doubtful. It
has seriously reduced the reserves, and as times are had
and the big lawsuit against the co. isn’t quite settled
even yet, the other party having appealed to the Privy
Council, this looks a very ill-chosen moment to encroach
on the reserves.

• • • •
Q. Government, which has just shut down the
Bowen line, has now the satisfaction of learning that the
Townsville Harbour Board is practically insolvent. The
statement re insolvency was publicly made by W. J.
Castling, a leading member of the Board and a shrewd
business man. For some time the Board, in defiance of
the opinion of so eminent an engineer as Sir John
Coode, has been endeavoring to excavate a harbour
in Ross Creek, and the result has been a disas-
trous failure. Some few years ago the famous
Sigma tornado played havoc with the Townsville
brsakwater, and it is highly probable that a similar
gale will some day wipe out the lovely artificial
harbor altogether. It would probably be a public
blessing if such a thing did occur, for then the North
would have to rely on Bowen as its port, and a huge
waste of public money would be prevented. Mean-
time, any extension of the Bowen line which
might enable that port to tap its appropriate
back-country is strictly tabooed , the existing
line is closed down indefinitely, and a local station-
master, with the aid of sundry dismissed officials, is
offering to lease the line from Government at a pound a
week rental. A short time ago Treasurer Cribb suggested
the selling of Government railways to syndicates. Per-
haps the Philp Cabinet is preparing Queensland to take
leasing or selling with a good grace by giving a prepara-
tory dose of railways shut down entirely.

The drought and the consequent depression is telling on
many institutions ; still the Victoria Coffee Palace shows
gross earnings of rather over 9 per cent. Another £950
has been paid during 1902 off the depressing mortgage
burden, reducing it to £22,850. At the close of 1899 it
stood at £25,750, so respectable progress is being made.

™ gallons in the import of Australian wine for

* * * .
Lead having shown signs of keeping on the
healthy Blde £l2, the B.H. British has brushed away
the dust and cobwebs of 21 months and started producing
again. The other mines which are still idle are the. Junc-
tion, the North, and the Junction North ; and their
chances of an early start are pretty well indicated hy the
following comparison of the prices at which they were
producing lead (silver being then about 2s. 4d.) in the
latter half of 1900 : British, £lO Us. 6d.; North, £l3 Us.;
Block 14, £l3 15s. 9d. ; Junction and Junction North,
over £l5. Of these, the Junction can now work a
good deal cheaper, and the North a little cheaper, than
at the date of closure.

A.J.H.C. (Euriowie) asks: Tin is worth £133
ss. per ton—what is the value per cwt. of ore assaying 80
per cent, metallic tin ? This question is being reserved
for Inaudi on his reappearance in Sydney.
• • m *
Mount Boppy, near Cobar (N.S.W.), is assert-
ing itself as the boss gold mine of the State. Jan. return,
22000 z. from 2200 tons. Recent returns would seem to
indicate that a policy of averaging at an ounce to the ton
is being pursued. Shares quoted in London—buyers,
355. ; no declared sellers. The deep workings are reveal-
ing some high-grade ore. Sydney speculators who are
freely subscribing from £lO to £5O apiece to send pro-
spectors to Oentralia—some of the alleged prospectors
were never on a mine in their lives, and others are noted
for their failure to distinguish between mine and thine—
might take note of the fact that in the Boppy district
there is ample scope for legitimate prospecting work,
with the almost “ certain hope ” of some big things being
found. But, of course, these hills are not far enough
to look green.
• * • .
. On position there is no better area of mining
ground in N.S.W. than the freehold property of the
North Cobar company. It adjoins the Great Cobar, and
the old workings of the latter are within 400 or 500 feet of
the boundary. According to ancient reports there was a
strong lode showing in the end. Some feeble attempts at
prospecting were made in the early days, but for fully 15
years not a pick was put into the ground. The demise of
a large holder, who would not pay calls and who could
not.be compelled to do so, gave other holders an oppor-
tunity to proceed with prospecting work. A shaft was
sunk 500 ft., and a crosscut is now being driven to the
east. This drive is in about 130 ft., and some of the
holders are sanguine enough to expect that the lode will
be cut in less than 50ft. more. Writer’s opinion is that
shaft is much too far to the west, and that 100 ft. at least
will have to be added to the estimated distance to be
driven. The so-called “ buck ” reef, which lies to the east
of the Great Cobar lode—at depth it is reported to be a
splendid copper formation—is plainly discernible on the
surface of North Cobar. It ought to be an infallible indi-
cator of the position of the other lode. Present owners
of North Cobar deserve to strike the lode where it is rich
and wide.
* * • •
Great Cobar Western is a mine—save the mark
—with many interesting records. It is entitled to a book
all to itself, and instructive indeed it would be. It would
be a statement of mining methods beyond comparison, of
shepherding reduced to a fine art, of financial manipu-
lation which would make the most skilful of State
Treasurers green with envy. The cute crowd that con-
trols the show has hitherto had marvellously good luck—
when there has been a lack of available pay ore there
has also been a lack of water, and thus there have been
regular periods of longduration when solitude has reigned
supreme on one of the most valuable properties in the
State. But at last the crowd finds itself in a tight corner.
Tributors recently took the mine in hand, but exhausted
their little capital in patching the boiler and getting the
rattle-trap toy battery in order, and then found they
could not get at payable ore without doing a lot of dead
work. Mine is again idle, and mines’ officials are begin-
ning to talk “nasty.” They are on the right track.
Owners ought to be compelled to work the mine or make
way for others who would willingly do so. Some day
some men of enterprise will get hold of it, and then it
will become something other than the laughing stock of
Cobar. Work in the past has been equivalent to a small
mouse gnawing at a mighty mountain.
• • • *
Barrier mine, Wyalong (N.S.W.): Since
tributors bought this property, about four months
ago, for £2OO, they have won from development work
alone on the new reef which they discovered over £2OOO
worth of gold, and have now started stoping ore which is
described as “ literally bristling with gold.” The Lady
Grace mine, on the same field, is winning wonderfully
rich sulphide ore from a depth of 700 ft. This mine,
which was originally known as the Bantam, adjoins
Neeld’s on the north, and the recent rich discovery
should considerably enhance the value of the pioneers
property.
* • • . •
Brown’s Creek gold mine, near Blayney
(N.S.W.),’is one of the geological curiosities of Australia.
It was purchased some years ago by a French co., which
erected a 60-head heavy stamp mill—the finest battery in
N.S.W.— and sent out a pair of the largest rotary rock
breakers ever made—to crush mud. The plant includes
a compound condensing engine that would almost drive
all the stampers in the State-ran engine which 90 out of
100 of the certificated so-called mining engine-drivers
would not know how to start. As an engineering blunder
this plant is without equal in Australia, and who is
responsible for its erection no one seems to know. Cer-
tain it is that Irwin and Callahan, who were the managers,
were not, as there is correspondence extant to show
that they vigorously protested against such absolutely
unsuitable machinery and appurtenances being erected.
The mill ran for a few weeks only, but long enough to
knock the bottoms out of some of the boxes and to smash
up the co. About 10,000 tons of mud was crushed. The
average of daily assays was about 3dwt. per ton, but the
actual recovery was only about 6s. per ton. Mine has
been idle ever since, but is to be re-worked immediately
under the personal direction of “ Jack ” Roberts, who has
become associated with Frank Gardiner in a partnership
in it. It is expected that it will take some months to un-
water the mine, but meanwhile extensive tests are to be
made upon material from the open cut, to determine
whether some more expeditious and economical method
of treatment than direct battery process can be found.
• • • •
Murchison Associated, Day Dawn (W.A.),
which has been the subject of some wild gambling lately,
is a fifth construction of the old Murchison Gift. Co. has
a nominal capital of £75,000 in 300,000 shares of ss. each.
For the past 18 months work has been proceeding very
slowly on a 24-acre lease known as the Rubicon, situated
about a mile north of the Great Fingall. The reef being
worked is supposed to be the N.W. branch, or leg of the
Fingall reef, the character of the quartz and country
being exactly similar to that of the big mine. The main
shaft is 375 ft. deep, and levels are being driven at the
150 ft. and 350 ft. Till recently the ore has been low
grade, but, at the 150 ft. level, a chute of stone averaging
2oz. per ton has been met with, hence the sudden jump
in scrip, and the subsequent wild fluctuations. It is still
too ear! vto predict the future of the co. The Great Fin-
gall was comparatively low grade in the upper levels,
and, as it is generally believed that this co. is working
the same reef, it is conjectured that there are great
possibilities before it.
• * • •
Great Fingall Consols (W.A.) is increasing its
capital by 25,000 shares, or 10 per cent., for the alleged
purpose of adding 40 head of stamps, which seems a very
peculiar deal in face of recent disclosures. For four
years this co. has held on to 24,000 Sons of Gwalia shares,
which, during that time, have touched £7 apiece, and
for two years it has held 100,000 West Fingalls, which
have been quoted as high as £2 ss. Why did directors
not dispose of part at least of these holdings, and place
co. in a strong position ? The cost of a new 40-head mill,
including engine-power, erection, and all accessories, is
£30,800 according to the Ivanhoe’s experience. Then
why issue 25,000 shares worth £150,000? It is certain
that scrip will have an almost immediate substantial rise
on the strength of the very large increase in output and
divs., which an extra 40 head of stamps will ensure.
• * • m
Croesus South, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), after a very
chequered career of eight years, and after two recon-
structions, has again closed down. The last attempt to
raise the wind was in December, 1901, when 19,983 pref.
shares, bearing 10 per cent, interest, were offered, but
only 9285 placed. Mine appears to be exceptionally well-
situated, as it adjoins the Brown Hill on the north, but,
unfortunately, the rich chute, which outcropped on the
latter mine close to the Croesus boundary, dipped south
and nothing payable has been struck on this property.
Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), has bought the
full equipment of the defunct Freemantle Co., Kunanal-
ling, including a ,10-head battery. The plant is being
dismantled, and will be re-erected in its new situation
with all speed, so that another mine will shortly be added
to the regular producers. As here stated, mine is one of
the richest outside Kalgoorlie, and should give a very
good account of itself. A large quantity of stone is al-
ready developed, and, as it is estimated to yield 3oz. per
ton, early and substantial divs. may be looked for.
* * • *
Ida H., Laverton (W.A.), has been closed
down for a month, as the crown of the boiler came down
owing to salt water being used. The boiler is condemned
and a new one has had to be bought. Co. has done
fairly well so far, hut is not looking so well now. The
vendors are still unable to sell their scrip as the co. has
not yet applied to the London Stock Exchanges for a
special settlement for vendors’ shares.
* • • •
Merton’s Reward (W.A.) has been equipped
v/ith a rock-drilling plant, and is to be systematically
opened up at 100, 200 and 300 ft. Hitherto a first-class
system of pig-rooting has been indulged in, and all avail-
able pay-ore has been taken out, the bulk of it by Fred
Merton, and what was left, by his successor, Fred Slorgan.
Before being taken over by Kaufmann, this mine was
sampled over and over by the alleged best experts of
Kalgoorlie, and yet Fred Merton beat the lot.
• • • •
North Kalgurli (W.A.) is still under inspection
by Jim Henderson, late manager of Kalgurli Consols.
The mine, which now includes the Brookman’s Boulder,
has been so handled in the past that it is very difficult to
trace the lodes, or to form any idea whatever of their
values. Holes and drives of all sorts have been put in
promiscuously without any regard to system. A parcel
of 68 tons, taken from the east reef at 600 ft., has just
yielded 4£oz. per ton at the Dapto smelters, but the policy
of rooting out such ore and despatching it at a cost of £7
a ton is more than questionable.

• * • •
Vesuvius (Charters Towers) is in eruption.
Some remarks in this column about that dreadful mine
enraged the Charters Towers Eagle, which says that The
Bulletin’s Wild Cat Column is an Ass—that it has no
knowledge of practical mining, and that it is more at
home in examining the bottom of a long beer glass than,
a mine plan. Since the Eagle’s screech, Vesuvius shares
have fallen from buyers 245. to sellers 16s. 9d., but—
quoting the reputable N.Q. Register —“ as this is a
speculative stock fluctuations are not much noticed.”
The Wild Cat sees no reason to modify the “ silly para-
graph,” in which it said “ Vesuvius may continue sinking
until Sheol is reached and probably the only gold that
will be seen will be in the spiked forks used by Nicholas,
for toasting mining liars.” An indignant correspondent
writes on the same subject and suggests that the Wild
Cat Column should be put into a sack and pitched over
South Head. He prophecies that when the Wild Cat
Column is “writhing in the tortures of hell” Vesuvius
mine will be paying big divs. At that rate, it is going to
be a remarkably long time before the Vesuvius pays,
dividends.

“Flabby Paul” writes: Jordan Creek G.M.
Co. (Geraldton, N.Q.), has to contend with hard luck ;
Co. is trying to erect a “ ten-head ” battery ; has to pay
£3O a ton carriage on machinery from head of tramway, a
distance of 20 miles. So far (in three months) only two
waggons have succeeded in reachingche battery site ; by
frightful roads, over precipitous hills. A surveyor is on
the grbund attempting to bring water from a creek two
miles Away ; battery is to be driven by water power. 500
tons of ore at grass, reputed to go 3Joz. to the ton. Wet
season has set in, making roads worse than before. In all
probability ’twill be at least another six months before
operations can commence.

Reported that Ernest Govett, ex-Broken Hill
journalist, and Randolph Bedford, of Everywhere, are
associated in the management of a big lead-zinc mine in
Italy, the former as general manager, and the latter as
assistant. Talking will doubtless be left to Bedford, who
will soon acquire command of all the expletives in the
Piedmontese vocabulary. The average Italian workman
is said to be like the average Australian bullock—no good
unless sworn at violently and persistently-
The newly-appointed Governor of Malta
is another Clarke —Sir Charles Mansfield Clarke,
This outbreak of Clarkes grows bewildering.
One of the inmates of Dunwich (Q.) work-
house is an ex-aide-de-camp to a long-dead
Queensland Governor. Later on he was an
inspector of police, but a run of bad luck brought
him by stages to the vocations of lime-burner,
surveyor’s cook and rouseabout, and last of all to
Dunwich.
Colonel Lynch’s father fought at Eureka
Stockade against England with Lalor —the man
who earned the distinction of having been twice
condemned to death for treason, and then becoming
Speaker of the Victorian Assembly. So Lynch’s
early education didn’t fit him to accurately esti-
mate the consequences of “treason.”
Barr-Smith (partner and brother-in-law of
the late Thomas Elder, the most munificent of
Adelaide citizens), is supposed to have an income
of over £50,000 a year. He has married off all his
daughters and set up all his sons on their own,
and so is accumulating cash rapidly, for his living
expenses do not amount to a tenth of his revenue.
The absui'd anachronism of charging
Lynch with treason and sentencing him to death
(sentence afterwards commuted to the almost as
savage one of perpetual imprisonment) was clearly
shown in the indictment that he,
not having the fear of God in his heart, and being moved
and seduced thereto by the instigation of the Devil, did
aid the enemies of the Queen, ahd by force and arms did
unlawfully, maliciously, and Jraitorously take an oath
of allegiance to the Queen 5 ? enemies, and did take up
arms against her v 'and incite others to do the same.
Eancy a personal, instigating Devil in the 20th
century! .Thereare, by the way, now two Aus-
tralians doomed to end their days in English gaols
—Lynch for fighting with the Boers, and Witton
for fighting against the Boers in a clumsily en-
thusiastic fashion.

A small woman, with a baby and a black
eye, lately put a question to the Bench, at Rich-
mond (Vic.), that practically knocked nine cen-
turies of British civilisation into a cocked hat.
Her husband, who was responsible for the black
eye aforesaid, was given “ .£5 or a month,” and
elected to “take it out.” Then the small woman
stepped up to the Bench and pitifully asked who
was to keep her and her children while her hus-
band was in gaol. The Bench glared, cogitated,
and finally blurted out, “Dunno,” or words to
that effect. And the same answer has been given
for ages by'the Law, .Judges, Parliaments, Reform
Leagues, even the A.N.A. “Dunno!” A simple
question is put in a casual sort of way by a small,
unimportant woman, and the combined wisdom of
all Australia, with that of the whole British
Empire on top of it, cannot discover an answer.
Yet Germany has solved the problem. That out-
of-date, derided country makes such men as these
toil at remunerative occupations in gaol; maintains

INSOLVENT (cheerfully): “ That's all right; I won't quarrel over a pound or
two; just you stick it in with the rest of the debts."
them in a strictly.thrifty manner, and devotes the
proceeds of their industry to maintaining the wife
and children. Seems as if Australia is too beastly
proud to learn from Germany.
• • • *
Maoriland is suffering from an epidemic of
sneak thievery. Quite a swarm of men wander
about, knocking at doors ; when the doors are
opened they give a military salute and inquire,
“Does Mr. Brown live here?” If there is no
answer to the knock, the ex-hero from South
Africa reconnoitres through the window, and then
climbs in and loots. Almost every member of the
sneak-thief brigade has the military salute habit.
Lowther Clarke, Melbourne’s new Anglican
bishop, has already managed to offend some of
his holy people. It is alleged that on the day of
his enthronement he took what is known as the
“eastward position” at the Communion. Anumber
of his clergy and laity were shocked at this ter-
rible “eastward position,” and are to address a
protest to the bishop. There is supposed to be
some unholy connection between the “ eastward
position ” and the R.C. church ceremonials, and
if poor Clarke doesn’t be careful he will have the
whole of the yellow brigade down upon him like a
billion tons of sulphur.
* • • •
The enthronement (“ My kingdom is not
of this world”) of Melbourne’s new Bishop,
Lowther Clarke, was truly typical of Christianity,
with its respectable but stodgy followers, and
their respectable but uncomely wives and
daughters. Even the House of God itself was
placarded “Admission by ticket only,” “Blue
tickets this way,” and so forth; and the eternal
flashlight camera fiend must needs throw in his
cheap imitation of the light that streams from the
Great White Throne. Already the new prelate
is being advertised as “ fully in touch ” with the
working classes of Lancashire and Yorkshire. The
Melbourne worker grins sardonically.
• • • •
South Australia seems likely to add one
more to its series of unsolved mysteries. According
to the available evidence old man Ninnes went to
bed with a lighted lamp and a bottle of brandy,
and during the night he blazed up so effectually
that he died. There are stories about another
man who, Mrs. Ninnes declared, wasn’t about the
house at the time of the fire; also a servant says
the other man was there, and that both he and
Mrs. Ninnes asked her not to mention his
presence; and there is the energetic swear of the
other man that the servant’s evidence is untrue.
Also there is an untraceable report that old man
Ninnes blazed up just in time to prevent him
altering his will —in fact, the air is full of rumors.
Meanwhile the coroner’s jury gives it up.

A Prahran (Vic.) Bench of Shallows has
fined a man for leading an unlighted bicycle at
night! What is the penalty in Prahran for lead-
ing an unlighted dog ?

Smith and Jones, civil servants, got a cir-
cular from their respective departments asking
them to state their religion. Smith told Jones
he would decline to state his, as a matter of prin-
ciple. Jones said, “I’m not ashamed of my
religion. L’llgivemine.” SpakoSmith: “That’s
not the point. I’m not ashamed of mj' back,
but I’m condemned if I’ll show it to either Pope
Dill Macky or Cardinal Moran.”
An instance of the brutal squalor of
Salvarmy administration. In
country town two young girls,
“captain” and “lieutenant,”
had charge of the local
“Army,” consisting of
three kanakas, a Chow,
and the town grocer,
who backslided into
drink regularly
every fortnight.
Rent of hall and
cottage (16s. week-
ly) had to be col-
lected before the
girls had a penny
for food; first
charge on collec-
tions being 10 per
cent, towards divi-
sional officer’s
salary. The captain
often had to draw
on the scant savings
of her servant-girl
days, when she was
more sensible than
saved, to keep star-
vation away from
herself and mate.
It was absolutely
necessary to barri-
cade their doors at
night to prevent the
“saved” Kanakas
from getting into their
room to discuss abstruse
points in theology ; and
also to keep the kitchen
fire burning at night to
stop the grocer from get-
ting down the chimney to
report that he had again
backslidden. The Chow
convert used to tap the
window at intervals dur-
ing the night with “lilly
plesent for missie. Pleasee
open doo’.” And the Army-
headquarters’ annual contribution towards keep-
ing the doors locked was only .£1 for uniform.

THE MEN WHO ARE PAID TO LOSE.
FIRST RACING CYCLIST : “ I’d like to see Bosher
win this race."
SECOND DITTO : “ That's a dirty thing to say.
He never done you no harm."
A love of the dramatic has been the
undoing of more men than one, noted and
otherwise, and it was certainly responsible for the
miscarrying of the plans of John Ball in Bendigo
Criminal Court, t’other day. Ball said that,
“ Rather than go to Pentridge for another term,
I’ll cut my throat.” Then he produced a knife
and out his throat in the dock. Of course, he
failed—a perfect heap of constables fell on him
before he was half through with the job. If he
had said nothing he might have cut his throat in
his cell at leisure without two tons of fatted con-
stable throwing itself upon him. Now his yearn-
ing for dramatic effect has caused him to lose his
knife and his opportunity, and he has gained
nothing but a casual newspaper paragraph.
Sunday morning in Fiji as described by
the Rev. R. W. Nolan, Methodist missionary:
My brother missionary, Mr. Burton, and I were in-
formed that the Catholics were preparing to sav mass in
our Protestant church. We jumped out of bed,‘hurriedly
dressed ourselves, and ran with ail haste to the cnurch.
We got there just in time to see the candles alight and
two priests putting on their robes. Many of the natives
had assembled, and the service was about to begin. My
pulpit was turned upside down, and arranged as an altar.
Mr. Danks inquired what all this meant. In the mean-
time I gathered the people at the other end of the church
and started singing a hymn from our hymn-book at the
top of my voice. The natives joined in the hymn, and
we, by this means, got a start with our service. We con-
tinued this service while the priests looked on. They
dared not interfere with the service, and by this means
we prevented them from holding theirs. The priests
admitted the game was up and put out the lights.
When Governor Maogregor took charge of British
New Guinea one of the first things he did was to
mark out missionary spheres and forbid, under
penalties, the missioner of one sphere crossing
into the territory of another. Something like this
seems to be necessary in missionary lands
generally. Only for public opinion and the police
force even Australia would need to be so parried
out.
BOY LOVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The cruel years that lead through so much sin and sorrow
Have taken all my boyhood’s trust, the simple faith
That held one woman’s hand and, holding, feared no
morrow
And would have held it to th; very gates of Heath !
Ah! Boyish love! the jest of earth, the greybeards
scorning!—
Yet not for all the light and lore of after years
Would I have lost the flush of that first golden morning,
Those love-tossed nights, that passion, and those tears !
WILL H. OGILVIE.
TO “ DIMTLES.”
I’m dying for you nearly,
And yet I cannot dare
(Ah, me ! I love you dearly !)
The matrimonial snare,
With prices pointing queerly.
Like demons of despair.
If blindly I have fooled you
(Oh ! Cupid seldom sees !)
If, Dimples—if I schooled you
In passing pleasantries;
And if, my dear, I gulled you—
Ah, you’ll forgive me, please?
Far better to forsake you
I-i easy, early life
Than, thinking not,.to take you
Through starving years of strife j
Far kinder than to make you
A wan, repentant wife.
Go, seek some true-heart fellows
Who richer are than I
Fll never inure be jealous
(The old, untruthful cry !)
Till hard existence mellows
In kinder years, good-bye!

REFLECTIONS.
The sun never sets on the British empire ; ergo, said
sun never goes down on a Britisher’s wrath.
Accept everything in the spirit in which it is given—
even water in whisky.
Give good advice, that your son may see the folly of
your bad example.
Will oats grow best by gaslight.
A punter is one who, after backing four winners and
?P en ,., ln £> ” ls tram-fare on a beer, knows a dead cert for
the fifth event.
A man who believes in loans would pawn his boots to
get his socks dauaed.
Live, eat, sleep, drink, think, work and play well, that
thy days may be less like the hell some peopie make for
themselves in evading an alleged purgatory.
Slow horses and fast women send their patrons to the
well, a dog always knows when he’s had enough.
one comes as I gaze into the empty glass—Were
the barmaid less anxious to put a good head on it, a
longer neck would accrue and more beer be sold thereby.

The usual stone-throwing mystery tran-
spired at the Glebe (Sydney) t’other day, and, on
being solved, was found to have eventuated in the
same old way. A local resident found stones,
children’s tops, lumps of coal, half bars of soap,
etc., crashing through his windows atodd moments,
but for a time could find no sign of the perpetra-
tor. On the police being called in the bombard-
ment ceased. The officer, however, understood
stone-throwing mysteries, and interviewed the
small household drudge, who owned up to the job
with tears, but could give no explanation of her
conduct. The desire of the small slavey to pitch
household utensils around is as unaccountable as
it is persistent.
• • • •
That luckless woman who has apparently
been so near to coming in for a large share of the
Tyson estate, but who has twice been biffed out
of court by reason of a lack of certainty about her
pedigree, represents Tantalus in real life. On the
stage anybody—especially the villain—can prove
his pedigree in five minutes. He gets a copy of
the birth certificate of Ferdinand Bad egg, and the
fact of his having that in his hand proves that he
is Ferdinand Badegg ; the policeman looks at the
document and hands over the estate at once. But
in real life the law considers the plaintiff
coldly over the rim of its spectacles, and asks her
to prove that she is the person mentioned
in the document. Anyone can buy Isaac’s
birth certificate very cheaply; it is in the
Bible. But the possession of a Bible
doesn’t prove that you are Isaac. In real
life plaintiff has to get barrow-loads of affidavits
from the oldest inhabitant, and to prove by old
letters that the lady mentioned in the certificate
had the same strawberry mark on her back that
plaintiff possesses; and then the Law lifts its
bleared eye from the documents and decides that
another barrow-load of corroboration is required.
It isn’t at all like the dramatist’s off-hand way of
throwing huge estates around two or three times
a day.
• • • •
Sydney has been following the example of
Melbourne, and gathering in a few of the fortune-
telling brigade on a charge of getting money by
false pretences. They are mostly a pitiably
ignorant crowd who seem to have taken to reveal-
ing the past, present and future because they
didn’t know enough to take in washing. One of
the gang who told fortunes by the planets, and
also expounded the state of the patient’s health,
prescribed for him and treated him by massage,
admitted in court that she didn’t know what a
planet was and had never heard the name of one.
She merely babbled about them as part of her
stock-in-trade This seems a remarkable case of
escaping information. Even a charwoman often
chances to hear something about a planet at least
once in her life.
AN ATTACK IN FRONT.
BALLET SKIRT: “ Why does that Thompson woman
keep her head turned away from the johnny,
and that grin on her face?"
FEATHER KILT: “She took her teeth out in the
dressing-room, and I hid them."
A correspondent (surely a profane man)
to the Age suggests as a means to make the com-
munion cup more healthy:—
To provide each communicant with a straw through
which the “ mouthful of wine ” could be absorbed. The
straws could afterwards be “ burned with fire." This
method would be clean, inexpensive and expeditious, and
should offend no susceptibilities. We have recent
priestly and kingly precedent for the rehearsing of
ceremonies, and a little private rehearsal would enable
the veriest novice to become expert in the use of the
straw.
It suggests a weird picture of a congregation
bringing its own straw, and chewing it medita-
tively, or wearing it behind its ear while waiting
events.

The non-maturing of Majah Randal Carey’s
title, or something, is going to be responsible for
dire and grave happenings. Of late, Sydney
Dairy Telegraph has been carefully dropping the
hardly-earned handles of politicians, and twice
within one week the public has read that
“Mr.” John See did something or other,
and that "Mr.” Joseph Ward, of M.L., has
been over here; while several dimmer public
lights have also been decapitated seemingly
with malice aforethought. Soon _ the public
will pick up its daily penn’orth in fear and
trembling lest it should suddenly strike something
like this: “Mr. Wettin yesterday opened the
British Parliament with a brilliant speech
(obviously written by somebody else) which he
delivered in a thick voice with a strong German
accent. , . . England must be in sore straits
indeed when she is compelled to go to foreign
countries (and more particularly, to Germany) for
her rulers.”

* • * »
The recent very successful raid by the Yic.
’tecs on the horde of fortune-telling harpies that
infest Melbourne and suburbs is something worth
copying in other cities. The batch (20 or so) run
in contained a rare variety of imposters, from the
shrivelled and slatternly hags—almost all called
. Madame ” —who should have been dropped
into the nearest waterhole on general prin-
ciples, to a great hulking Ram Jam Sammy,
with tin medals and brass brooches stuck all over
him. Women and girls shivered in the gorgeous
presence of this hoodlum, and he treated his
clients to all sorts of indignities, according to
their degrees of foolishness. Ram was the most
arrogant minion in the crowd, and did about the
best business as a consequence. He used to spit
and scoff copiously in presence of his clients, aud
when telling the detective’s fortune—and doing it
very badly—he dropped off to sleep. An ordinary
client would be expected, in such cases, to wait
patiently and not breathe too loud for fear of
waking Ram Jam Sam, but the ’tec shook his
nigger up contemptuously and took him away by
the ear.

Some months back a good-looking married
woman cleared out with another man from a back-
blox town. They settled at Broken Hill, and the
substitute got a job
in the mines. He also
got leaded, and died.
The lady then returned
to her lawful owner,
and was forgiven. They
went rabbitting, and
li f e went smoothly un-
til a teamster camped
by the gate. She took
a sudden interest in
teamsters, and when
this one left the boun-
dary fence she went
too as off-sider. Some
miles down the road
an accident put him
in the hospital for a
long spell. She re-
turned to her lawful
owner, and was for-
given some more. They
then got a married
couple’s billet on a
station where the only
other man was the
manager. Two months
of peace followed ; and
the lady was missing some more. She’d gone off
with the manager, who’d got another billet.
“Never mind, Jack,” a friend said, consolingly;
“you jes’wait quietly; she’ll come back to you
ag’in when he dies.” But this time the lawful
owner threatens to be unforgiving.
• • * •
After scaring the liquor crowd nearly to
death, the Pump party in Maoriland has drawn a
long breath and started to fight the foe a few
more rounds. Down in the whisky province,
Otago, they have declared that they are going to
sweep the last remaining beers off the earth. The
announcement was made a day or two prior to the
date fixed for hearing the petition of Bung to
have the poll in Dunedin —whereby the hotels are
to be reduced —declared invalid. The temperance
people’s lawyers turned up with a cubic mile of
evidence, but Bung discovered that he had no
proof to offer, and that, in effect, he was not sure
why he ever tried to upset the poll at all. From
Dunedin also comes the news that the free lunch
is going to become one of the “ has-beens.”
The gift feed and the gratis oyster are
going to melt gradually and beautifully
out of sight, like the sunlight of an autumn
day. As the man who consumes the unpaid-for
sausage-roll and oyster is never by any chance
a prohibitionist, it is hard to see where
Bung’s diplomacy comes in. Perhaps the
idea is to placate the restaurant-keepers,
who hate to see a man buy a small beer,
retire to a corner of the bar and engulf enough
provisions to make him fat and arrogant, and who
voted No-License as a protest against the free
lunches. One way and another, Bung is getting
a harried look, and the coat-tails of his mental
clothing are frayed, and he has a wild glare in his
eye, and firmly believes that he is the modern
Ishmael. He has been so badly rattled that he is
almost ready to throw up the sponge, the fight,
and the whole busted universe in a writhing mass,
utter a large, delirious yell and take to the bush.

Sydney University’s grand ideas of cele-
brating the jubilee of the institution came to an
UPWARDS.
LAWYER : “ Well, you can only get a divorce
from your husband for desertion when lei’s
gone three years or upwards.”
SHE: “ Well, he’s not gone three years, and Ids
not gone upwards; so, I suppose, there’s
nothing to be done."
inglorious end with the finish of the junketting.
It was proposed to raise a permanent mark of the
great event in the shape of buildings to be used
for clubs, &c., and to attain this end presidents,
committees, and sub-committees appointed them-
selves. But these bodies first began to adjourn
their meetings, then postpone the adjournments,
then to forego the postponements, and finally
dropped the whole thing. It went the same way
as our permanent grief about the death of Queen
Victoria, and our perpetual remembrance of the
services of our Farm-burning Contingents, and
many other perpetual things which fell like a
drop of water on the hob of Below, and fizzed
there for a second, and then were seen no more.
Now that the lethal chamber is at work fft
Moore Park, Sydney, there should be no longer
hesitation about getting rid of all dogs that rush at
passers-by. The rush of a dog, particularly if the
animal be above medium size, should be held to
be an assault. It is all very well for the owner to
say, “It is only his way; he barks and jumps
around, but never bites.” If the owner barked
and jumped around he would be justly suppressed,
and his dog should be similarly treated.
•*' • •
Melbourne City Council abattoirs are
located on the Saltwater River, in the midst of
the noxious trades area. To make quite sure that
the meat will have all reasonable chances of con-
tamination, the council has had erected next the
abattoirs a dessicator, where it destroys
All sorts of refuse, including stinking fish, bad oysters,
and decaying rabbits, which are rejected at the city fish*
markets. Loads of this manure material are taken from
the city to the desiccator every week, and the vehicles in
which it is carried are literally smothered with flies,
myriads of which, it is safe to assume, after feeding on
the filth, transfer their attentions and the germs that
have attached themselves to their bodies to the con-
tiguous killing pens. Not far from the abattoirs lie heaps
of refuse known as “ hashmagandy,” a kind of semi-solid
malodorous matter, really the residuum of the offal from
boiling-down vats, &c.,; and although this is kept
covered and enclosed as much as possible, it constitutes an
additional danger.
Yet a Melbourne man assured this paper, the
other day, that the meat-supply of his city was
“almost perfect.” Had he ever inspected the
“ hashmagandy ” ?
• * • *
After seriously considering the ways of
Parson M'Cullagh, of Sydney, and his supersti-
tious following of faith-healers, this paper would
like to suggest the addition of some new tenets to
their creed. The M’Cullagh crowd professes to
believe in healing the human body by prayer
instead of sending for a doctor, and as the human
body often recovers without either prayer or a
doctor, it is impossible to always verify the sect’s
pretensions. But if the sect will add to its belief
a faith in healing the window by prayer without
the aid of a glazier, and the boots without the
assistance of a bootmaker, that will be a square
and honest test. Boots never recover by their
own volition, nor do broken windows or pants
that have begun to go. But when a trial of the
faith under conditions like these is suggested, the
sects always get out of the difficulty by denounc-
ing the proposition as “blasphemous.”

J.F.D.*’ as to old-age-pensions payments
in N.S.W.
I live a few doors from the southern branch of the Bank
of N.S.W., corner of George and Regent streets, and the
scrambling, bustling crowd of played-out humanity that
stood around its doors a few days back from 9 a.m. till
3 p.m. was a disgrace to a civilised community. Poor
old hobbling wretches, barely able to stand, arrived in
cabs, and then had to sit for hours on the kerbstone
before the crush slackened. One old dame informed me
that she dreaded the pay-day coming with the thoughts
of that push and scramble and long wait. Surely an
extra clerk or two could be put on at busy paying offices
for the day, and the pension given decently and
humanely.
“ 00100 ” supplies a tale of horror :
Re “ Cheetah ’’ (B. 28/2/’O3) and incompetent medicos.
I know a case where a Vic. country doctor opened a man
for an appendicitis operation, and, after gouging around
in the hole he had dug, had to close it up without finding
the appendix. The patient developed dangerous
symptoms, and his friends had him removed to Mel-
bourne in an almost moribund condition. One of the Mel-
bourne high-priced surgeons re-opened the patient, and
found the wound made by the other doctor in a very foul
state from pure uncleanliness on the part of the operator.
The Melbourne man found the appendix in the very hole
dug by the first man, although that flesher had actually
proposed sinking a fresh shaft in the patient. He also
discovered a piece of gauze that No. 1 had inadvertently
•ewed up in the unfortunate patient’s anatomy. The
victim survived, and is again recovering strength, and he
himself told me the story. If he had died of the wound
made by No. 1, it is highly improbable that the finding of
the foreign matter in the wound, although it were dis-
closed by the autopsy, would ever have been heard of.
Medical men stick together as close as wax so long as
offences committed are mere trivial delinquencies such as
killing a patient. But should a practitioner offend against
the awful etiquette of the profession by saving a man’s
life when a brother practitioner was killing him, then let
the offender look out for squalls.

From Melb. Argus (4/3/’O3) •
An emaciated young woman, carrying a very young
baby, Thispresentedaterrible spectacle; its handsand feet
were skeleton-like, the nails growing beyond the fingers
like claws. Its eyes were glassy, its skin almost trans-
parent, and its face sunken and haggard. Another child,
aged two years, was fearfully emaciated and ghastly
white, its limbs being merely skin and bone, and its eyes,
owing to their sunken sockets, appearing extraordinarily
large. All three were manifestly dying of starvation.
This isn’t taken from a report of a Chinese or
Indian missionary, but what was actually seen—it
was a case of wife desertion, as usual—at Mel-
bourne City Court, at the very moment when
Bishop Clarke was being enthroned in an odor of
sanctity and an atmosphere of respectability. Had
it not been .for the constable who found the above
trio, they would have been dead in a very few
days.

A lad of 18 giving evidence against a
woman at Melb. Criminal Court, stated that he
had known the prisoner for 100 years—ever since
her husband died, in fact. Cross-examined as to
how long it was since the husband died the witness
said “six weeks,’’after which he gave “twelve
years ” as the length of time the woman had been
a widow. Finally the amusing youth pleaded
guilty to the nick-name of Silly Tony. In a
district where he didn t happen to have become
known as Silly Tony his evidence might have
Ilxnged somebody.

“It was generally servant-girls and
ignorant persons that were deluded by fortune-
tellers,” said Crown Prosecutor Harris in a case
last week. “ Servant-girls and ignorant persons ! ”
And people like Mr. Harris wonder that girls
refuse to become domestic servants and have gibes
like that thrown at them. The statement was
foolish as well as insulting. Two or three fortune-
tellers, at all events, find their best patrons
among the well-to-do Sydneyites.
WE ATHER-BOUND.
PARSON: “ You didn’t come to church this morn-
ing, Johnnie?”
JOHNNIE: “No, sir. Please, sir, it was too wet,
sir, so I went out bird-nestin’, sir.”
Australian labor-unions are seeking to
introduce the “union-label” from America to
this continent. The system provides that em-
ployers of union-labor shall have issued to them
by the labor council special labels to mark their
goods ; union workmen are then urged to patronise
only the union-label articles. The first effort is to
be made with the breweries, though how the label
will be persuaded to stick to draught beer it is
hard to say.

The estate of Wivenhoe, Camden (N.S.W.),
is in the market for sale. It was long the home
of Charles Cowper, and was presented to him by
public subscription.
