for
A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, March 10, 1903.
My Dear Arini,—
There are likely to be some startling political
developments from the clash of woman’s ignorance
with man’s guile in affairs of State. One recent
Sydney suburban meeting of women, convened to
assist the cause of Liberal Reform, was faced by
a proposal from one lady, who had evidently been
sitting at the feet of an unwise shepherd, that
what the country needed as a cure for the drought
and the borrowing policy was the dismissal of all
Roman Catholics from employment. She had
this glowing idea in proper resolution form too,
and seemed hurt when it didn’t find prompt
acceptance. I haven’t heard on the other side, as
yet, of a lady with the front name of Veronica
proposing as her political panacea that anybody
refusing to keep St. Patrick’s Day holy should be
executed without the option of a fine; but possibly
that proposition is also being got ready.
The dearth of the capable servant is becoming
as severe as the dearth of grass in the west. Spite
of hard times, the serving damsel known as a
“ general ”is scarcer than ever. For consolation,
missuses might read H. G. Wells’ optimistic
“ Anticipations”—the chapter dealing with “ Cer-
tain Social Relations.” After sketching the
average home of 1950, the author says the manage
will, in all probability, be servantless for two very
excellent reasons. First, because it will not want
servants; and second, because it would not get
them if it did:
The only good servants of to-day are the chil-
dren of servants, or the children of the old labor
base of the social pyramid. Machinery has smashed
that base and scattered its fragments. The tra-
dition of self-respecting inferiority is being utterly
destroyed in the world. In the community of the United
States no native-born race of white servants has ap-
peared, and the emancipated young negress degenerates
towards the impossible. . , . Such servants as wealth
will retain will be About as really loyal and servile as
hotel waiters and on the same terms.
The average house of the future that Mr. Wells
anticipates will be a practically automatic flat.
The great proportion of the servants’ duties of
to-day consists of drudgery that the stupidities of
our present methods of house construction entail,
and which the sanely-constructed house of the
future will avoid. There will be no stair climb-
I gather. Many of the “situations wanted ”
in Sydney advts. .stipulate for no stairs and a gas
stove. Wells writes contemptuously of the fact
that in existing houses the skirting-board meets
the floor at right angles, and so makes sweeping
twice as troublesome. The use of tarnishable
metals, the lack of warming appliances (which
makes for a mighty fetching and carrying of
coal), the awful “washing-up” (when it should
be. possible to immerse all dirty tableware in a
suitable solvent and then run that off for the
articles to dry) are items discussed. Ingenuity
of sanitary fittings should reduce the work “up-
stairs ” to bed-making only, and, says the man
writer. “ a bed does not take five minutes to
make, ” and goes on :
There remains the oooking. To-day cooking with the
incidentals is a very serious business, the coaling, the
ashes, the horrible moments of heat, the hot black things
to handle, the silly vague recipes, the want of neat
apparatus. One always imagines a cook working with
crimsoned face and bare blackened arms. But with a
neat little range heated by electricity and provided with
thermometers, with quite oontrollable temperatures and
proper heat screens, cooking might very easily be made a
pleasant amusement for invalid ladies.
The houses of the future, by the way, will have no
ohimneys. None will be required and the roof
will be an addition to the garden space of the
home. Will some Sydney speculator experiment
with the erection of a series of labor-saving houses ?
Before I visited Hobart I used to hear that the
native-born men left it as soon as they came to
years of. discretion, and so caused an overplus of
old maids. Society and sub-society, and the
fringe of society (there are as many cliques as
hills), is frightfully over-girled; but to walk
through the city streets after night-fall you’d
think that. Hobart was over-manned. It is as
crowded with men every night as Sydney is on a
Saturday evening. Silent, orderly, smoking,
decently-dressed, empty-eyed, the chosen vessels
loaf along the centre of their wide, clean, traffic-
less streets, or stand hundreds deep round a street
singer or Salvation ranter.
The forenoon is the time to see the Hobart
damsel, representative of all the laminations of its
nice society. She swings along quickly, swaying
one arm ; the hand of the other holds her long-
tailed frock tight around her slender figure. She
is mostly slender, and mezzo in coloring, and
carries herself prettily, and has a longer stride
than the Sydney girl, but, in spite of her soft
climate and skies—“ the hollow of God’s hand ”
she looks older than her years, and ages soon.
Mental stagnation, I suppose, is the cause.
There’s a curious primness about her. She is
reminiscent, of some of Mary Wilkins’s New
England girls, who. spend their lives sewing
rag mats and making “sass” of whatever
fruit is in season. And as she hasn’t
got possession of her own identity or ego,
she seems to be in deadly fear that the clique
above her—the clique she lives in hope of entering
—will say that she’s not a lady, and will dis-
continue its (supercilious) recognition when she
and it meet on the Anglican church door-mat.
Then, of course, the abiding fear that “ the men
won’t come ” must be a crow-footing thing in this
sweet land of no outlook.
In New York or London the domination of
clique over clique, the wriggles and heart-burnings
of the society-aspirant, are pathetic—but under-
standable. In this little flower-starred slumber
land, where there is really no class, and where
grandfathers are not as numerous as tourists, these
things are to the onlooker muoh funnier than
seeing a stout gentleman sit down where thejre is
no chair.
A strange feature of the Dolores enthusiasm is
that her most ardent admirers are women. Mdft
certainly do get excited, and wave their hats and
brandish their walking-sticks in a dangerous man-
ner when she sings, but her own sex goes quite
mad. Women claw at her, trying to kiss her
hands, and snatch fragments of her bouquets and
drapery as souvenirs. Said that Mdlle. has been
offered a lucrative permanent appointment in the
State Opera House, Vienna. Vienna is said to be
a charming place to live in and to contain the
finest brass bands in the world.
Miss Linda O’Hara’s concert at Sydney
Y.M.O.A. hall last week revealed a robust young
lady of about seventeen, with her ’tween-shades
hair hanging over a dainty frock of white. Her
ohest development indicated great lung power,
and her singing revealed a contralto voice of con-
siderable quality in the middle register. There
were flashes of beautiful tone and organ-like
vibrations, which indicated that properpreparation
should bnng to light a vein of rich melody. But
the singer is only a girl, and her voice as yet is
quite und v eloped.
The liov Dunstan (Sydney) having filed his
schedule (debts £3106, assets £74) shows two
widows in the list of creditors; one heads it with a
debt of £316 65., a seoond is close up with £250.
The widow’s mite always does suffer when the
church gets into difficulties. One pathetic-look-
ingentry is “Alfred Fathers, sexton, £55.” It may
not be so in this case, but the popular idea of a
sexton is a grey-haired old man supporting his
declining days with great difficulty by looking
after “ interments. ” I hope that the sexton to
whom Mr. Dunstan owes £55 is younger and more
prosperous than the usual type.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The real’Varsity clique was conspicuous by
its absence t’other Saturday, when the University
Extension Board treated suburban ‘persons’ and
others to tea and twaddle. It wouldn’t do, you
know, for best professional butter to spread
itself on minnows, who dabble in Extension shal-
lows —the presumptuous tadpoles might pose as
possessors of real University culture if they
weren’t kept in their places. Ungrudging old
Prof. Laurie, one of the exceptions, squired a
galaxy of girls to the tea-rooin. On the platform,
Father Bevan prattled of ‘ Mediaeval Universi-
ties,’ but the girls near me drowned his voice,
tittering over one of their number’s chronicles of
the lecturer’s last jaunt abroad. ’Board ship, so
the narrator averred, there was a fire-scare. Out
burst the startled Bevan from his berth, with all
his hoary head in curl-papers.
After the addresses, visitors went over the
affiliated colleges. ‘Trinity,’ the Anglican fold,
a plain, old building, wrapped tenderly in ivy, is
the fairest to look upon, in my humble opinion,
‘Ormond,’ built and endowed by rich Presby-
terians, is almost too ornate and palatial, though
its dining-hall, with carved, high-backed chairs,
dais, etc., all solid oak, is a noble room. Wesleyan
‘Queen’s,’barrack-like without and shabby within,
nevertheless offers more opportunities to its in-
mates of putting on flesh than the neighboring
institutions, so say sharp-set students. The
master of ‘ Queen’s ’ is an Englishman, while
‘ Ormond ’ and ‘ Trinity ’ are ruled by Scot and
Irishman respectively. John Bull, as an epicure,
can give points to Paddy and Sandy and beat
them easily.
“The State Governor’s charming daughter is
preparing to decamp to Marchesi for further
lessons in singing. Miss Clarke does not go with
Melba—her plans are made independently of her
whilom friend. Eighteen months will be the term
of tuition. ‘ I need quite 18 months more work—
and I love Marchesi.' There is some talk of Miss
Mars Buckley taking the trip with Miss Clarke.
The two have been close eh urns.
“ The dame of Pastor Bevan has set going a
series of petticoat Congresses, with tea and cake
accompaniments, to train the woman voter in the
way she should go. Light is to be thrown on
political conundrums by well-seasoned speakers;
and domestics and fashionables are to be invited to
be enlightened. Mrs. Bevan is an ornamental
lady, and does some parish good in a queenly way,
beneath an inspiring head drape of real lace.
. “ With Miss Mary Clarke and her brother, the
lieutenant, back from the war, the Cliveden widow
has all her children round her again. Now that
her two sons have ‘done time’ in the army and
law respectively, their mother wants them to
settle down in Australia.
“The new Anglican Bishop, following the
example of his predecessors, preached his first
regular sermon last Sunday morning (March Bth)
in St. James’ Church, formerly the Anglican pro-
cathedral. Sixty years ago, when this old church
was new, it loomed large, standing in the midst
of paddocks and visible from the decks of all
the vessels nearing Melbourne. But its career as
a landmark has been over for many a day, and for
long enough it has ranked only as a half-forgotten
ecclesiastical relic, poked away in Little Collins-st.,
dwarfed and overshadowed by such buildings as
those of James Service and Co., St. James Building
Society, the Australian Club and Menzies’ Hotel.
Poor old shrine, not only are its best days over,
but its worst are still to come, if there be any
truth in the rumor that the Church, unable to
trouble further with its unprofitable old member,
means to sell it for commercial purposes.
Bishop Lowther Clarke is a fine big, person-
able P^T son > as mu ?b like our Prime Minister as a
i-Ooy Barton profile, wavy, gray hair growing
low on his forehead, and projecting lips can make
him. In height, aotivity, and genial smile, the
promoted rector has all the beet of it. A parson
evidently keeps his looks better on tea and buns
than a politician on turkey and fizz. Otherwise
the new prelate has a pleasant voice* and a tund
of common-sensical platitudes, and eyes set close
together, hinting at North Country caution and
canny shrewdness. However, for all his pawky
reserve, some of his new clergy have already found
him out to the extent of feeling themselves im-
pelled to address “His Lordship” a “respectful
remonstrance” re the “Eastward Position, as-
sumed by him when dispensing the Communion.
Poor man ! his troubles are beginning soon. The
new Bishop looks handsome, dignified, and
healthy. He will probably prove sensible, mas-
terful, and hardworking, but so far he doesn t
impress hearers as eloquent, deep, or bookish.
However, one can’t have everything. Poor old
Shepherd Goe was a kindly saint. His clergy
were fond but not proud of the gentle ancient,
whose appearance made those who came to pray
retire to smile. Bishop Moorehouse went down
well with the public, but subordinates felt no
calling to raise a tabernacle to their high-handed
boss. As for the Bishopess, parsons who felt her
yoke testify that ‘ ’twere better to be a dog and
bay the moon ’ than a curate in the Moorehouse
era.
‘ ‘ The new Bishopess, Mrs. Lowther Clarke, is
a bright-faced, little matron with the sort of
sunny smile that buttons up her eyes with dimples
if even the ghost of a joke steals into notice.
Hubby announced, plump and plain, at the
Mayor’s reception, that those who intend the
little lady’s life in Melbourne to be one long com-
mittee-meeting, must kill her lord to encompass
their designs.
“ From a matrimonial point of view the Durbar
seems to be panning out better, so far as Aus-
tralians are concerned, than did the Common-
wealth’s own reception of royalty. Experts re-
gard as a very promising streak of ‘color’ the
engagement of Miss Mary Clarke, of Cliveden, to
the Hon. Captain Lindsay, third son of the Earl
of Crawford, at present one of the Curzon’s
aides. The bride-elect, whose betrothal was
formally and authoritatively announced in the
Melb. daily papers, soon as she got from Delhi, is
a slight, blonde, retiring young lady, far more like
her paternal forbears, the easy-going, taciturn,
unceremonious Clarkes, than her energetic, zest-
ful mother, the widow of Cliveden.
‘ ‘ Of other wonderful engagements reported as
of Durbar origin, who has faith to spare for them?
The persistent hints thrown out by one haughty
clan as to the capture of a prize general with long,
gory S’Afrioan records, by the beauty of the
family, yet await authentication.
“A popular item at recent A.N.A. competi-
tions was the Melba singing contest, the diva
haying promised £7 to the amateur earning most
points inarthreefold test, which included (1) selec-
tion from opera or oratorio, (2) Australian ballad,
(3) British ditto. The result of the struggle
proved rather disappointing, the award falling to
a soprano, personally prosaic and vocall v mediocre,
though correct as to time and tune. Enthusiastic
‘Natives’ had pictured to themselves the winner
of Melba’s prize as some sensational young singer,
crowned with promise and haloed with possibili-
ties. Alas, although there was material on hand
not unlike the stuff such dreams were made of,
these warblers at the tests were sometimes guilty
of inaccuracy, prone in pathetic passages to
funeral-march their tempo, d la the Amy Castles
rendering of * Home, Sweet Home’; and that’s how
the favorites were beat!
“At the competitions, interest also flared up
round the unpublished, original musical works by
Australian composers. Fifty were sent in, most
of them creditable. Chief among the native-born
composers who tried their luck were Theodore
Tourrier, of Kew (Vic.), and' Horace Gleeson,
also of Melb., who shared first prize and also won
second prize and honorable mention, respec-
tively. Tourrier’s song, ‘ The Phantom,’
suffered, I thought, from a thunderous bass-
grumbling in the piano accompaniment, at the
mention of a ‘ pair of feet in slippers neat,’
the property of the phantom, which haunted
the composer. Light, treble friskings would
seem more in keeping with the ‘ blue-eyed sprite ’
referred to. Now, if the ‘phantom’ of the song
had been a fat German spook, with creaky boots
and asthmatic wheeze, basso pianoforte rumblings
would describe his ‘ atmosphere ’ to a T.
A certain advantageous post goes begging
because of the terrible fear all women entertain
towards the strenuous lady who offers it. The
first popular candidate to have her claims recog-
nised, and herself rushed in impetuous delight by
the employer, was said by the whole of Society to
be built by Nature and adorned by Fashion for
the post, and Toorak hurrahed her. Hardly had
it done so when one of the temper bombs of
the strenuous lady burst overhead, and when the
atmosphere was thoroughly cleared, the debris
swept aside, and the damage surveyed, the novice
was found to be missing from her new post.
“Polo excitement reached a high pitch on Fri-
day—Gymkhana day. The size of the crowd was
amazing. All the stations throughout Victoria
must have had their representatives on the spot
Toorak, in grand array, stormed the course—and
Kooyong provides a special vantage ground for
dress display. Who could speak of a diminished
wool-clip in the face of such extravagant displays ?
“General Hutton brought the Austrian Excel-
lency, Baron von Something, and presented him to
Governor Clarke. The negotiation of civilities
was carried on first in apologetic English, then in
wooden-legged German, and finally in French
Miss Clarke supplying the latter. The Governor
left talk with the notable foreigner in her hands.
On all sides one collided with returned coronators
and Durbarites. But not one instance of new
ideas in dress could I notice. The Misses Mary
Clarke, Brodribb, Grimwade, and a dozen others,
showed up in nondescript styles. The Rajahs are
voted by Miss Clarke —who was in the midst of
them, thanks to an energetic fianc£—to be most
charming and picturesque princes, but bores to
hold converse with.
“The Dianas’Bending-race provided the keenest
excitement of the day. A stranger variety of
riders, considering they were all of about co-
relative social degree, could not be imagined
outside a Folly ball-room set. Some wore linen
coats and bowlers, others the inky habit and
hard hat; a few struck a compromise between
warm weather and ordinary riding garments,
while one youngster came out in the popular
Honolulu garb, with the orthodox helmet
skewered to her wispisli hair. Mrs. Everard
Browne had backed out of the contest at the last
moment. Miss Buckley made the best display
for perfect accoutrements and showy mount, but
Miss Millicent Fairbairn, with her fair hair
plaited under her neat boxer, and an attempt at
the horsemanship ‘ that witches the world,’ cut a
finer figure. The race was won by Mrs. Johnson.
“For that bumper farewell-house, last Thurs-
day, Dolores dressed her plump person in a
glistening black, mildly illuminated with corals.
A deep coarse lace cape was a further concession
to the prevailing craze for relief. Facing a record
audience, after nearly two years’ acquaintance
with Australia, Dolores smiled a message of warm
thanks. The crowd had toiletted as if for a Cup
garden-party; albeit, rain and dust alternated
outside. One fine dress was that of Miss T.
Robertson —a Western girl of fortune. A
‘ Geisha ’ party thumped and raged for encores as
only sister artistes do when one of their number
farewells for matrimonial or other reasons. Miss
Carrie Moore, amongst them, looked more
brownish after a Sydney scorching, and added to
the sultry impression by a wintry garb of brown
frieze and warm red.
“Croquet has come to stay. Time was when
only curates and other mild ladies knew anything
of the game, but now the smart set patronises the
sport. At Kooyong, the other day, the first big
game of the season came off, and a great rally of
she-sports mustered to see it.
“Senator Dobson’s wife went to the Big Smoke
some months ago for her health’s sake. Now Tas-
manians are advised that the convalescent is
energetically negotiating for tbe starting of an
Austral Club in the cold country. Mrs. Dobson’s
name is associated with successes. She has ambi-
tion and will, and is a clear and fluent speaker.
So probably this new oiub—foolish as the idea
seems—will be formed.

of the necessity for youthfulness that her forceful
legs become almost an irritation in their friski-
ness. Madame Slap, doesn t look a sweet-and-20
heroine, so what’s the use of giving three busy
selves more work? Less skittishness and a re-
trenchment of smile are wanted. t
“ The women who haunt the massage parlors
for the removal of freckles should take heart.
John Oliver Hobbes, in her last book, says com-
posedly of one of her clever characters :
She sat well in the sunshine, which showed her nose
and cheek almost powdered with small freckles. They
were not unbecoming.
“ Time and chance were forward in helping a
Pentridge inmate (in for a miscarried financial
scheme) to cultivate three or four languages during
his years a 3 a Government guest. He came out
t’other day, looking bright, handsome and
elegant, his tongue, ’tis said, bristling with foreign
languages. He secured charge of the library
during his term, gained an excellent knowledge of
French, Italian and German, amassed a store of
literary lore, and grew famous at his post for
marvellous feats of memory. It was a pleasure
and a delight to have so fine a gentleman and
scholar on the premises, said fellow lodgers and
officials.
“A wandering scribe, who has been some years

The sun never sets on the British empire.
It is a good old boast, and quit© true, but as the
sun also never sets on the territory of Holland,
Portugal, Russia, the United States, France or
.Denmark, the boast isn’t nearly so exclusive as is
generally supposed.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The estate of the late Arthur Hodgson, of
Queensland, has been proved for probate purposes
at £l4 short of £60,000.
Labor members Holman and Hughes have
passed the final examination in law, and will pre-
sently be called to the N.S.W. Bar.
Arthur Griffith (N.S.W.), M.L.A., is
putting in part of the Parliamentary vacation
organising for the Labor party in Tasmania.
The late Queensland Minister for Lands,
William Bligh Henry O’Connell, was great-grand-
son of Governor Bligh and related to O’Connell
the Irish Liberator.
Squatter A. B. Murray, a member of the
first S.A. Parliament, recently celebrated his
87th year at Magill (Adelaide). There are a few
other survivors of that House.
A. R. Addison, M.L.C. (S. Aus.), is
a miller, racehorse owner, lay preacher of the
Church of England, and a politician of a kind.
Also, the State’s champion revolver shot.
Cardinal Moran hotly resents candid
advice. Senator R. E. O’Connor tried to prevent
him putting his foot into it over the Federal Con-
vention elections, and has not been forgiven yet.
Those who know say that Cardinal Moran’s
secretary is responsible for leading him into most
of his blunders. Yec Dr. O’Haran has the knowing
nose of the departed “Wally ; ” he hasn’t his
nous.
The late C. G. Duffy wrote in his history
of the Young Ireland party that the best of the
Irish patriots died prematurely from “suppressed
emotion.” Duffy lived to be 86, so evidently he
didn't suppress his emotion.
Recently-deceased pulpiteer Parker, of
London City Temple, claimed to be a good
minister for business fnen. And he was a good
business man for a minister, since he left property
valued at £23,623 —a nice, substantial camel to
lump through Heaven’s needle-eye.
The head of Victoria’s Thomas Bent is
visibly swelling. As he was passing along to his
office, the other day, a solitary voice, somewhere
at the back of an unemployed crowd, said
“Cabbages ! ” Bent refused, shortly afterwards,
to receive a deputation from those who had “so
insulted him.” Now, suppose the voice had
called out “Bank!” or “Building society!” or
“ Land shindykit ! ”
There has been a remarkable series of
happenings among the Lands Ministers of Aus-
tralia during the last few months. Larry O’Lough-
lin (S.A.) was retrenched under the terms of the
Parliamentary Economies Act; M’Kenzie (Vic.)
had to resign ; Crick (N.S.W.) was nearly smashed
up by a boulder while visiting Maoriland; Dr.
Jamison (W. A.) was called away from Australia
to the Transvaal; and now O’Connell (Q) has
died.

“Australia is a land of topsy-turveydom.”— Gag from various

Recently at Surry Hills. Sydney, his
parishioneis presented the Rev. R. O. Todd with
a hand-bag in which to carry his sermons. The
gift bore the initials on the outside in large gilt
letters, “R.O.T” Stated that the parson has
decided to use the bag for carrying the parochial
Council’s minute-book.
Lands Minister O’Connell, dead in Brisbane
at 52, crowded many occupations into his life.
Aide-de-camp to his uncle, Acting-Governor
Maurice O’Connell, at 22, he afterwards became
banker, merchant, alderman, inspector of Poly-
nesians, immigration agent, storekeeper, member
of Parliament, and Minister of the Crown.
The Adelaide friends of Federal Customs
Minister Kingston are grieved because his physical
strength appears to be wearing out. They notice
the change from his olden vigor all the more
because they have seen so little of him lately.
Politically he is stronger in S.A. than ever before,
and no opponent could come near him on a popu-
lar vote.
Chief Justice Darley’s extra six months’
holiday excites comment. When Windeyer asked
for an extension Premier Reid’s reply was, “ Half-
pay or retire.” In Darley’s case there is no plea
of ill-health ; in fact, he bluntly says his health
is good enough to serve the British Government
as member of a South African Commission.
Should N.S.W. pay a man in such circumstances ?
Jas. Howard, who died at Edi (Vic.)
recently in his 102nd year, arrived in Sydney in
’2B, and after serving as shepherd for the Messrs.
Faithful in the north-eastern part of Victoria for
a number of years, tried digging at Bendigo, and
then returned to shepherding. In the old country
he distinguished himself as a pugilist ; he remem-
bered seeing, in his 14th year, the troops leaving
his native county to take part 'n the Waterloo
campaign.
Retired Fijian missionary Frederick
Langham, on whom the University of Glasgow
t’other day conferred the honorary degree of D. D.,
left Castlemaine (Vic.) for the islands in 58, and
remained there for over 30 years. Most of the
natives were cannibals in the early period of
Langham’s ministrations. They did not then
waste time in burning Bibles —their great anxiety
was not to burn the preacher but just to brown
him -nicely, as witness the fate of Rev. Baker (in
’67), whose top boots were the despair of the
Fijian cook, that chef mistaking them for part of
his body.

Swanston, recently deceased in
I iji after a residence of nearly 50 years, was
thoroughly Fijianised. He married a handsome
longa woman, and though his daughters were
educated in Sydney, he never cared to visit

Dear B. : Re McMahon and Chiniquy’s
picture. The McMahon, of Bungendore, is dead
some years, and is not likely to call around with a
contradiction. I was skull-driving at Bungendore
at the time the joke was perpetrated, and stayed
at the Marshal’s pub.—he claimed relationship
with Marshal McMahon. The poor old fellow
died, of a broken heart, across a favorite daughter’s
grave. He was an honester man than (name of
the lass honest person suppressed for fear of libel).
—Yaerum.
A rumor to the effect that Willoughby
Andrew was released, or about to be released,
from prison excited a good deal of speculation last
week among those acquainted with him in the old
time. Andrew, it may be remembered, shot
Bartlett, a draper, 13 years ago, owing to a
dispute, about a dishonored cheque. He was an
eccentric man, and during the first five or six
years in gaol continued to be eccentric, but has
lately changed greatly for the better. He stood
for Parliament for Molong on one occasion, but
Dr. Ross easily topped the poll.

About Premier Seddon’s close-fi9tedness (B. 7/2/’O3)
I have no hesitation in criticising our Boss ; but Tiib
Bulletin would be the first to ask for fair.play, and let
us be fair to Seddon. Unscrupulous and vain as he is in
many ways, Seddon has a heart afte • all, and it is quite
unfair to call him stingy. It is certainly true that he is
frugal, that he doesn’t entertain, and that he insists,
apart from his public duties, on living, while in Wel-
lington, as a private person of strong domestic pro-
pensities ; and all honor to him. In the matter of plain
living and domestic qualities Seddon sets an example to
be admired and followed ; in fact, in that respect he re-
mains a democrat heart and soul.
Elijah Do wie, when his workmen of Zion
City struck work because no pay was forthcoming,
went out before them and flapped bis purple
wings and urged them “to have faith and con-
tinue their labors.” And one brutal man of toil
took his pipe out of his mouth and spat reflec-
tively, and remarked that that “wasn’t good
enough.” Then no thunderbolt fell from Heaven,
and no chariot of fire appeared ; not even a bear
rushed from the forest, nor did a plague of rattle-
snakes smite the man. He left in peace—left
whilst Elijah 11. fluttered his ineffectual, though
beautiful, purple wings feebly like a soaked
rooster.
Parson Joseph Nicholson,
who is making a fight against the
legalising of the totalisator in
Victoria, put, up a similar fight in
South Australia. Joseph has offered
to debate the question with any
member of the v.R.C. In his way
Joseph is a character. He flies
around Melbourne on a bike with
the tails of his clerical coat pinned
up at the back to prevent them
becoming entangled in the works.
When he dismounts, his coat gives
one the idea that its owner has
lately been interviewing a fierce
bull-pup, which attacked him in
the rear. A good story of Joseph
and his old dad goes the rounds in
South Australia. Joseph and dad
happened to be widowers together.
Dad lived a lonely life in Adelaide,
while Joseph did likewise in the
country. But Joseph had a house-
keeper to look after him. She was
a good housekeeper and Joseph
liked her much. He did not like
her as much as he did someone
else, and eventually he took a
second wife. Whereupon he wrote
to his father commending his house-
keeper and casually remarking that
he believed she’d make the old man as good a wife
shed made him a housekeeper. The old man
immediately secured Joseph’s left-over house-
keeper, and from being housekeeper to Joseph’s
dad she soon became Joseph s step-mother,
Joseph proved a good judge of women, for both
marriages panned out happily.
David Murray, the Adelaide warehouse-
man, just returned from London (making his 22nd
trip there), started a retail business with brother
William with next to nothing. Now he owns a
good portion of the main streets of Adelaide and
Broken Hill, and has businesses all over Aus-
tralia.

Morning Herald (Perth, W.A.) summed
up Yes-No Reid neatly: —
Mr. Reid can always amuse an audience, and audiences
ahvays like to be amused but at the same time-with a
Zi o, v"sr£:
or are supposed to be, a serious matter, and many, if not
the majority, are inclined to distrust the political leader
who is not serious. Possibly the feeling of the general
public towards its entertainers is a relic of feudal days,
when a professional jester-a slave-was a necessary part
of a noble s household. . . . Life must have been dull at
times in those picturesque but comfortless castles, and
the fool helped to make the time pass more pleasantlj\
But he was still a fool and slave, and in station he was
thus considered far below those who condescended to be
amused by him.

“ Offside-Mac ” : Maoriland’s Hone
Heke, M.H.R., grand-nephew of the
old warrior who razed the British flag,
staff at the Bay of Islands, speaks
English almost faultlessly when he
does speak it. Sometimes he
doesn’t. One evening, when
an attenuated Opposition
was trying to stonewall
a Government measure
till after midnight, so
that no new business
could come on, Hone
stood up, and, ad-
dressing the Speaker
in Maori, said that
he was a member of
the native race, and
required the services
of an interpreter.
When the House re-
covered itself, Seddon
remarked brusquely
that it was all bosh;
everybody knew that
Heke could Hilk Eng-
lish. He was merely
wasting time. Still
speaking in Maori, the
native appealed to the
Speaker, and Maurice
O’Rorke replied that Heke
was quite within his rights.
There was no native inter-
preter in the House, and
members are not allowed to
act. It was a wet night, but
Seddon sent messengers and
rang telephones, and raged up
and down and to and fro, seeking
for a linguist to attend the Maori.
Heke stood with never a smile lighting
his face whilst Seddon fumed, and the House
fairly rippled. Supper was finished, with things
still as they were, and Heke still stationary on
his substantial feet. Eventually, just on midnight,
the messengers came back and reported that they
had scoured all the bar-parlors of the windy city
for an interpreter, but to no avail. The House
then adjourned.
Premier See will gain little by temporising
longer about a successor to N.S.W. Police
Inspector-General Fosbery. Mr. Fosbery wants
to get out of harness, and his long service entitles
him to release. In fact, he asked to be released
in Reid’s time, but his retirement means a pension
close up to his present salary, and Reid, arguing
that that meant two salaries for the office, pre-
vailed on “ the distinguished man to continue his
distinguished services.” If Fosbery were not
legally entitled to that substantial pension he
would have had a successor long ago. Garvin,
Garland and Larkin are local competitors for the
office, but See totters between two opinions as to
a Board or a solitary head.
For the present there is no more litigation
re the financial fiddlings of old John Wallace, that
sapient and respected pillar of Vic. Leg. Council
who said of himself, in an electioneering pamphlet
—“I have never had a black mark against my
name.” The plaintiff, son of Wallace deceased,
has come to a settlement with his step-mother,
the executrix of her late husband’s will. Practi-
cally the settlement is an admission that John
Wallace embezzled an immense amount of the
Monahan money, which he was supposed to hold
in trust for his children. His widow, it seems,
undertakes to pay £83,000, in instalments, to the
Monahan estate, on account of arrogant old
John’s defalcations, and the court has gravely
approved the settlement. Either Wallace was an
ignorant ass with an enormous belief in his own
shrewdness as a mining investor and general
speculator, or he was a case-hardened rogue.
Anyway, he ranked as an elder in the Yic. House
of Fatmen. He loomed importantly and rep
resentatively among those unyielding Tories
whom the Abgus earnestly exhorts to stand firm
and save the Constitution.
Alf. Vincent, the Bulletin’s Melbourne
cartoonist, who suffers in the springtime from
visions and dreams of the Cup races and doesn’t
always dream the right horse, has discovered the
true strength of his occult faculty. Alfred is a
water finder. The other week when he was
visiting an anxious grazier in the Fern Tree
Gully his host detected the light of
divination in his—A.V.’s—lovely brown eyes,
and, before the artist could lodge a polite protest,
he found himself walking around the property
with a two-pronged rod in his hands. At a
certain spot the “pointer” started to dip with
great determination, although the blushing
medium tried his hardest to hold it straight in
front of him as before. The more he struggled
against the invisible attraction the more powerless
he became. Then he learned that a previous
diviner had lost control of the rod at the same
spot. Some further tests confirmed the first
result. The pointer dipped every time, and the
water was there sure enough—they bored for it,
and soon struck an abundant supply. Since then
Alf. Vincent has watched himself carefully, and
he finds that his hair stands on end at the
approach of a thunderstorm.

AFTER MANY YEARS.
WILLIAM HENRY : “ You see, the Prodigal Son has returned, father.”
THE OLD MAN: “Hi, Jim! kill a dozen sheep and two calves !”
“ 8.5.” : Cookies here (N.-W. Victoria)
mostly have their dams where the drainage from
houses, stables, pig-styes, &c., must flow into
them. Also they carefully fence the dams to en-
sure themselves good water, and erect pumps.
Then they wonder where the devil the yearly-
recurring typhoid comes from. By the way, the
drought, which the Melbourne dailies broke up
with such a flourish some two months ago, is
again in full blast, and if it doesn’t break in
reality soon, what land isn’t blown into the dams
and water channels will have left bodily in dust
clouds for the eastern part of the State.
• • • •
One of the attractions of a recent Maori-
land church bazaar was a weight-guessing compe-
tition, the animal to be guessed being a pig. The
bazaar lasted two or three days, and nobody
remembered to feed the grunter during that
period; therefore, it was a very faint and cruelly
diminished pig that was handed back when the
function was over.
“ Rusty ” : Re par. in B. (28/2/’O3). If
“ J.S.R.” had ever been a poor man on the land
he would not feel so sad about the wholesale
shooting of “ the happy family.” I have known
these birds to eat, in two days, the whole of the
seed wheat that was sown on a 10-acre patch.
Anywhere out Bourke way, when seed wheat is
sown, they simply flock on to it in thousands.
Also, carriers, when feeding on the road, must
stand by the whole of the time to keep the birds
out of the feeders.
• * • •
“F.C.” : As to foxes and rabbits ; in ’93
I wintered on the ranges below Lancefield —for
the discouragement of an attack of phthisis—and
trapped rabbits for a livelihood. Cats—tame
pussies run wild, and fiercer than the domestic
tabby—eat head, neck, and heart of trapped rab-
bits. Sometimes the cats themselves get trapped ;
then you have to kill them to recover your trap ;
you can’t otherwise get near them. Foxes do kill
trapped bunnies; tear them to pieces, and eat the
heart and tenderest parts of the flesh. “ Elmore ”
says reynard is too suspicious to go near a
trapped bunny. How comes it, then, that I have
myself trapped over a dozen foxes, besides seeing
them “ do a get ” when I was lying in wait near
my traps about 5 p.m. ? As for making money out
of bunny. Mate and I made a good living, and a
bit over, during those six months, though neither
of us at first knew how to set a trap, or what to
do with bunny when we got him. We averaged
a pair, clear ; but this was before there was
any export trade in bunny; to-day trappers should
realise sd. or 6d. a pair net. We didn’t work in
thick rabbit country. To-day, if my chest troubled
me again, I would go up on the Dividing Range,
between five and ten miles from the railway, with
a horse and cart, a tent, and 50 traps; and I’d
have no misgivings about clearing between £2 and
£3 a week—and that, with good health and an in-
dependent life, isn’t to be sneezed at. I have
been told of men who made £lO and £l5 a week
out of skins only—not trapping, but netting water-
holes and tanks; but this was always said to be
“ away back ” in some vague part of N.S.W., or
Q., or Gehenna.

Vic. ex-Govt. Astronomer Ellery confi-
dently states that the drought is now coming to
an end, and that the seasons will gradually
mend, for a few years at least.

“ W. W.” : It is not an uncommon thing
round Wee Waa for any person who is fortunate
enough to possess a horse to find in the morning
the animal bathed in lather and sweat from head
to foot and great blood-stains down its sides. It
is another tale of the drought. Some father of a
starving family has used (or stolen for the time
being) that horse, and ridden him to where he
could safely steal a sheep. The blood-stains over
the horse’s flanks show where the blood has gushed
from the gashed throat of the sheep. The owners
of the horses seldom prosecute—tjjey know the
stress of the drought.
From “ Paddy Melon ” :
“Yeppoon’s” yarn (8., 21/2/’O3) about the colt that
bucked itself clean out of its hoofs and left them in the
sticky soil, originated about the time Captain Cook
landed in Botany Bay. It is one of a series told by the
“ hands ” to the green-horn jackeroo, and forms part of
the curriculum of initiation into the mysteries of “ colo-
nial experience.” The busting of the eye of an axe
caused by the swelling of the handle as the result of a
snake-bite, the sheep-dog that can yard a chicken (or a
blow-fly) into a jam-tin, the old-man kangaroos that
kill gohannas with waddies, and the straining of rifles by
potting shy dingoes at too long a range, and such like,
all help to set the tender-foot thinking. It is all “ fair
dinkum ” too. The axe is produced (one that’s been run
over by a waggon), and Bill wears a belt made out of the
snake-skin. The tin is still there, so is the chicken (the
first old rooster that runs past will do). It was during
his chickenhood that he was yarded. Unfortunately,
though, the old dog is dead. Otherwise they’d get him
to do it again. Mick’s got the skins of the kangaroos
that fought with the waddies under the mattress on his
bunk to keep the weevil out, and is preserving them till
winter when the prices will go up, and he oils his strained
rifle with the fat taken out of the murdered gohanna.
What more d’ye want ?

“ J.” : Apropos of the Lownes (W.A.) inci-
dent. Once, in Bananaland, I was startled by a loud
explosion at the back of the house, followed by the
most truly awful yells I ever heard. Rushed out,
and found they proceeded from a youthful
Kanaka domestic, who had a lump like a volcano
on her forehead, and much blood flowing from
her glossy countenance. After administering
suitable relief and calming the yells, which took
much time, an investigation was held, and it was
found that the wounded one had industriously
lighted the washhouse fire with some paper and
two detonator caps. She was vigorously blowing
the flames when things blew up, and the firewood
and part of the roof went heavenward, and the
washhouse copper landed on her countenance.
The injuries soon healed, though the lady’s face,
if carefully assayed, should have floated off well
as a copper-show. The firewood, to the best of
my belief, has not come down yet.
• • • •
The latest great Victorian dust-storm has
choked up most of the irrigation channels in the
Mallee country that the previous dust-storm
missed, and the local supply of objurgation having
run out, it will soon be necessary to import cuss
language from Melbourne to express the situation.
• • • *
“F.” : The writer (8., 7/3/’O3) who wasn’t
quite sure that there was a blacksmith’s shop at
Pooncarie may be excused for his doubt on the
subject. Sometimes there is a shop, and at other
times, after a prolonged blastiferous duststorm,
there isn’t. At least, it has to be dug out of
several feet of red sand. Pooncarie claims to be
the only “town” that has had its cemetery blown
away. The cemetery is on a bit of a sand-bank,
and, a few years ago, a big storm blew some Bft.
of the surface of the cemetery clean away—over to
Hay, probably. All the coffins and bones were
exposed, and some of them probably blew to Hay
also. But, by sticking in scrub and other dust-
gathering obstacles, a new cemetery was collected.
Near Pooncarie there is a short line of fencing
over a sandhill which consists of three distinct
fences, one above the other. The bottom two are
buried in sand, and when I saw it last the third
was half-buried. People who wish to “go on the
land ” around Pooncarie have to be particularly
spry, as the “land ” has a fashion of shifting in a
hurry, and it is no uncommon thing for a selector
to get up in the morning and find half of his
selection blown into the next district.
• • • •
The drought has reached even well-watered
Gippsland, where little rain has fallen for over
two months. The Mitchell and Tambo Rivers
are now merely backwaters of the lakes, and are
salt for a higher distance than has been known for
years.

• • • •
Years back, up on the Queensland border,
an old shepherd was found dead in a gully. Two
neighboring squatter Jaypees, for whom he had
recently worked, held a magisterial inquiry and
found a verdict of death by Providential visita-
tion. Two cheques for £52 in all were found on
the corpse, and each cheque was signed by one of
the gentlemen who held the inquest. After con-
sultation they decided that it would be wrong to
interfere with the property of the dead, so the
corpse and cheques were buried in the one grave.
It was a reverent act, and those squatters are £52
ahead to this day.
“ Galvo ”on smells :
The B.’s sub-leader (7/2/’O3), which calls Hobart a dirty
place because its Rivulet can be smelt from Bass’s Straits,
or thereabouts, brings odorous and odious memories of
other large fragrances which I have inhaled in different
parts of Australia. Nature provided me with bad hear*
ing and wretched eyesight, but compensated—doubtfully
—by providing an extra keen sense of smell. Some Bul-
letin correspondents, a few months ago, claimed that
different colors appealed to them as distinct musical
sounds. Smells appeal to me just the same way. A vile
odor is as much a shock as a false chord to a musician,
and Australia contains many such shocks.
Sydney possesses a good system of drainage, on the
whole, but until the low level system is put into opera-
tion (and, for certain engineering reasons, it will not be
for many months, despite an untrue departmental asser-
tion to the oontrary), Blackwattle Bay will continue to be
something more tangible than a memory. At low tide,
under a hot sun, with a puffing little ferry-boat to stir
things up, it is something to treasure in the memory’. I
had to carry out some work in the neighborhood last
summer, and the memory of bullock refuse from the
abattoirs, old fish, old hat, old rags, old garbage, old
hogs, dogs and devils lingers in my nostrils still.
Melbourne also can hold its end up. Passed a whole
night two years ago in a steamer cabin on the Yarra, and
hope never to repeat the performance. The Yarra strikes
a lower note than Blackwattle Bay. It is less acrid but
more subtle, and appears to flow in and out and change
its quality of smell continually’. It cannot be shut out,
as the Blackwattle Bay'stink, to a certain extent, can, by’
closing doors and windows—it :s more insistent and
demands entrance like a bailiff. It may be doing
Adelaide an injustice when I say it smells sweetest of all
—an octave higher than Melbourne or Sydney. At any
rate, I can recall nothing especially bad in the way of
odors.
It is reserved for Perth to provide the concentrated
perfume of all. Perth posseses an institution known as
Olaisebrook Drain, into which flows most of the drainage
from the eastern portion of the city. It is an open ditch,
some 20ft. wide, and as deep. I had to pass along about
300yds. of this daily during one very hot summer, and
bought a bicycle for the express purpose of sprinting by
without taking breath. I found it impossible to walk
that distance with only one breath, but I could ride it.
The smell of Olaisebrook Drain is like unto no other
smell on earth. It shrieks aloud like Tommy Bent when
a train gets in late, or like the ruffian who sells Wild
Rabbit.
Perth possesses another odorous institution worthy
of notice, called a “ dry well.” Underneath each
house a large pit is dug, and the house drains simply flow
into it. As the soil is pure sand, the liquids sink and
leave a lovely scum on the surface, which becomes dry,
hard, and sometimes prevents further moisture from
escaping. Then the “dry well” overflows into the
house, and choice is the fragrance thereof. I boarded in
a house the well of which misconducted itself thus, but I
didn’t wait for its subsidence—l fled, with my nose in
my hand, for another hash house. If it were not for the
deodorising qualities of the sand, Perth would have been
wiped out of existence by typhoid long ago—even now it
is bad enough.

Victoria. By the way, what is the record flock
of crows ?
A REAL QUEENSLAND EPISODE.
A one-eyed traveller, camped on the creek, was
fly-bitten in the good optic and blinded. Luckily
he was not alone. Herewith procession into town.
A Western Queensland station manager
was asked by the owners to report as to how the
station had come out of the drought ordeal.
Accordingly he made up a parcel of sundries —a
piece of hide containing the station brand, three
hoofs, a green-hide girth, a broken bridle, a pair
of horns, the skin of the cattle dog, and a rotten
This he forwarded with the adden-
dum : Gentlemen, these are the assets.”
SLEEPER-HEWING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When you’re grafting sleeper-getting,
Grimly bullocking away.
From the rising to the setting
Of the sun, each blessed day,
And the timber’s tough and sappy,
And the broad axe isn’t light,
And the flies don’t make you happy,
Or particularly bright;
It’s enough to make you curse things
In the wildest style you’ve got,
For few, indeed, are worse things
• Than a sleeper-getter’s lot.
Then you saw it into sections,
And you quarter up the tree,
And you groove it where deflections
Twist the grain contrari'y,
And you chop it where it’s “ stringing,"
And you keep the maul a-swing
Till the ranges are a-ringing
With the echoes answering,
And you never lose a minute,
Though the weather’s blazing hot,
And hardly tucker in it
Has the sleeper-getter’s lot.
The soldier ant goes proudly
On the battle path alone ;
The jackass rollicks loudly
In a way that’s all his own.
And you hear the rush and stumble
Of the lusty kangaroo
When he’s coming all a-tumble
In the evening time to woo.
But you 1 what is your life, man,
With your heart and mind a-rot,
For neither love nor wife, man,
Has the sleeper-getter’s lot.
But it’s stiffen every muscle,
And put weight in every blow,
For while you’ve strength to hustle
You must grit your teeth and go ;
And when the shadows dully
Close around you, crawl to camp,
In the weird and lonesome gully,
And you curse the old slush lamp.
And you sleep with little dreaming,
For the land of dreams can not
Send e’en one bright ray beaming
On the sleeper-getter’s lot.

J. : Was employed in Mount Brown
Forest Plantation (S.A.) at latter part of the
eighties, and caught young opossum in bottom of
red gum we had felled for sleepers. Took ’possum
to the tent where I had a pet cat that had
lately given birth to kittens, all of which I had
drowned. Placed opossum alongside of cat, and
after few days was surprised to see it cling to back
of the cat and ride about the place. Before I had it
a week cat would allow ’possum to suck it, and in
this way it was reared.

“ G.” : Re winnowers and “complete
harvesters (“ Chass ” and “ Eiddal,” B. 10/1/’O3).
A good sample in either winnower, harvester, or
thresher is usually, obtained at the expense of
quantity, because wind-power sufficient to remove
a« rubbish will also blow out some of the wheat.
Dut although the gram gained by use of the har-
vester as compared with the winnowers may not
be much, ‘‘think of the souls, mon ; think of the
souls! . For the winnower is probably the most
cursed invention that ever struck wheat-land
Jf ew farm-laborers will regret its exit, for ’twas
heart-breaking work. A great point about the
harvester is that, thanks to the enterprise and
inventiveness of a Ballarat manufacturer, it is
made locally, and thus gives much-needed labor to
Australian workmen.
“J. F. D.” : The goat
shares with the boy the dis-
tinction of being about the
most contrary animal in the
empire. When a boy, Dad
bought two billy-goats (why two
billy-goats I don’t know). After
a while, to prevent them slipping
into neighbors’ gardens during
their daily rambles, he fixed a
piece of hardwood three feet
long to the two leather collars,
thus yoking the goats together.
The first day under the new
treatment they did not return
till 7 p.m., their ordinary time
was 4. Next night it was
later, and the following night,
no goats turning up at 9 p.m.,
five of us boys (we are a large
family) were turned out from
the fireside corner, where we
were listening to ghost stories,
to hunt them up. We found
them about half a mile away,
one on each side of a post that
happened in their way, and
pushing with all their strength.
They must have been at it for
hours as each had a small trench
dug with his hoofs. Dan, who
was a particular friend of the
white billy-goat, wanted the
other one to give way so that
the white billy-goat would not
feel hurt, but young Bill thou ght
the other goat had as much
right to a fair go as the white
one. Bill and Dan quickly got
into “holts,” and Joe and I
taking different sides also went
at it as hard as we could. Paddy was umpiring, but
Peter, being sent to discover the cause of the delay,
quickly gave Paddy a better task than umpiring.
Dad sneaked on us about 15 minutes later and we
went home much quicker than the goats; they
had broken the piece of wood that joined them
together. _ If we had exhibited the spirit of
compromise and dug up the post so that the two-
goats might settle the matter without loss of
dignity, there might have been fewer black
eyes and strap bruises in the Doolan family.
« • • •
“J.R.E.” : Tom Coward, of Adelaide,
who in the early days was a mounted trooper and.
an explorer (he accompanied one or two expedi-
tions into unknown country), says that some
tribes he came across were Jews and that he
witnessed the rite of circumcision more than
once. He stated they were a different class of
blacks altogether from the ordinary, and they
used Masonic signs. But he didn’t, like Sydney
Klein, throw in any extra frills about a Hebrew
praver-book, and his geography wasn’t so erratic
as Klein’s.
TOO LATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The stock lie thick round the boggy pool,
The crows their gluttony sate ;
The bushman swears—like a Pagan fool—
And the parsons bid us wait;
Wait—till the starving stock are dead,
Till the children cry for their daily bread,
Till the Lord shall take it into His head
To send us the rain—too late.
'Tis easy to speak of a perfect trust,
’Tis easy to preach and prate :
“ The rain will come ” (well, of course, it must)
If we trust in the Lord—and wait.”
For the Lord does things in his gradual way,
But He’ll hear our prayers—so the parsons say—
And He’ll send the rain—or, at least, He may—
What odds if it is too late 1
Then pray ! ye men of the Western plains ;
Thank God for your kindly fate ;
Till the blood dries up in your fever’d veins
Put your trust in the Lord—and wait,
With a tightened belt, for your daily fare,
While the bones of the perished stock lie bare,
Till the Lord looks down with a languid air
And sends us the rain - too late.

But when the S.M. Herald speaks of us as about the
most heavily-taxed people on earth, it is talking ROT.
National taxes are heavier in N.S.W. than in England
But, then, in England police, education and charity ex-
penditure are paid out of local rates, and local rates
average about 6s. in the £ of rental (they are over 9s,
in the £ in some places) against about Is. 6d.
in Sydney and suburbs. Taking all taxes, N.S.W.
gets off very lightly compared with r England

TO RITA.
It Rita was a flower,
I’d like to be a flea.
But like a cur I’d cower
To nip at such as she.
The sweetness of this Rita—
Not honey—far, far sweeter.
Or in her pretty bower
Mosquito would I he.
Above I’d hum and tower
My Rita for to see.
As she lay there a-sleeping
Round her face I’d go peeping.
Ah ! to puncture her sweet cheek [
And sip the hone.v dew !
Oh ! so cruel a thing to seek
I could not—could not do 1
So outside her snow-white veil
I would try—and try—and fail.
Ah ! But should I just once slip !
A sad thing, O, alack—
And her rosy cheek did nip,
Then to die of a whack.
Oh 1 could my death he sweeter
From your dear hand, my Rita ?

“ Peril ” tells a railway-story :
I was ganger on a section of a W.A. railway, and had
my gang shifting a bit of line somewhat groggy for want
of ballast. Took the job on at a slack time of day, when
no train was expected for two hours at least. And graft-
ing with a will we soon had the cronk permanent-way
snug and rails relaid ready for girding and spiking. A
stiff breeze blew across the track, and we had to curse
fairly loud to make one another hear. Suddenly a sound
smote my ear that made me jump, and, looking up,
there was a special plunging at us down the rise. No
time for anything but to leap back and await the destruc-
tion ; but, to our amazement and relief, she flew over
those loose rails like a bird. I grinned a ghastly grin to
the guard’s inquiring look at my attitude, but he was
none the wiser. I may add that a ganger when .on work
of this kind is supposed to have a danger flag hoisted a
quarter-of-a-mile on either side of operations. I hadn’t.
The prodigal country’s progress . Loan,
Lien, Lean. It is now Lean time coming.
Former energetic supporters of night
lectures at the Melb. University seen to be letting
their side of the case go by default, as far as evi-
dence before the University Commission now
sitting is concerned. Professors (who naturally
want their evenings for Toorak drawing-rooms and
for writing leaders for the dailies) object to even-
ing lectures as “impracticable,” and testify to the
Commission to that effect; but those who remem-
ber that the-University charter expressly says
that the purpose of that institution is to spread
“sound learning among all classes of the com-
munity,” should at least inform the Commision
that all classes of the community cannot attend
lectures given during their ordinary working
hours.

TO WOMEN
WHO SUFFER FROfl
KIDNEY & LIVER DISEASE
Amongst life’s cruellest problems are the pain and suffering which many women are called upon to
endure. The radiant days of girlhood have scarcely passed ere the immature woman is forced to
assume the burden of her sex, and, in many instances, she never again knows the enjoyment of perfect
health. The health of woman, too, is of supreme importance. Upon it depends the vigor of
posterity. The happy home life of husband and children is based upon the cheerfulness and content-
ment of the wife and mother. To the credit of woman, be it said, she carries her troubles, and
continues to present a smiling face, under circumstances which would strain to breaking point the
nervous system of-the strongest man. But even woman, with all her wonderful power of endurance,
cannot stand the pressure of suffering indefinitely without aid. Thousands of women have cause to
bless the day when first they tried the healing, health-restoring effect of Warner’s Safe Cure. The
following letters from women are quoted in order that other women, who similarly suffer, may learn
how to regain health and strength, even though they may have resigned themselves, in desperation,
to the prospect of never being thoroughly well again.
Mrs. R, L, Head, Vine Cottage, Second Street, Port Pirie, S.A., says:—
“ It is with the greatest pleasure that I write to tell you of the great and lasting benefit I derived
from the use of Warner’s Safe Cure, for I feel that, but for that medicine, I should now be in my
grave. I had been suffering for a very long time from a complaint common to women of middle age.
I was losing flesh at an alarming rate. The doctors could do nothing for me, and I commenced to use
Warner’s Safe Cure with a very forlorn hope. Greatly to the surprise of myself andfriends, I began
to improve in health from the first bottle. I continued to take the medicine, and gained health and
strength every day. In a few weeks I was up and about my household work, and am now quite stout
and in good health.”
Mrs, E. Bade, Camrock, Elwood Street, North Brighton, Vic., says: —
“ I have proved Warner’s Safe Cure to be a most wonderful medicine when suffering from that
languid and tired feeling most women are subject to, and which makes one unfit to attend to one’j
household duties. In my case Warner’s Safe Cure had an immediate and lasting beneficial effect.”
Mrs. M. A. Cherry, Lake Street, Perth, W.A., says:—
“ For some time I had been gradually declining in health, suffering from nervous exhaustion.
My household work became a burden to me. I had to sit down and rest after the slightest exertion.
This state continued until I became quite prostrated. I was advised to take Warner’s Safe Cure by
someone who had derived great benefit from it. When I had taken three bottles of the medicine I
became quite strong again, my energy and vigour returned, and my work became a pleasure to me
once more.”
MrS« Emma Darvell) Ladies’ Nurse, 8 Carlton Street, Prahran, Vic.,
says :
“I am pleased to report the benefit I derived from taking Warner’s Safe Cure and Warner’s
Safe Pills. After many years of pain and suffering from kidney and liver complaint, I, who was
accustomed to minister to others, was brought to a bed of sickness myself, and the doctors could give
me no hope of recovery or relief, they giving me but a short time to live. At last I tried Warner’s
Safe Cure. From the first bottle I began to improve in health. Gradually the pains left me. I con-
tinued to take the medicine for some time, until I was perfectly cured and sound in body and in
mind. I have continued to keep well ever since, though some years have elapsed since that time. I
may state that the doctors were more than surprised to see me up again. I have heard of many other
cases during my professional career which have been cured by Warner’s Safe Cure.”
’S SAFE CURE
is a specific cure for all kidney and liver disorders. It possesses the power to compel the kidneys
and liver to extract and expel from the system all urinary and biliary poisons, which are the cause
of rearly all the ailments to which most women are subject, Warner s Safe Cure cures all Rheu-
matic Pains, Persistent Headache, Lame Back, Sciatica, Neuralgia, Anaemia, Debility,
Indigestion, Torpid Liver, Gravel, Stone and Bladder Troubles, simply because it rids
the system of the urinary and biliary poisons which produce these diseases.

remarkable cures, will be sent free, on application, by H. H. Warner & Co., Limited, Australasian

“ Ami.” ■' Cathcart’s bitterest bit ot anecdotage was
about his voyage from Australia to San Francisco in 1865,
with the Keans. Poor Coppin got it hot for leading them
into such a quagmire. They were three months going,
in a very inferior tub, and were nearly starved towards
the end, having to live on biscuits and potatoes 1
“Melb.” : Ellen Mortyn, not Mortyne (recently alluded
to) was wife of Chas. G. Mortyn, a Dublin gentleman, and
came to Australia in consequence of domestic troubles.
An old bill informs me that her first Melb. appearance,
just upon *o years ago, was at the Theatre Royal, in Tom
Tavlor’s “Unequal Match.” Cast: Hester Cranbrook,
Miss Mortyn; Bessie Hebblethwaite, Miss Mortimer;
Mrs. Montressor, Mrs. A Phillips ; Sir Harry Arncliffe, R.
Younge ; Capt. Chillingham, Gladstone ; Cranbrook,
Tannett; Dr. Boteherby, Lambert; Blenkinsop, F
Younge ; Tofts, Harlowe (another of the Younge brothers).
Afterwards Miss Mortyn appeared in the “Two Gentle-
men of Verona.” Cast included : Sylvia, Miss Mortyn ;
Julia, Mrs. Heir ; Valentine, G. V. Brooke ; Proteus,
Heir '; Speed, Lambert ; Sir Eglamour, Ireland—his first
appearance as a pro. Launce was acted by Wigan, not
connected with the well-known Alfred or Horace Wigan.
It is curious that our familiar Nelly Mortyne is the wife
of Charles Mortyne, an Australian actor.
Amy Castles scored a record in her singing of “ Home,
Sweet Home” at the Melbourne Town Hall the other
Saturday. Taking the tedious old Yankee ballad as
slowly as possible, her thoughts must have wandered from
the words, and she left out a part of the second verse
unconsciously. The fact is neither the words nor music of
“ Home, Sweet Home ” appeal to the “ heart ” —the
artistic sense—of any professional vocalist. It belongs to
the class of maudlin ditties that are nowadays sung with
illustrations on a sheet, at Rickards’s. „
The Perry girls, Florence and Beatrice, who came to
Australia for W. &. M.’s first production of “ The Geisha,”
are sisters-in-law to Ben Davies, the popular English
tenor. Which helps to explain why the neat little Miss
Florence (with Beatty attached) flourishes as a leading
lady in successful musical shows. ,
A Maoriland theatrical proprietary recently announced
with a great blast of trumpets that it had fixed a sliding-
roof to its splendid playhouse, and hundreds of people
went along to see how it worked. In the middle of the
performance a deluge of rain suddenly struck the build-
ing, and the sliding roof, which was open, refused to
slide, and the people who remained in the stalls watched
the rest of the show with their umbrellas up.
At the interminable A.N.A. competitions in Melbourne
the ot.ier week, a vocal candidate showed sagacity
enough in elect ng to sing a song composed by the judge
But perhaps he defeated his own ends by trying to itn
prove upon the text of the song. Herr de Chaneet, the
judge, announced with cruel irony, that the singer had
lost first prize through “getting some of the words
wrong.”
“ A.D.’”'Par. in B. (14/2/’O3) re Kyrle Bellew’s multi-
tude of clothes seems a bit apocryphal. According to his
own account he landed in Melbourne with a small quan-
tity of cash and a large quantity of letters of intro-
duction. He had a good time while the former lasted,
hut one Christmas Eve he found himself dossing out with
no possessions save what he lay down in. After this he
gravitated to the Solferino diggings, and here luck was
so bad that it is reported he acted as grave-digger to the
community and once buried a Chinaman for a small con-
sideration. After his Herald experiences he got a job
as lecturer to a d.orama in the Burke-street wax-
works. Eventually a friendly skipper allowed him to
work his way back to England as third mate. There he
unde his first appearance with the Bancrofts, owing to
their friendship with his father, Bellew, the reciter.
As regards his clothes, he has had the reputation for
years”of being the worst dressed man on the English
stage, or, more correctly, off it; for on the boards his
logs were always faultless. The same remark might
almost apply to' his partner, la Gown-Trotter. Writer
knows as a fact, that on their last tour, some of Kyrle’s
street clothes were ready made slops, purchased at a big
Sheeny clothing emporium in New York. The greatest
clothes-possessor to date on the Australian stage was
Charles Thursby, leading man in Cartwright’s last tour.
Is he the hero?” once asked an interested inquirer, as a
perspiring dresser lumped the last Saratoga out of the
hotel vestibule. “Yes,’es the ’ero,” said the p.d., “the
blooming ’ero of a’ undred suits.”
Frances Tyrrell Gill supplied the words for a new part
song—“ The Message Bringers”—which should interest
niusica’ societies hereabouts. Novello is publishing the
song, thanks to its fashionable composer, Waldo Warner,
who has a gold medal and a comic opera to his credit, and
is said (by F.T.G.) to have set the words beautifully.
Dear Bulletin. —The remarks of " Fingleur ” an
“ J.A.C.” re “a season of legitimate comic-opera”erad
interesting. Most of the operas named by these writers
really belong to what in the “ purfesh ” is known as
1 yrand opera.” For instance, Mozart's “Le Nozzi de
Figaro” is an opera only to be found in the repertoire of
grand opera companies. In London one never hears
of such a work being produced at the Savoy,
though it frequently figures in the bill at Covent
Garden. However, if one must have legitimate
comic opera, to Fingleur’s repertoire let me add Auber’s
“ Crown Diamonds,” a most sparkling work ; Rossini’s
“ La Cenerentola,” Offenbach’s “ Orphee Aux Enters,”
Flotow’s “Martha,” and Nicolai’s “Merry Wives of
Windsor.” Most of these works possess good choral
numbers, the absence of which “ J.A.C.” states
would militate against the financial success of the
list suggested by “ Fingleur.” But this theory of
“J.A.C.” would not hold good in regard to Donizetti’s
“ Daughter of the Regiment ” and Auber’s “ Fra Diavolo,”
two of the operas on Fingleur’s list, the former in par-
ticular having some telling military choruses. The list
should .certainly include Boieldieu’s comic opera, “ La
Dame Blanche,” founded on the story of the White Lady
of Avenel. This is a charming work and was for some
years in the repertoire of the Carl Rosa Co., which invaria-
bly played it in Edinburgh, Glasgow and other Scottish
cities. (The story is Scotch, ye ken.) When produced
“ some 22 years ago by the late Martin Simonsen, during
a tour of Maoriland,” to quote “J.A.C.,” itwasagreat
hit in Dunedin, where the whisky-drinkers rolled up in
swarms every night. If one stretched a point and in-
cluded romantic opera, what price Benedict’s “Lily of
Killarney ” or Auber’s “ Masaniello ” ?— Louis Paul.
“ N.” : Re Melba, and the trumpery watch presented
by Queen Victoria. It is a wonder M. did not get one of
the cashmere shawls. Bales of them were presented to
Victoria when she was young, and she could hardly get
away with them, throughout her life.
The musical critics of Westralia mean well, but they
don’t know. While Melba was scattering her vocal pearls
before the people of Perth, a pen-jostler on one of the
local papers wrote :—“ Madame Melba wore a faultless
dress of lace, with roses on the hem of the skirt, and a
lovely pearl and emerald cross at her neck. Tosti’s
‘ Good-bye ’ made some people sob aloud.” Rushing in
where even an angel might fear to tread, unless she was
a choir angel, the critic of a weekly paper promptly
struck him with this large, healthy club of censure ;
“ The notice of Madame Melba’s concert just quoted was
evidently written by a woman. Characteristic of the
sex. Melba is praised for her dressing and her jewellery,
and Tosti (probably some underpaid Italian vocalist),
who, it is admitted, sang so as to make people cry, is
dismissed in one line.” And then critic No. 3 fell on No.
2, and urged him to “ relinquish the pen, and make a
vigorous clutch at his natural implements, the pick and
shovel, or settle down into his proper place—between the
shafts of a wheel-barrow.’*
“ A.D.” : Sydney D.T. recently had a column interview
witn J. C. Williamson headed “ The Australian Twang,”
and mentions the difficulty of finding a local successor to
Carrie Moore. The interviewer remarks What is the
reason of this small output of operatic talent, and the
defic.ency also applies to the other branch of the pro-
fession—the drama—where the demand is so great? Mr.
Williamson supplies one reason, which, however, has
been heard before—‘ An objectionable and horrible
colonial accent ’ is his opinion why so many young
women do not succeed on the stage. . . . Mr.
Williamson will probably fill Miss Moore’s place
with a player from England or America.” In
this connection, “America” is good. The Australian
accent isn’t musical, but, “ sakes alive!” is it as
bad as that indulged in by J.C.W.’s compatriots? Leav-
ing histrionic ability out of the question, coHld any band
of Australian mummers have made such a ludicrous bur-
lesque of old English comedy as that presented by'Nat.
Goodwin’s company in their Sydney production of “ The
Rivals ”? “ 11. G.” recently had a logical defence of the
Australian twang in The Bulletin’s “ Red Page,”
mt he omitted to point out that Australian orthoepy
i- just as correct as, say, the fashionable Oxford
° r Gamhridge drawl. Is the local girl’s accent any'
more trying than, say, Nellie Farren’s or Janet Wal-
dorf’s, to say nothing of scores of popular imported
cockney music-hall artists? As to the demand for
the local article being great—well, it evidently only
exists in the writer’s imagination, nor is it likely to be
otherwise when Williamson, Musgrove and others import
several complete English and American companies every
year. If it is so great, why is it that so many actors and
actresses, good, bad, or indifferent, leave Australia as
soon as. they can manage to raise the fare and a few
pounds besides. In the old stock days, when one would
imagine there would be a dearth of local talent, Aus-
tralian actors were good enough to support such stars as
Brooke, Montgomery, and Matthews. It was even so
many years later when Williamson and Maggie Moore
first struck oil here. The manager replies—“But the
public want complete imported companies.” Yet
Bland Holt and Anderson, who probably do better con-
sistent business than any other managers from one year’s
end to the other, employ the native-born mummers
almost exclusively.
The manager of the defunct Melbourne twice-a-night
variety show, which died in great agony at the
end of its second week, started his hopeless enter-
prise by advertising for a young gentleman trea-
surer who could find “£100 cash security.” The
£100 was said to be an “indispensable” minimum, but
the desired youthful capitalist didn’t present himself.
Ultimately two or three young men came along and de-
posited small sums in consideration of the glory of being
launched in theatrical life. Suggests that Melbourne is
about ripe for a Shakesperian “ gaff,” such as flourished
in Houndsditch, London, when Dickens wrote his
“ Sketches by Boz ” where the amateur actors bought
their positions in the cast on a sliding scale of charges.
The price of the “ star ” part in “ Richard the Third ” was
£2, but any aspiring young bloke could get a chance as
Lord Mayor for two-and-six. The “private” theatres
that Dickens spoke of produced their tragedies off the
stage as well as on. “ That stupid-looking milksop, with
light hair and bow legs, is fresh caught: he plays Malcolm
to-night just to accustom himself to an audience. He
will play Othello in a month, and in a month more will
probably be apprehended On a charge of embezzlement.”

“A.D.” : Latest advices state that “ Tapu, a musical
comedy by J. C. Williamson, Arthur Adams and Bert
Royle, music by Alfred Hill, has been successfully pro-
duced by the Pollard opera co. at Wellington (M.L.), the
Poi and Haka dance shaking things up considerably.
J.C.W. originally intended to produce the opera in
Sydney, John Brunton having painted the scenery some
time ago. A Maori expert was imported to explain the
mysteries of the native dances, but eventually it was de-
cided to try “Tapu ” on the native dog.
Seven thousand Wesleyans—monkey-shaven men, with
their wives and daughters in big beetle-crusher boots—
lately assembled at Westminster Aquarium to gloat over
the fact that they had converted the former Temple of
Tottieinto their “ headquarters.” The Aquarium in its
young days was so frankly and generously wicked that it
never quite lost its attraction for ladies who stay out late
“ on their own.” For the sake of its giddy past ihe build-
ing ought to have been preserved from the clutches of
Wesleyanism.
The worm turns 1 In answer to the attack of a Chicago
Presbyterian minister on Miss Virgin a Harned for her
impersonation of Pinero’s Iris in that city, her husband,
E. H. Southern, thus hits back in an open letter to the
church :—I have read your article on the play of “Iris,”
and it is incredible that a minister of the Gospel, a pro-
fessed follower of Jesus Christ, should write as you do. My
wife chooses to play this part. She thinks it worthy of her
art, and so do I, and so does Mr. Pinero—quite a clever
man, believe me, and possibly a Christian, too, but not of
the kind you would understand. “The stage should be
as free from vice as the church,’’ you cry from the top of
your steeple. It is. A thousand times it is ! You don’t
know what you are talking about. The refrain of all
plays is : “The wages of sin is death,” “ In virtue alone
is happiness.” Plays which have not this refrain cannot
succeed. And these very plays of “ Mrs. Tanqueray,” and
“ Iris ” burn these lessons into one’s soul. There is much
talk just now of a church and stage alliance. I wonder
what we actors and play-writers have to learn from such
men as you.
“ H.” : Re “ cruel joke ” (B., 17/l/’03), here’s another
which an old barnstormer named Kane unintentionally
helped to work off on himself. Telling the tearful tale,
he said ; “ On striking a hash-house in Brisbane, where
our variety show was to open, the landlady required
references from me, and was so firm about it that after
giving her the name of our manager, I made out a list of
27 different persons scattered over the State and gave it
to her. She took me on trial for a fortnight, but when
that was up she fired me out. It seems she had written
to every blessed name on my list, and about 20 had
answered. All these had taken the thing as a joke, and
given me a character for immorality and dishonesty that
would have sent any 50 men to the gallows anywhere.
Consequently I got home one night in time to find my
trunk in the hall. The landlady, looking very grim and
determined, handed me letter after letter until the last
one was reached, and then pointed to the door. I wanted
to explain, but she rang me down with, ‘ There can be
no explanation, sir, after what your friends have written.
I had thought of sending for the police, but thinking you
might possibly reform if given a chance, I changed my
mind. You could be gaoled for life over the matter of
the girl at Barnaby’s alone.’ You may think that the
phrase, ‘ references exchanged,’ is a hollow mockery,”
continued Mr. Kane, “ but don’t try it on that woman.”
“ Petramos ” : The American comic tramp has sup"
planted the Cockney coster on the Vaudeville market-
Have heard it stated that J. F. Sheridan originated the
idea in Australia years ago, but don’t recollect the
occasion. Latona was by far the cleverest in his line that
we have ever had, though Sweet was, perhaps, funnier
and longer winded. Most people forgot the fact that it
didn’t require any particular cleverness for the burglar to
play on the piano as he did. The “ Tired Tramp ” didn’t
catch on. His voice was failing, and Sweet got here
before him, with the same gags. The latest tramp is
Victor Kelly (Kelly and Ashby) who has a knack of
knocking himself down and falling on his head, etc.,
without hurting himself. Also he commands reverence
for his knowledge of how to go to sleep on a beer barrel.

When a travelling opera co. strikes a cathedral town
the R.C. organist endeavors to secure some member of
the team to “do an offertory for Sunday.” This hap-
pened when Abbey’s troupe struck New York, and the
celebrated Brignoli was secured to do a religious “ turn ”
at St. Agnes’s. Brignoli came to church late (having
come home “early” that morning), and, after divesting
himself of as many wraps as an Egyptian mummy mighl
wear, tumbling over music-stands, trying his voice and
fussing round for quarter of an hour or so, he felt fit. By
this time the sermon had commenced, but Brignoli
leaned over the front of the choir and tried to attract the
preacher’s attention by a series of wild pantomime
antics, suggestive of a Punch in the horrors. But the
preacher kept steadily on with his discourse. Then the
frantic singer bent further over the rails and shrieked-
“ Hey ! holda on down there. Stoppa ze preach ! Stoppa
ze preach! I am ready for ze sing. You must not a
preach ven ze gr-reat Brignoli vanta sing!” So “ ze
preach " stopped and Brignoli sang.

is solicited.

ca4 opponent) attended Kingston’s reception in

must still be in the ascendant in S.A., for Baker
has no use for declining planets.

ment has proceeded with public works there would long

With this constant assurance by the Owe’ person
that it is only Bull’s loan money that is staving
off the crisis, what wonder Bull is alarmed.

except when they used bad language. E very time

Brisbane Courier sums up Premier Philp
as hopelessly incapable ; Dalrymple as tired and
