Melbourne Argus has now definitely
broken with the “Reform” Government it
barracked so persistently for, and is a straight-out
supporter of tiie Council in the dispute over the
Reform Bill. The Age, which lost some prestige
in weakly following the rival Argus over the

the Council too. David Syme’s journal is now
top dog again.

Manufactures:—
If the (Factories) legislation was to act in a proper way

one would stand on the same footing. Mr. Kingston was
preparing to introduce an Arbitration Bill, and there was
no reason why it should not be stretched out to cover the
wages question.

Two Westralian Freetraders Mahon,
M.H.R., and Senator Staniforth-Smith—were to

was no campaign. The two missionaries lost their
way at Seymour, and now one of them is unreliably
said to have accused the other of causing them to
lose the train by spending too j[much time curling
his hair and fixing his corsets.
Premier Seddon preserves the good quality
of down-rightness. Premier Barton having made
a shuffling attempt to tone down Chamber-
lain’s recent “notice-to-quit” speech, Seddon
points out that that plain intimation to the
“ colonies ” that they must eventually part up or
part was but a repetition of what Chamber-
lain and others of the British Cabinet said at the
recent secret Colonial Conference.
If there is one man in Australia that a
huge proportion of the N.S.W. Irish population
would gladly see shifted permanently to the
farthest ends of the earth it is Cardinal Moran,
for his foolish hint that the Irish electors would
vote for the See Government if St. Patrick’s Day
were made a public holiday, and otherwise they
would not. The proposition that the Irish voter
isn’t guided by any intelligent interest in great
political questions, but that his vote can be bought
by a Patrick’s Day holiday, is about the clumsiest
insult that a silly cleric ever put upon his flock.

Thomas Tait, the young Canadian railway
man selected for Chief Victorian Railway Coin-
missioner, probably doesn’t know it is loaded.
Under present conditions, the big Victorian
billet, for all the fat salary, is a really good one
for any ambitious person to keep away from. The
Irvine-Bent Govt, is appointing Tait under the
existing Act, which leaves him entirely at the
mercy of an unscrupulous Minister like Bent and
an unscrupulous Treasurer like Shiels, and also
at the mercy of a Parliament which is
trying to raid the Treasury for new, unprotitaoie
lines, the maintenance of which has caused the
whole huge* railway deficit in Victoria. Appa-
rently Tait has beerTgiven no guarantee that he
will, with his two fellow Commissioners, be re-
lieved of the dreadful burden of Thomas Bent, and
the constant visitation of bullyragging politicians,
and the undue interference of David Syme.

Poll-tax Land (the new name for Q.) is at
boiling-point with the Philp-Leahy-Cribb Cabi-

The N.S.W. Public Service Board sends
out an occasional circular to the younger members
of the service inviting them to pass an examina-
tion, and thereby obtain increased salary. Passing
a certain examination is supposed to lead to a
certain fixed increase—which looks honest and
impartial enough. But The Bulletin knows of
one office—there may be many, but it knows of
one—where a young man with no influence passed
the exam, which qualified him for a certain rise of
salary, and didn’t get any rise. The Board just
left him where he was. And, in the same office, a
much more affluent man got the rise of salary
without passing the exam, at all. Presumably he
had influence.
There was one little episode in the recent
Federal Westralian trip which showed up Yes-
No Reid in not too pretty a light. In the literary
fight between him and Crouch, in the West
Australian, from which the jolly and fat man
came out so much mauled and injured, Reid
complained one day to the interviewer.
I didn’t come with the party. Mr. John Forrest wrote
inviting me, but I didn’t even trouble to answer him.
Crouch replied next day :
Mr. Reid is developing a new phase of humor. He is
going to issue a book on manners. “ The Joker’s Book of
Etiquette," by George H. Reid, P.C., K. 0., D.G., M.P.,
ex-Trea.surer of N.S.W., &c. Ohap. 1: When it is polite
to receive invitations and not answer them; my latest
joke in courtesy, &c., &c.
It is said that the jolly one nearly wept over this
jibe.
Justice Simpson’s questioning of N.S.W.
Solicitor-General’s power to file presentments, and
the consequent possible invalidity of sundry con-
victions, brings to mind one-time Solicitor-General
Isaacs’ claim in Victoria to file a presentment in
one of the Matthew Davies’ cases when his col-
league, Attorney-General O’Loghlen, had refused
to move. There was a great dusting of antique
law books oyer the matter, with the result that
Isaacs’ position was thought to be untenable.
There is little doubt, however, that if the Vic.
Attorney-General had been away from the State,
as in the N.S.W. case, the Solicitor-General’s
power to file presentments would have been quite
valid. The difficulty was got over by the use of
the cumbrous and obsolete grand jury procedure.
Sydney D.T. on the day-labor question :
For a man to suppose that workmen animated by the
ordinary aversion to labor, interested in prolonging the
work they have in hand as long as possible, and subject
to no effective, accelerating supervision, would work just
as fast as those kept up to normal rate of speed by the
stimulating vigilance of a contractor, is only conceivable
on the supposition that he has seen so much of the
wonder-working effects of “ flapdoodle ” that he has come
to enjoy partaking of that illusory form of nourishment
himself.
Which, as a piece of reasoning, is pitifully paltry
and sophistical. Item 1: Workmen are not
ordinarily animated by an aversion to labor—Mr.
Randal Carey may think so from a Bus. Co. ex-
perience, but it is not so. Item 2: It is not a
condition of day labor that the men should be
subject to no effective, accelerating supervision ”
—such supervision should be as easily got by a
day-labor superintendent as by a contractor. It’s
an easy matter to prove anything, if you are
given a free hand to imagine false premises.

SYME'S NEW PATENT SYMPATHETIC
CAPITALIST.
“The capitalist makes the great mistake of thinking that the
chief function of capital is to yield interest.”—Given to the
world by the AGE, Feb. 14.
COHEN (who is being paid over his interest): “ But, my
dear man, can you afford all this? Are you, sure
you are not robbing your family. Here, don’t
think of me. Take it all back."
Blusterer Bent has reduced the minions
of his dept, to a state of nervous prostration.
T’other day the train pulled up a hundred yards
or so outside the Kyabram station, and could not
be induced to budge, though the signal stood at
“right.” On inquiring, the statiomnaster found
that the driver had mistaken the telegraph-mes-
senger (who had appeared with his uniform for
the first time under Federal auspices and was
standing on the platform an interested spectator),
for a danger signal!
It is reported that a petition is being pre-
pared among Victorian railway men, asking the
British Government to veto the Bill which shuts
them out from the general body of electors. If
this is so, it is a lamentable mistake. Should the
request be granted it establishes a precedent
for further British interference with our self-
governing powers. The King never dares to veto
a Bill passed by the British Parliament, and he
only dares to veto Australian Bills on the ground
that the Britisher who doesn’t live in England is
necessarily an inferior creature as compared with
the one who does. Australia should protest
against that insulting veto all the time.
“ Dogspike,” on Vic. railway economies .
Vic. Railway Dept, has stooped to the trick of com-
pelling its employees to rent any departmental houses in,
their districts, whether said employees want them or not,
and nobody questions the justice or legality of the-
action. I know single men who have paid over a year’s
rent for houses into which they have never stepped.
Further, I know married men who, despite the fact oi
owning houses, are compelled to pay rent for depart-
mental cribs. The employees are wondering whether
this is robbery, or only economy,
Treasurer Shiels, the Sick Raven of
Victoria, has declared furiously in the Assembly
that he regards Old Age Pensions as a mere State
charity—not as a duty owing by the State to its
worn-out taxpayers in their later years. He also
said that he knew of 37 cases of undeserving;
people among the pensioners ; that in some dis-
trict which he refused to name some person,
unspecified had informed him that 80 per cent, of
the pensions went in drink ; and that in another
anonymous district he had it on anonymous
authority that not 5 per cent, of the pensioners
were deserving people. Premier Irvine was
absent, but in view of some of his recent demo-
cratic utterances there are hopes that had he been
present he would have got Shiels.down and sat on
his sparse chest.
When Westralia’s new Governor, Admiral
Bedford, arrives, the ring of British naval and
military administrators in Australasia will be-
nearly complete: only S.A. will lack a fighting
Governor. Whilst it must be said of these new
type Governors that they show, on an average, a
conspicuous superiority in mind and manners over
most of their predecessors of the peerage, Aus-
tralia postpones satisfaction until it is definitely
ascertained what the new Imperial policy is
stuffed with. There is more than a suspicion that
the British Government intends to make a very
resolute effort to secure much larger naval and
military help from Australia. What part are the
Admirals and Generals in the State Government,
Houses intended to play ?

Wfi en Toutcher, M.L.A., came forward in
Vic. A assembly with a motion containing a harm-
less ea pression of opinion that there is no further
need for imported State Governors, which is
prettj' obvious to everybody except the people
who M ant those personages to open suburban bun-
strugg ;les, Irvine rose to say that the motion was
not in order, any more than it would be to suggest
that h here was no longer any use for a king. Also,
he stated that poor, collapsed old Dunky Gillies
had pondered over the situation in his sick room
and considered the motion neither decent nor
propejr. The House was too sorry to laugh. As a
matter of fact, Gillies has pretty well given up
considering anything nowadays.
Mr* G. W. LORD, Silver Lake, Maine, writes:
“ I have used Jayne’s Expectorant for twenty years,
and AM SURE THAT IT SAVED MY LIFE in one
or two instances.”*
It is only luck that is deferring an
awful railway smash in Victoria. Travel-
ling Adelaidewards through the Wimmera
district one can see broken sleeper-ends tilting
up as the engine passes over them. Also
many old and utterly useless messmate sleepers
are still in the road. further, thousands of
sleepers, though unbroken, are too defective to
hold the dog-spikes, which in many places may be
drawn with the fingers. The maintenance gangs
are effecting some renewals, but, with a day a
week off, it’s slow work. All of which goes to
show that if the belated repairs aren’t taken in
hand suddenly, the smash must come, and a
terrible sin of omission will lie on the doormat of
the Irvine Government.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Employees in electric power-houses have fre-
quently to work in close proximity to “ live ” wires, and
their lives are thus in constant danger. Gloves and
shoes of indiarubber, or similar safeguards, afford at best
but insufficient protection, are always uncomfortable,
and often so cumbersome that they are laid aside when
possible. Professor Artemiew, Of Kiev, has now in-
vented a complete dress which envelopes the body from
head to foot, hands and all; woven of the finest copper-
wire, it is so light and pliable that it does not seriously
impede the movements of the wearer. The perfect pro-
tection it affords was demonstrated by its inventor to an
assemblage of Berlin electricians. Clothed in the fabric
he moved fearlessly between wires carrying 150,000 volts’
and drew from them towards his body loudly crackling
long sparks, and large luminous arcs without receiving
the slightest harm.

“ Boro ”: Jewellery and trinkets which get into
the hands of the Sydney police and are not claimed are put
up at auction, and the proceeds are devoted to the police
fund. As worked, this scheme is simply a mixture of
lottery and swindle. The buyer is often outrageously
victimised. The articles are put up, like certain customs
overstocks, “ with all faults.” Enough time for examina-
tion is not giiven and many of the articles are undercover
so that there is nothing but the description to go by!
Often worthless Brummagen is sold at good prices
* * • •
S.A. district-council clerks are putting forth
various claims as to having no rates in arrear. Gawler
claimed that it had collected every penny of its rates for
three consecutive years. Laura claimed a record of five
years. Then a remote spot called Rapid Bay broke up
the show by alleging that it had never had any rates in
arrear, an agile clerk having run down every ratepayer
before the close of the financial year ever since Rapid Bay
existed.
• * • *
There was a time when nearly every agricultural
implement to be found in Maoriland was made in the
local factories ; but of late years the Murkan brands have
been increasing so rapidly that they almost equal the
others. The local manufacturers have taken alarm, and
it is intended to apply to Parliament for a greater
measure of Protection. The Murkans are Bellin<>- ab-
solutely below cost in order to capture the market and
ruin the local factories, and if the latter can be done the
Volcanic Isles will then be made to pay through the nose
and make up the money lost in destroying their own
industries. In the meantime, the five firms who carry on
the agricultural-machine-making business, have formed
a combine on rather original lines. After a payment of
6 per cent, on the capital involved and the provision of
reserve and emergency funds, all remaining pfofit is to
be divided equally between employers, employees and
buyers. The combine hopes, by the abolition of
internal competition and the saving of expenses con-
sequent on co-operation, to sell machinery at present
prices and have from 10 to 15 per cent, profit on capital
for the final distribution. Apparently the combine is in-
evitable, and it is better to have to deal with a local com-
bine subject to the local Arbitration Court, composed
of men who live in the country, and spend their profits
there, whatever they may be, than with the outside
combine which is beyond all local regulation.
• • * •
“Eko ”: Isn’t it about time the tail of the
second-hand dealer was trodden on ? I don’t refer to the
old clo’ man nor the marine dealer—they, as a rule, are
pretty fair dealers. The man I mean calls himself a
jeweller or a tobacconist, and styles his biz. “ Provisional
Purchasing.” He gives you £1 for your watch or ring,
you retaining the option of repurchase within a month
for 255. or 305., or 355., according to your- apparent
verdancy. I hold no brief for the pawnbroker, but he
deserves fair play. He pays a big license fee, must keep
the article six months, can charge only Is. in the £, must
keep a strict account of his pledges, and is directly under
police supervision. Now the Provisional Purchaser pays
no license fee, keeps the article a month, charges any-
thing he can get, keeps no record, and does, in short, as
he darn well pleases. The pawnbroker has mighty little
chance now qf getting “.stolen property.” As soon as a
theft is reported, the police are off to every pawnshop in
the district. They look up the pledge-books, and, if any
article answers to the description of the stolen
one, it is produced and inspected. Then follows a
description of the pawner, and his funeral commences.
But the P.P. is different. The police are not likely even
to know that he ever bought anything from anyone. If
he should be questioned, there are no books to put his
show away. Here’s a case that lately occurred in a Vic-
torian town. A decent young fellow, hard-up, “ popped "
his diamond ring for a week. A lady missed hers and
reported loss to the police. They made inquiries, and
got the decent young fellow’s at the pawnshop. The
lady identified it as hers, and he was arrested. A day or
so later a boy told a policeman he had seen another boy
with a diamond ring. This was then traced to a jeweller’s
shop. It was already broken up ready for the smelting
pot. This was the ring, and the decent young fellow
was discharged. Another day and it would have been in
the pot. Of course, it may be said that any legislation
in this direction would be detrimental to liberty: but
be that as it may, I maintain that when you see k
fence you should straightway chop him down.

“L. 8.” : He ring confidence trick (B. 7/2/’O3).
Once struck a slight variant on usual scheme. The
sharp being shown the way to Circular Quay, picked up
a packet with ring wrapped up in a George-street
jeweller s bill-form, receipted for £3 10s. Offered to sell
it to writer for 10s. Went to borrow the half-quid at a
shop where I was known, and left the other party to
wait. Happened to nod to two plain-clothes men on my
way. When I came back the gentleman had left.
• * * *
Related that, some time ago, a successful pros-
pector, while on the way from Albany to Adelaide,
amused himself by throwing sovereigns at a gull which
was hovering astern. Nearly 90 coins had plunked into
the sea when an exasperated skipper rushed him and had
him locked up for safety* Five months later the
Sovereign Fool bolted back to W.A., leaving a destitute
wife behind him ; and when seen quite recently he was
washing up dishes in a coffee-stall.
• * * *
.The Bank of New South Wales has opened a
quaint, little branch office oh Simeon Lord’s corner, Pitt-
st., Bridge-st. and Macquarie.place, Sydney. The original
grant to Lord was 1 acre 7 perches, given over 100 years
ago as a lease and converted into a freehold in the first
instance by Colonel Foveaux during the suspension of
Governor Bligh, and confirmed by Governor Macquarie in
1810. In 1817 the bank was established and opened for
the transaction of business in a house belonging to Mrs.
Reibey in Macquarie-place, so that after a lapse of over 80
years it comes back almost to the old spot.
* • * •
“The Lad” supplies a commercial item :
“ How does this strike you for accuracy in weigh-bridges ?
Was recently staying in a northern S.A. town, which is
one of the dustiest shops I’ve struck yet. Now, on the
most dusty road of this very dusty town is a weigh-bridge
on which the neighboring cockies are wont to weigh their
hay and wood. A rumor got in the wind that the bridge
was ‘skew-whiff,’ and the cockies thought they were
getting done—-cockies are always suspicious. They
moved in consequence to the other weigh-bridge—the
township boasted two. Now the owner of the first men-
tioned bridge didn’t concur at all with the new arrange-
ments, for, being the proprietor of the nearest
pub, he lost considerably more than the price of
weighing. Accordingly he issued a declaration to the
effect that the bridge would be tested publicly, just to
show the cockies how stupid it was to be suspicious.
He got a ton of limestone and deposited it on the bridge
at a certain Saturday noon, all the dwellers capable being
assembled to test the bridge (likewise drinks). It tallied
one ton exactly—the bridge was true and the publican
jubilant. Just then, when all were watching, the lever
went up. What a moment ago weighed one ton, now
weighed more. The owner was sorely perplexed, but on
looking out of the bridge-house window the cause of this
discrepancy was ascertained. A fox-terrier dog was
leaning up against the limestone. The crowd cheered
.. . then beered. Truly this must be a record for
delicacj l of weigh-bridges, and all this occurred in a town
by nature dusty.
• • • •
“Buzz”: Shipments of tinned meat are going
north every week. Some thousand cases went to Towns-
ville a few days ago, and the Queensland ceutral district
has had two very large consignments lately. Frozen rab-
bits also are being sent to all the northern ports by every
boat. There are both sheep and cattle at the back of
beyond, but the vast dry belt that lies between them and
the coast is not crossable, and may not be for years yet.
So far there is no sign of the drought breaking in Central
Australia, and there is plenty of evidence that droughts
have lasted from 15 to 20 years in the past. So why not
this one ?
• • * •
N.S.W. Government Analyst Hamlet and the
Court argued thus the other day :
Mr. Hamlet: “If sugar were added to such a small
quantity of raspberry syrup that the fluid would contain
only an infinitesimal flavoring of raspberry, he would
pass it as genuine raspberry syrup.”
The Bench: “But that would not be of the quality
demanded. If there were 99 parts of sugar and water
and only one part of raspberry, would it still be raspberry
syrup?”
Witness : “ If I could trace the juice of the raspberry I
would pass it as genuine syrup.”
Does N.S.W. keep a public analyst to pass as genuine a
compound which only contains a trace of what it pre-
tends to be?

BUCHANAN WHISKY.
Messrs. Fairfax and Roberts, who have
been favored with every Optical appointment
issued from Government House for nearly a quar-
ter of a century, have again received the honor by
being appointed Opticians to his Excellency the
Governor, Sir Harry Rawson, R.N.*
Great Britain sent out 387,116 emigrants
in 1902— the number of people who thus fled from
the curse of Freetrade being 84,541 more than in
1901. And of these 387,116 emigrants, 299,854
made for Canada and the United States, the two
most heavily protected countries on earth. Per-
haps if Australia increased its duties 300 per cent.,
and brought its Protection to the Canadian and
U.S. level, this country might have an immigra-
tion boom.
That treasure of Treasurers, George Turner,
with an eye for any stray “ thrums ” that may be
raked into his coffers, opines that it’s about time
Australia coined its own silver. When Premier
of Victoria he endeavored to make the Colonial
Office see things in a favorable light; and lately,
on his strong representations. Premier Barton
communicated with the various State Govts, on
the matter, but without result. The Treasurer
thinks that the privilege can be secured if the
Commonwealth Government gives a sufficient
lift to the log of remonstrance, and the sum saved
would be about .£30,000 a year.
A Great Judgement.
Few understand the onerous duties of a Judge
upon the Bench. We expect more of him than
from other men. He must be rigid in his morals,
fearless and infallible, and yet merciful in his
decision ; and one whom we can respect. There
is only one higher judge than he upon the earth,
and that is The Public. A judge may err, but
the public never. Public judgement is as certain
as time and tide. The law of supply and demand
is fixed and unchangeable, and the thing of no
merit dies quickly; the thing of little merit
gasps in its birth, and feebly dies out, after being
tried and found wanting. The tide of public
favor demands that which it wants, and refuses
the thing that has failed to suit; and Mr. Richard
Murray, of Green’s Road, Sydney, speaks with
the rising tide : “I have much pleasure in letting
you know the benefit I have received in taking
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. I suffered for
many years with indigestion and biliousness to
such an extent that I found life unbearable, having
consulted doctors in every State. I was at last
induced to try your remedy, and I can faithfully
and truly say that I have found them a lasting
benefit, and can. conscientiously recommend them
to anyone suffering from bilious attack* I never
travel without them, and would advise everyone
to do the same. You can use this letter in any
way you think fit, as I think it my duty to let
people know the efficacy of Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills.’* Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a
perfect blood purifier, and a positive cure for
biliousness, indigestion, constipation, headaches,
sallow complexion, liver and kidney troubles, piles,
pimples and blotches, and fur female ailments,
i ut up in amber bottles and the full name blown'
thereon.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Although the solitary system acts badly •»
men, it seems to have a good and sobering effect on bad-
tempered horses. Shortly after the mighty Orme, sire of
Flying Fox, went to the stud he developed a dreadful
temper. He was never a quiet horse in training, but it
was thought he would cool down when his galloping
stopped. But he Soon grew absolutely unmanageable,
and as savage as a tiger. He marked and maimed
his attendants ; they dared not walk him out, and it was
a very dangerous job to even throw his feed to him. At.
last his owner, the Duke of Westminster, gave instruc-
tions to have him shot. While preparations for the
execution were being made, however, someone suggested
he should be kept for a time in a paddock by himself
Getting him to the paddock was a terrible task, but once
there he soon showed signs of improvement, and in a
short time became quite tractable—tar him. When
Vampire, the dam of Flying Fox, was sent to Persimmon
she taught and kicked and carried on so savagely that
hurried word was sent to take her away, as it was feared
she’d kill everyone about the place. A week in a paddock
by herself toned her down to a lamblike disposition, and
since then several other “ man-eaters ” have had a similar
cure worked upon them.
* * • •
The Australian Jockey Club people are queer
cattle. They will not allow a bookmaker who
runs a shop to bet on the courses under their
control, but they register the colors and accept
entries from owners who not alone run shops, but
are registered pony bookmakers. Better far to allow
a shopkeeper to run a book at Randwick than a
horse—for obvious reasons. However, strange as it may
appear, the shopkeepers who own horses are looked upon
by the betting public as amongst the very few horse-
owners who run their horses with as much straightness
as can be expected from a racing stable which intends to
pay its way.
• • » •
Wakeful won the Champion so brilliantly that
tar the moment it seems absurd to think she isn’t a
vastly better stayer than anything she met last Thurs-
day. Three furlongs from home Wakeful went to the
front, and there was only one horse in it. But last year
she went to the front in exactly the same way before La
Carabine came along and beat her badly. This year the
flying mare had only to pass Great Scot as soon as the
three-year-old tired. The Victory never got near her
after they had gone a mile. The Cup winner, who was
to have given Wakeful an awful gruelling, couldn’t go
fast enough to give her even a spoonful of gruel. If the
burly son of The Admiral is really a genuine three-miler
he must have been very “off” when the time came tar
proving his stamina. This scribe having been disposed
to overrate The Victory and underrate Wakeful is not
going to argue against facts. The great little mare has
now won about £15,000 in stakes. Carbine won £29,000,
sohie £ll,OOO of which was Melbourne Cup money. Ho
got £lOOO for running second to Bravo, and more than
£lO,OOO for winning in 1800. And he won two Sydney
Cups. Wakeful thus far has taken only one Sydney Cup,
and third place money in another.
Wakeful made a further addition to her list of
odds-on defeats last Saturday when Chantress gave her
a bad beating. The old mare looked anything but a
champion after the race, though there was nothing very
visibly wrong with her before the start. She had been
off her feed all the week, it seems, or rather she had
been taking tonics tar her jaded “ pecker.” What the
touts mistook for naturally high spirits in Wakeful was
really the outcome of whisky and bitters, or stimulants
to the same effect. Twice out of three times at Caulfield
meeting, and once out of twice at Flemington, when the
odds were on Wakeful she failed to do the trick.

Said to be in existence in Australia a “jockeys’’
ring,” embracing many of the leading riders. The ring-
has a special representative to put its money on in the
right quarters. This ring, if current reports be true, has
often a good deal more to say as to the result of a race
than the condition of the horses, the wishes of the
owners, or even the plans of Ikey Mo.
* • » •
Great Scot, as was stated by jockey Barden at
the time, would probably have beaten Abundance for the
Leger had he (Barden) been at liberty to let the big
chestnut make his own pace. Great Scot gallops freely
when given his head, but when he fights with his rider
his action gets out of gear, and he tires He nearly suc-
ceeded in running away with the Derby. The attempt t»
ride a waiting race for the Leger was a failure, so they
allowed Great Scot to go as he pleased in the Australian
Cup, and he was pleased to lead all the way. Abundance:
at his best might have been capable of wearing the-
Lochiel colt down at the finish, in which case the Aus-
tralian Cup would have repeated the V.R.C. Derby-
history. But Abundance was not at his best. Besides,
he was conceding a handicap of lOlbs. to his rival, and
lOlbs. extra is a considerable steadier for a young horse
when the ground is heavy and there are 2J miles to be
galloped. By the way, the owner of Abundance was
very anxious to purchase Great Scot some weeks ago.
Vanity Fair, who was placed in the Caulfield
and Melbourne Cups, seems to have had her temper
soured when she was asked to run off the dead-heat with
Eleanor for the Williamstown Cup last November. The
mare wouldn’t gallop in the run-off, and since then her
habit of boring-out has become a confirmed vice. Cooper,
who rode the mare in the Melbourne Cup, will have
nothing more to do with her.

Annotated startling reversal of “form” was
not more unsatisfactory than the handicapper’s treat-
ment of her. If the mare had been running “ sore,” her
defeats were no reason for taking weight off her back,
seeing that as lately as New Year’s Day she won the

favorite, carried 8.8 to victory in the Bagot. Last Thurs-

had got down to 8.3 in the handicapper’s estimation.
There was another mare in the field —Vanity Fair—who-
also had run very badly twice during the meeting, but’
the handicapper didn’t let her down in the weights.

Unusual, and by some considered impossible,
spectacle of a jockey, permanently with one stable, fight-
ing out a bitter finish in the interests of a casual
employer, and against those.of his “ boss,” was witnessed
at Moorefield R.O. meeting Saturday, when two of
Wootton’s jockeys, up on his Zue and Mrs. Burke’s Athol-
stone, in Kogarah Stakes, battled out from the home
turn to the post locked together—a dead-heat.
• • • •
Rev. Jos. Nicholson, the watchful anti-totalisa-
torian, was to the front again at the Methodist Confer-
ence in Melbourne the other day. Ikey Mo.’s reverend!
friend persuaded his professional brethren to sanction a
petition to the Vic. Leg. Assembly, embodying all the
moss-grown misstatements which pass for gospel in
snufllebustious circles. One clause of the petition
alleges that wherever the tote flourishes “ gambling is
greatly increased ; professional gamblers are augmented
in number ; women and youths are induced to gamble ;
and the increasing experience of these evils in Maoriland
and South Australia is intensifying the public desire for
repealing legislation in those States.” Every one of those
allegations is false, and the falsest of them all is contra-
dicted by Mr. Nicholson’s friend. If professional gam-
bling were “augmented” by the tote, the professional
Ikey would not be its sworn enemy.
• • • •
Vol. 15 of J. J. Miller’s Australian Racing
Chronicle carries the sad turf story from Aug. Ist, 1902,
to Jan. 31st, 1903. It includes the reports of 700 meet-
ings, and the names and pedigrees of 4 000 horses that ran
In the interests of Ikey Mo. during the half-year. And
among the general sporting information afforded by the
Chronicle there is a ready reckoner which tells the
small but hopeful investor how much he can win by
putting a “ bob ” upon a horse at odds of 11 to 10 on, or
100 to 15 against, and so forth. A reckoner which
reduces its reckonings to the requirements of shilling
punters is a pathetic sign of the times.
At Flemington, last Thursday, two horses
belonging to Mr. Jas. Grice were ridden by Richardson,
the jockey carrying overweight in each instance. Firstly,
The Austrian, handicapped at 6.7, carried 6.9. An hour
later “The Endeavor, 6.7, carried 6.12 (A. Richardson).’’
Richardson, seemingly, had grown Slbs. heavier during
the brief interval.

M. top-notch trotter Ribbonwood —matched
against Australian Fritz—is but 4yrs. old, and will come
on a lot, or admitted good judges of such things are very
much out. Details of his recent successful assault (first
time asked) on Fritz’ mile record, which Ribbonwood
brought down from 2.13 to 2.11 2-5, are that he had a
galloper pacing him, and did the first furlong in 16sec., and
the half-mile in 65sec. Just prior to thi3 Ribbonwood
trotted in an easy winner of a 2*mile event, secured in
4.35 4-s—another best tar the continent.

Second nag (Adjuster) in Australian Cup cost

Obit: Mr. Richard Rouse (“ Guntawang,”
N.S.W.), a sterling old sport, tar long honorary starter to
the A.J.C., and 40 years a leading light at Mudgee and
breeder of high-class horses, sheep and cattle. Promi-
nent nags successful in the deceased’s colors years back
were Viva, Nydia, Reprieve and Lord Lyon. First-named
gathered in two Tatt.’s Cups. Mr. Rouse was born on
the Hawkesbury.

It remains to be seen whether F. Burn’s experi-
mental way of riding Freedom over the big sticks will
have any imitators. Burn adopts the Tod Sloan style
even when he is taking the jumps. On Australian Cup
day it was successful. Freedom won. But on Saturday,
when the horse was going splendidly, he came to grief at
a fence, and the Tod Sloan system was blamed for the
mishap.

handicapper, G. Dowse. At time of death « testi-
monial was afoot for him.
• • • •
Race for Australasian professional sculling pre-
miership between Dick Tresidder and Harry Pearce on
Parramatta River, Saturday, was exciting only till the
mile had been negotiated. There Pearce hadn’t a clear
length to the good, though before he had two. A quarter
mile further Tresidder led and stayed till he won, easing
up, in 21min. 25£sec., with Pearce', llsec. behind, com-
pletely pumped. Pearce shot his bolt in the first mile,
and subsequently never threatened the slightest danger.
Betting in the city that morning was 6 to 4 on Pearce,
but weight of Newcastle coin on the boats following the
race made Tresidder favorite, and he started 5 to 4 on.
Time (6m. 2sec.) compares favorably with the best ever
previously accomplished over the same course under like
conditions of wind and tide.
• • • •
Victorian left-hand trundler Saunders well-nigh
gave the records a tilt in S.A.-Vic. fixture. He had a
hand in disposing of nine out of ten wickets in S.A.’s
second innings—clean bowling six, catching two, and the
remaining wicket was caught off his own bowling. The
only other trundler to get a wicket was Collins.

The easy march of Lord Hawke’s team through
Maoriland (but why Lord Hawke’s team? My lord never
left England with his men) is no line as to how the Eng-
lishmen will fare against Trumper, Noble, and Co.
•Cricket in Maoriland never seemed to get going. Its
weakness may be guessed when in the All Maoriland
’return match a back number like Charlie Richardson, he
of the crab-agai nst-a-wall style of batting, who flourished
in the old Belvidere days, went in first for his side and
made 1.
• • * •
The Victorian cricketers in Adelaide beat S. A.,
as usual, comfortably but not crushingly. Indeed, an
amazing stand for the last wicket of the match brought
the majority down to 35. George Giffen and Travers
•(the latter reputed no batsman) actually added 101 after
all seemed over. Giffen’s performance in the match was
7 wickets for 75 and 1 for 110, with scores of 81 and 97 not
out—an amazing record for a man of 44. The young
players on the side mostly failed completely. On the last
evening but one of the game, Clem. Hill, as captain, re-
fused to sacrifice his other men on a sticky pitch and
keep himself back for the morning. This unselfish policy
probably cost S.A. the match.

“Jab.”; Struggle for heavy-weight bruising
supremacy of the continent ’tween Billy M’Ooll
and Dan Creedon at Sydney National S.C. last week was
exciting from go, but was rarely a good fight, beinf
mostly bullocking, head down, on M’Coll’s part, with
t’other man scoring hard punches on all exposed spots.
M’Ooll proved impenetrable stuff, as beyond dazing him
three times and spreadeagling him for 9 seconds once (in
the 4th round) Creedon’s skill and heavily charged
thumps did nothing. In the second round M’Coll was
stupid from a weighty blow, and, ere he recovered
another made him worse ; but all the time (rattled or
otherwise) the champion kept going chockful of grit and
with the one idea that he must win. Creedon brought
superb science to bear and a large amount of force in
the first few rounds, but the human bull in front of
him proved irresistible. Such was the fury of the
thing that the first round lengthened well into the
minute’s spell (until the referee separated the com-
batants), they had not heard the powerful electric
gong. M’Coll floored Creedon for four seconds in
the second round. M’Coll was in a bad wav in
the third round, but a mis - sizing of the situa-
tion on the referee’s part caused a cessation, and
this respite was M’Coll’s salvation. A collision in the
fifth round opened an old wound over Creedon's risrht
eye, which bled freely, as M’Coll’s nose had been doing
for some time. Though M’Coll was bad in the sixth

• * • *
How the double over-arm (or “ trudgeon ”)
swimming stroke has developed. Much less than a
decade back English swimming scribes wrote it marvellous
that an English champion had swum a quarter-mile with
that method ; then Balmain (Sydney) champion Peter
Murphy used it for half-a-mile, and the swimming world
wondered. By-and-bye some Australians “ trudged ” for
a mile, but the latest feat in that line beats them all.
T’other day Hobbs, of Sydney, swam every yard of three
miles double over-arm, and created a new world’s record
(lhr. 28m. 17sec.) for the distance.
• • • •
A good finale for the Major Taylor season
would be a “ pursuit race ” between that dusky Christian
and, say, four of the fleetest Australian pagans, as dis-
closed in the Sydney Thousand handicaps. The four
pagans could be placed equi-distant round the ridiculous
5. course, and when the Major overtook one, that
pagan should drop out, and the “cullured” person
streak on to overtake the others. It would be exciting
enough for anything, and should be a first-class test of
speed, endurance and pluck.
* • • *
A tyre to be of entire satisfaction must fulfil at
least three desirable features. It must withstand the
effects of sharp flints, undergo high air pressure and
support the rider’s weight. A tyre which satisfactorily
meets these requirements is the new vulcanised Dunlop.
Care should be taken that the genuine article is obtained,
the safest way being to accept no tyre unless it bears the
company's trade mark.*
The Victorian tennis players who stayed on in
Adelaide for the championships were too good for the
local men. Blair and Heath, after a great battle, beat
Bowen and Baker, the local cracks, and in the singles
Blair beat Heath (last year’s winner). The weather
turned hot at the end, and Heath had to be dosed with
restoratives before he could go into the court towards the
finish. Miss Payne and Miss Parr, the Adelaide pair,
again proved quite a class above any lady opponents. -
How the casual English amateur of Lord
Hawke’s team takes his cricket. During recent match
with Otago an English amatah sat in front of the players’
pavilion at Carisbrook reading the latest—in literature ;
wHle skipper Warner was smiting his way into his second
century. Even when P.F.W. made an exceptionally big
hit and was in danger of being dismissed by a catch, the
book-engrossed one read on, merely raising his eyes for a
moment to see what all the disturbance was about, and
with a don’t-disturb-me-again look continued his studies.
A wicket fell, a companion at his elbow gave him a nudge
apprising him that he was next but one man in. The
book-worm placed the novel on the seat, sauntered inside
the pavilion, and returned padded in his right cricketing
mind ; took up the book, and resumed his reading.
Another wicket fell, another nudge, and the padded one
reluctantly placed his literature on the seat and stalked
out to the crease. One ball—a stroke for the grand old
game ; another ball, and (remembering the book on the
seat) played all round it—a clatter of falling timber. The
book had won 1

Just dead, George Pinder, Yorkshire wicket-
keeper, contemporary of Ulyett, Emmett, and Ephraim
Lockwod. He played against the Australian teams of 78
and ’BO, and was reckoned to be—on the rougher wickets
of those days—almost unequalled at taking fast bowling,
and especially on the leg side.

Vic. Football Association is putting forth big
efforts to popularise the Australian game. To this
end, crack Vic. clubs (Fitzroy and Collingwood) will
send best teams to play a match in Sydney during
May which will rank as a Vic. premiership contest.
Same two clubs also promise to finance their reps.,
leaving “ gate ” to those behind the club-forming move-
ment in N.S.W
* • • •
The ill-luck of Oxenham’s T.M.S. recalls the
fact that little grey flier Sardonyx started 36 times, won
6, ran 13 seconds; three thirds, and was unplaced 14
times. He ran second for the Hawkesbury Grand three
times, twice second for the Summer Cup, twice for
Tattersall's Cup, and three seconds for Anniversary Cup.
He was only a pony, but all there was of him was real
grit.

Cardinal Moran and the NSW. Govern-
ment are now effectually estranged. Even
the Cardinal’s best friends admit he has made
a huge tactical blunder, not only in bullying
Owe’Sullivan, but in being foolish enough to
publish the correspondence. Said that Owe’S.
was very wroth at the letters being made
public, although some of his colleagues in the
Ministry enjoyed the situation. The Cardinal
was too sure of his ground beforehand in per-
mitting the Church’s organ to state that his
Eminence would ask for St. Patrick’s holiday
merely as a formal matter, and that immediately
thereafter it would be gazetted. Even John See
got his back up at that.
The members of the Victorian Chamber of
Manufacturers, who got raw throats shouting
down Turner’s Wages Boards, don’t like the
alternative proposal of the Factories Commission
to have a Conciliation and Arbitration Act. At
a recent meeting a person called Scarlett, who
happens to be president, denounced the report of
the commission until his breath gave out, and he
couldn’t denounce any longer. Then other mem-
bers of the Chamber took up the great curse, and,
when they became too tired, it was determined to
take a second wind and bring the matter up at a
subsequent meeting. Scarlett, among a number
of other barbaric remarks, said : “I think we
should ask the Government to be good enough to
leave us alone altogether in regard to wages, and
let employers fix their own wages. They always
did so iii the past, and they are just as capable of
doing so to-day.” But employers did not always
fix the wages of those who worked for them in the
past. When the employer was left quite untram-
melled he did not fix any wages at all. He sent
out ships and collared a mob of slaves, whom he
coupled together like young steers and worked to
death. Then, when he wanted more, he merely
sent out another ship and roped in another mob.
■Scarlett, Derham and co. declare that they don’t
want to sweat their employees. Of course they
don t. Sweating is paying inadequate wages.
1'hese men would gladly pay no waves at. all.

(Greener's Representative).
What a contemptible lie is that of the fat
Tories that Upper Houses are necessary as a check
on hasty legislation ! About the only proposal
in Irvine’s Reform Bill that was hasty—that
hadn’t been considered and talked over and
demanded frequently—was the separate repre-
sentation of public servants, and that was almost
the only item the House of 48 Rich Landlords
failed to review or amend or reject. If its
hypocritical platitude really meant anything, it
should have dallied over such a revolutionary
proposition for at least ten years.
Scarlett, chairman of Melbourne Chamber
of Manufactures, assures all whom it may concern
that if Compulsory Arbitration has succeeded in
Maoriland, it is only because Maori land is “ prin
cipally an agricultural State.” Maoriland, the
Socialistic State with the very heavy Protective
tariff, has, in proportion to its population, a larger
manufacturing population than any other State in
Australasia ; its factory employees increased from
27,336 in 1896 to 48,718 in 1901; and the output
of its factories is almost exactly equal to that bf
Victoria, though it has only two-thirds Victoria’s
population. Therefore Mr. Scarlett may consider
himself a squashed Tory and a bowled-out ro-
mancer.
“ Glenara ” writes :
What a blistering “ front ” these Victorian farmer-men
have 1 After holding an “ indignation ” meeting in
Warracknabeal to protest against the inadequacy of the
Government seed-wheat relief to distressed farmers, a
number of them trotted down to interview Lands Minis-
ter Taverner and beg for more. And the -modest pro-
posal of the cadging crowd was that, as the granted
£100,000 was insufficient, another £150,000 should be
splashed in the Mallee and Wimmera districts. And,
year by year, the brave old Argus comes along with its
“Save the Mallee” and “The Land Our Mainstay"
articles, and G. Reid and his under-studies wander round
the Commonweath cursing violently at “ coddled indus-
tries” and enlarging on the value of “our natural
resources.” No wonder an aggravated community sits
- blasts daily papers and poli-
ticians with a fearful blast.
YVlien To life her, M.L.A., recently moved,
in Vic. Assembly, “that the office of State
Governor be abolished,” he trod on many public
corns. Irvine opposed the motion on the ground
that “our relations with ‘the dear old mother
country’ had to be considered.” He said that
the Governor was the medium between the States
and the Imperial authorities, and he had tried
to maintain the States’ right to communicate
with the said Imperial authorities independently
of the Federal Government. Finally, he darkly
hinted that if the motion was pressed it would be
the first step to depriving Victoria of those rights,
and breaking the close relations which existed
between the States and the Old Country. After
that, the debate was indefinitely postponed with
enthusiasm. Surely if Australia wants to crawl
to any far-off Government, the Governor-General
can wipe the floor with his weskit on our account
without the State Governors to help him.
“ Leila ” on Woman’s Suffrage
Re the troubles of policemen who collect female voters’
names. In our village recently an old Scotchwoman
stamped her foot violently and “ chivvied " the offending:
“bobby ” off the premises. She’d gie her name to nae man
at that hour o’ night" (8 p.m. on a Summer evening).
On this Women’s Franchise subject it’s Bickening to hear
the “ tommy rot ” talked by the man who scruples not to
make white slaves of women. Heard one lately say that
no " pure and lofty woman ” should ever degrade herself
by visiting a polling-booth. I inquired if he minded send-
ing a “ pure and lofty woman ” to milk cows in dirty stock-
yards, or hiring her at starvation wages to go down on her
knees and scrub his dirty floors. He told somebody after-
wards that he was much disappointed in me.

“John R.” writes (The Bulletin pub-
lishes this with inward misgiving):—
What is the purport of all this row about Mr. Prynnge
stating from the judicial bench the real motives of mili-
tary establishment? Seems strange that such candor
should be considered censurable in a Judge who merely
verified what has been alleged by the Labor party. The
Judge laid down the law as it exists with all its oppressive
incidence. Its outward visible sign is repressive force,
centred in militarism, to which it is useless to oppose
moral force. It is advisable to induce the masses to
peacefully submit to measures instituted to ipake them
pay for the maintenance of force to render the said
masses duly submissive ; to forge their own fetters, as it
were. Alas, for our Judges I Compelled to sit on an
elevated, stuffy bench, bedizened in grotesque headgear
and Guy Fawkes’ toggery, to play bogey to an unsophisti-
cated crowd. And then, should they occasionally move
the gag of official reticence and blurt out the truth as to
the maintenance of “ law and order,” the democratic
press is down on them like a ton of feathers. The
Sorrows of Satan are trivial in comparison with the in-
felicitous position of a Judge addicted to outbursts of
candor calculated to give the whole legal show away.
But even su pposing Mr. Prynnge is a Labor
party emissary giving the show away on the
bench, why need “John R.” thus give Mr.
Prynnge and the Labor party away ?

Zola’s gruesomeincident in “DoctorPascal”
of the boy degenerate who bleeds on being as
much as touched, recorded a rare medical phe-
nomenon which was matched at Tarnagulla (vie.)
the other day : —
The third son of Constable Foers who was nearly
thirteen years of age, was seized with a fit of bleeding
from the nose two or three weeks ago, and last week he
started bleeding from the eyes and all parts of the body,
and rapidly bled to death. The medical efforts to stop
the bleeding were unavailing, as when it was stopped in
one place it broke out in another part of the body.
This disease is said to exist as a regular
thing in one part of the world only—a valley of
the higher Andes. Through the lack of air
pressure at that enormous height people of frail
constitution, and unacclimatised strangers, it is
alleged, often die through an escape of blood from
all parts of the body.
An American gentleman, who is almost as
well known in Sydney as Owe’Sullivan, on publio
meetings:—
I can’t understand your people at political meetings. I
vas here right through the Federal referendums and the
oelebrations, when the streets were packed with people,
and they were the best behaved crowds in the world-
better than the British, and better than our people. But
let a man get up to give a political address, and they’re a
lot of savages. In our country, people sit as quiet at a
political meeting as at a lecture. They come to hear
what the speaker has to say, and interruption is practi-
cally unknown. I remember one solitary exception,
down Carolina, among a pretty rough crowd. There was
a woman speaker, a regular he-woman and very aggres-
sive. They were not used to women speakers, and there
was a bit of interruption and something was thrown, not
at the speaker, but to create a disturbance. After all,
there wasn’t as much interruption as there is at what you
call a good meeting here ; but next day it was over the
whole country, and that place was taboo and they
couldn’t get a speaker to address a meeting there on
either side for quite a time.

JAYNE’S EXPECTORANT
CURES THE WORSTCOUGHSJ
Indeterminate Sentences.
'‘Burglar Bill” (of practical experience) joins in
the criminology debate
" Kiwi’s ” assertions are splendid; they breathe a pride
that has attained full maturity, "The criminal is an-
thropologically, socially, physically, and morally ab-
normal!” What beautifully worded rot 1 If he had
consorted with the “type abnormal” on his own level
during long, long days of mad remorse when the heart
opens with confidence, heard from his own lips the story
of his slip, marked the stumbling-blocks which Fate had
thrown in his way, he would then have the whole inside
life of the poor wretch before him. Take away the
sexual offender (who is in most cases a sexual lunatic)
and the kleptomanic, and you remove the only two
abnormal types. The others are mere victims of fate,
which squeezes them in weak places. I have met men of
“ Kiwi’s ” stamp who considered themselves “ normal ” In
fact, on bail once, one such refused to recognise me. Yet, 18
months afterwards, I shared a “ kit” of hominy with him.
“He does not feel punishment,’’ says “ Kiwi,” “ as we
fancy he does, but we measure him by our own standard
just the same.” This is the assertion of the man who
doesn’t know, and cannot know until he himself, through
some mysterious push, falls, and gazes aghast at his own
position 1 Then ne finds whether the criminal feels or
not, whether the torment of an active brain ceaselessly
working through hours of dull, mechanical labor ana
long, sleepless nights is not hell itself. I hope “Kiwi”
remains in ignorance of what the criminal feels—knowing
just as much as the criminologist who takes the daylight
bravado of the criminal at its full measure. Regarding
“ illiterate warders,” whom Ellis severely condemns,!
have found from actual experience, with my eyes open,
that the illiterate warder (not the brutal warder ; do nob
confuse the two) handles a prisoner much more skilfully
than the supposed educated warder who has received a
smattering of criminology, and whose mind is full of fads
regarding abnormal types, hereditary criminals, etc. I
have seen illiterate warders, in many cases, stop small
gaol ructions which might have ended in riots, just
because they knew the criminal to be a man of their own
type, who had unfortunately struck a greasy patch and
slipped. Place “ Kiwi,” for example, in charge of a
batch of prisoners, and his gleanings from Ellis
and others would breed an instinctive dislike in the
prisoners towards him. No matter how he acted he could
not treat them as men, but only as “ abnormal types.”
As to the indeterminate sentence, all that the criminolo-
gist would have to guide him would be the reports of the
gaol-governor and personal observations of the deputy-
governor and senior warders, and I have found, by long
experience, that the “ gaol-smooger,” full of cunning and
hypocrisy, generally gets the good reports as to conduct*
& c., and generally bluffs the educated deputy-governor
that he is a much-injured person. I have noticed that
this class of prisoner is never friendly w T ith the shrewd,
illiterate “ screw,” who relies on practical experience
only. The prison system of this State is at present being
ruined by a method of superficial cramming, under which
the warder who passes a small tinpot examination, in
which criminology (Duchesne text-book) forms the main
subject, is decorated with a star upon the collar, granted
seven days extra in his annual leave, and is eligible fo*
promotion. That star generally builds a barrier between
the warder and his charge—the warder, with his thimble-
ful of knowledge, suffers from a daily headache in trying
to analyse imaginary things in the prisoner’s brain, ques-
tioning him on the causes that led up to his crime, &c. ;
and the prisoner, if at all sensitive, is chafing under the
examination. Put as warders middle-aged men (not the
boys at present in the service). Let them be humane in
their treatment (this can be done by not giving prefer-
ence in promotion to the warder who has most “ cases ”
before the visiting “ beak ”). Keep away from the
warders’ library all books written by Ellis, Duchesne,
Lombroso, Ferri, and that ilk, and that is about the only
improvement we can make in our existing methods.
And the voice of experience having spoken, let all
men regard.
The "Squire” Curse.
“ Theta ” on landlordism in Vic.:—
There are here, unfortunately, many landowners who
are slavishly copying old-world methods in landlordism.
They have placed on their land, at high rentals, a large
number of tenants, whose treatment is opposed to the
best interests of the community. The general rule is to
let these farms on short leases, so that the tenant is de-
prived of all security of tenure and kept in a meet and
proper state of humility, while the landlord is
able to take advantage of every increase in the
value of land. This increase of value has been very pro-
nounced during the past 10 years, west Vic. grass' lands
that were once let for 15s. or 18s. per acre now bring 355.
or 408., while as much as £3 per acre per year has been
given for choice farms. There are three results, equally
inevitable and equally undesirable, attending this system.
The first is that, as the population grows and competition
increases, the rental value will rise until it reaches
Ricardo’s limit, on which the landlord takes all that is
left after providing a bare subsistence for the tenant.
This limit is within measurable distance already, and
often rents are paid which could not be earned on the
land, were it not that the tenant has a large and strong
family willing to work simply for their food, clothes, and
an annual trip to the nearest race-meeting. The high
rents are mainly the cause of the labor difficulty, of which
so much is heard. The tenant grinds down wages to the
lowest point, gives his men the poorest food, and practises
by compulsion, every mean and petty economy that he
can think of. Finally, the insecurity of tenure makes the
tenant practically a nonentity in his district. He has no
abiding place in the community, so that he is the poorest
of citizens, and shirks his share of all local responsibities.
A quaint feature of the situation is the fact that those
landlords really believe that they are “ settling the people
on the land.” ' At present most of them let their lands
privately, and, in many cases, at reasonable rates. In
doing this they seem to stand astounded at their own
moderation, But even though individuals, such for
instance as J. 0. Manifold, M.H.R., are known as “ good
landlords,” it is certain that in the near future all of
these farms will be let by open competition. As a matter
of fact many of them are so let now. The Warrnamboo
(Vic.) Standard of Feb. 4th has an advt. of an “ Important
leasing of high-class agricultural farms ” —varying from 45
to 170 acres, and all belonging to one man—for which
tenders are invited. In the same district it has long been
the custom to let cultivation blocks at auction, by which
means the last penny is screwed out of the unfortunate
tenant.
We thus have the miserable anti-social spectacle of a
small, rich, landed class growing steadily richer at the
expense of the vast mass of the bone and sinew of the
community ; while at the same time the status of the
tenants is being steadily reduced by the fact that the
mean and poor-living farmer —who can pay the highest
rent —is ousting the decent and good-living tenant.
*
Mr. E. Maxky, of Gympie (Q), writes what
looks like good horse sense about irrigation :
Re the Red Page of a recent issue and Ernest
Favenc’s rhapsodising over glorious results to be achieved
in the cause of irrigation per medium of artesian bores
and conservation of water within convenient natural
reservoirs. Beyond affording a copious drinking supply
to thirsty beasts, what is the good of artesian water?
In many instances, it not only discourages the growth
of useful fodder grasses, hut raises in their place a thick
vegetation of glaring and useless weeds, heavily impreg-
nated with various weird mineral constituents, which no
self-respecting sheep or steer will nibble. Now, as to water
conservation by means of dams or locks. Whatabout Lake
George ? Was it not at least 30 or 40 miles in area with a
maximum depth of 20ft ? It was effectually dried up last
year. Ido not doubt fora moment that there are favorable
storage areas in the comparatively mountainous regions
of Australia—say Monaro, or the Darling and Lachlan
tablelands. But, for beneficial irrigation on a large scale,
you want to take hold of an enormous and characteristic
region such as Riverina. To have an adequate effect the
stored waters must not be hundreds of miles distant from
the arid districts. Where can large volumes of water be
imprisoned, during the infrequent wet seasons, that will
not be remorselessly evaporated by the next big drought
equal in intensity and bitterness to the one of 1895-1902?
Is there another country on the globe that can be com-
pared to Australia in its dearth of mountain systems,
lakes, and rivers ? Where is our Nile or Colorado :
where, even, our desert wells ?

The Treasurer of the State of Queensland, through the Queensland National. Bank Limited,
invites APPLICATIONS for the PURCHASE, at a fixed price of £lO2, of £600,000 TREASURY
BILLS, bearing interest at the rate of Four (4) Per Cent, per annum. , , . ... ,
Six months interest will be payable on the Ist July, 1903. Principal and all interest will be
payable, free of exchange, in Queensland, Sydney, Melbourne, or Adelaide.
Income arising from Queensland Government Treasury Bills is exempted from income tax under
“ The Income Tax Act of 1902 ” (Queensland). . , . ,
Full information will be given, prospectuses may be obtained, and applications will be received
at the Queensland National Bank Limited, in Queensland and Sydney, and the National Bank of
Australasia Limited, Melbourne and Adelaide. , ' ~, . _ _ T _
The LIST of APPLICATIONS will be closed on or before TUESDAY, the 31st MARCH, 1903.
The Treasury. Brisbane. ISth Febrnarv. y

An instance of real friendship is the case
of that North Melbourne man who had his ear
bitten off by his mate in the course of a discussion,
but refused to prosecute lest the mate should be
offended. The love of David and Jonathan never
stood such a test, for Jonathan, so far as we know,
never chewed David.

They sat right up in the back row of the gallery. He
had his arm around her waist, and she rested her head on
his shoulder. He bought her a packet of peanuts and a
bottle of lemonade and she was in heaven. They cheered
the hero and hooted the had nobleman—at least she did.
He was thinking. Then oame the heroine with her little
baby that toddled up to the knees of the bad nobleman
and cried, “ Daddy, Daddy.” “ Ob,” said the girl, wiping
her eyes, “ it’s crule.” " Shut up,” said the man, “ turn
your hose off.” It was over, and they went down the
long steps, out across Hyde Park, and home. At the
back gate he was leaving her and she was crying.
Jack, Jack, why don’t you act the man ? Everyone will
know soon.” He had started to walk across the road, but
he turned with a theatrical gesture. “ No, I won’t, he
said. " The flaming, crimson lord wouldn’t do it, so I
won’t.” Then he walked up Duke-street home.
Relations between two theatrical magnates in Australia
are strained. The other night Manager X. and ladies
called at Y.’s theatre, expecting the usual courtesy of the
manager’s box. But Y. was in a nasty mood, and stated
gruffly that he would sooner play to empty benches than
: to-adjective paper ; and anyone could have the box who
lifted to pay two guineas. X. paid the money rather than
look small; he is now waiting till Y. comes his way 1
“ Kaylee”: Yarn re threatened pugilistic encounter
’twixt Alf. Woods and the Mayor and town clerk of
Ararat, reminds writer that Woods is a dangerous mum-
mer for his size. I have vivid recollections of him
threatening sundry large cabmen, liftmen, railway
porters and orchestra-men (several dozen of the latter),
with a wild regardlessness of odds that was very ha *•
ful to behold. In a previous state of existence, Alt was
evidently a soldier ant.
Miss Lottie Lome, now with a variety co. In Tasmania,
is advertised as “ the well-known Imitator of her
(Johnnie Coleman’s) scarecrow dance." It seems as if

man family.

A Hindustani groveller composed a Coronation Song
for the Delhi Durbar. One verse of it:
“ In regal robes enthroned on high
Big princes thronged around :
A mother’s son 1 O sea-girt isle 1
Thrice happy, happy ground I”
A blaok grovel comes to pretty much the same thing as a
white grovel. In his next verse the bard exhorts all
people, young and old, to come and feast their sight on
the inoffensive stout gentleman who had given rise to the
Durbar.

The latest Paris play—" Leurs Amants is a cynical,
up-to-date study in female infidelity. Heroine wavers
awhile between two lovers, one a wealthy, elderly'person,
quite commonplace apart from his coin ; the other a hard-
working young litterateur. She throws in her lot with
the writer. He continues to be fairly successful as writers
go; her home is comfortable enough, but existence is dull.
A more moneyed life would be less monotonous ; so the
girl leaves her literary friend with calm protestations of
eternal friendship, and goes back to the old man. The
Story is truer to human nature than “ Zaza,” anyway.
This is not an age of romance, as the old fashioned word
was understood when Dumas’ fils wrote “ Camille.”
Romance began to perish with the earliest suggestion of
Woman Suffrage. The first knell of Valentine’s Day was
sounded by the advanced female on a public platform,
and the knickerbocker girl finally buried St. Valentine to
the ting of her passing bicycle bell. “ Romance ”is off
the programme at present. It will break out again in a
hew form after the social rules of sex relationship have
been carefully revised and amended, like the old
Scriptures.
“ T.D.” : The most humorous portion of the A.N.A.
competition, just worked off in Melb., was the series of
dreadful “ musical ” efforts turned off by local composers.
There were over 50 of these, and, in the language of the
newspaper fire reporter, the effect can be better imagined
than described. Each composer secured the services of a
fairly experienced singer to help his (or her) song through,
and in this way a few deserving and not too affluent
people came by an honest half-guinea or so. Thus the
A.N.A. shivoo has at least one thing to recommend it.
But some of the things were so maudlin, or so uncon-
sciously humorous, or so painfully wooden and churchy,
that the singers themselves could not preserve a serious
demeanor, and fled from the platform before they were
half through. In one case the audience became so
demonstrative that the composer herself suddenly quitted
the piano to have a good cry, and left a well-known
concert singer on the platform doing his best for a couple
of bars until he found there was no more accompaniment.
Than he also left, amid unavailing demands for an encore.
“ H.M.” writes to The Bulletin about Ellen Terry and
Henry Irving “ Ellen Terry—what expression and life,
what a voice, what a charm of manner, and above all,
what personality 1 She had invited me to her box, and
we sat through ‘Faust,’ in which Cissie LoftUs was
attempting to play her old part of. Marguerite, She
whispers frank comments on the company, her eyes
laughing and clouding in turn, her face flashing and
changing, her broad, good-humored mouth smiiing in
childish amusement now and then, and her large, capable
hand striking the knee of my startled self in her uncon-
scious anxiety. She is so thoroughly, so disarmingly
natural. She takes you to her heart at once, and you
are her friend. The charm of her manner lies, 1 believe,
in the youth of it. This faded actress of 53 is so
young—only a merry girl. But when, in the inter-
vals, the lights grow bright, you look into the face of
an old, eccentrically-dressed woman, with short-sighted
eyes, with the sweetness of perfect good humor in them
as in her mobile mouth, soft grey hair, and deep, half-
broken voice. And when I asked if the rumor that she
was going to Australia was true, she laughed and shook
her head. ‘Oh no,’ she said, 1 why should we ? ’ So many
beginners go out and copy us ; what chance would we
have ? Why, we would be accused of imitating them and
of being old. Ugh 1 that is the unpardonable sin.’
She told me they have often to evade the too
kind attention of unknown admirers. One day in
Londqn a lady accosted Sir Henry, gazed at him
adprihgly, and said : 1 Oh, you are Sir Henry Irving,
are you not? I want’ ‘ No, Madam, I'm not—l am
very like him, he answered, and took refuge in a passing
cab, leaving the astonished lady gazing after him from
the footpath. And now, a humble opinion of their
acting. 1 had heard so much of the peculiarities
and mannerisms of Irving that I was quite surprised to
find that I could hear him distinctly, and that, at times,
his acting was excellent. 1 don’t deny that he is
peculiar, sometimes even grotesque, but always clever
and interesting. One minute you are carried away by
the force and realism of his acting, and the next you are
irritated by some absurd exaggeration. But there is
always reason in his method. You can see what he
means, even if it does not appeal to you. For instance,
as Shylock. I was at first inclined to be disappointed
when he left out the frenzied ravings, the wild-repeated
cries for his money, his dueats, and his daughter, in the
scene where he finds he has lost them. He seemed quiet,
but later I saw his meaning, and 1 had to admit that he
made Shylock a much higher and more intellectual type
than any other actors 1 have seen. He was the Jew of
Venice, smarting uiider the sense of wrongs he could
never hope to*avenge, not the angry Jew of modern
London, disguised in a gaberdine. I have seen nothing
more artistic than his exit from the court, the blind feel-
ing for the door, the hopelessness of the face, the silence
—nothing could have been finer. One might say of Irving
that if his speech is sometimes leaden, his silence is
always golden. It was silent effect that made his fortune
in ‘The Bells.’"

A Wllcannia (N.S.W.) storekeeper, named John O’Oon-
nell, went to bed one night last week in apparently the

had become totally blind. He is now under special treat-
ment In Adelaide.

“HP”' Be the Charlton (Vic.) farmers;
going to Zion City (B. 14/2/’03): I remember a
family leaving for Salt Lake City about 20 j ears-
ago. They were “converts” of a couple of
“missionaries” from Brigham Youngs domain,
who came to our township. The family worki g
people—saved and scraped for some yeara to pay
their passages. For a long time nothing was
heard of them. At length patheticstones reached
us of the sufferings and hardshipsendured, without
hope for the future. The existence was slavery,
body and soul. “ Would that we could get back
to Australia,” was the burden of our old neighbors
cry. The poor Charlton farmers will probably
feel the same way. Those distant hills are dread-
fully brown when you get there.

Melb. lady recently solemnly stated she'd
found a human finger in a tin of factory jam. As
fruit has to be cut up and peeled in a hurry for
cheap jam, there are some possibilities in “four-
pence a tin.”
A practical people, the Italians. A rich
American has just bought a Botticelli for £14,000
from a hard-up prince, and the picture was
smuggled away in defiance of the law, which
forbids the sending of any ancient works of
art out of the country. But the curious fact of an
Italian prince having any money could not be
concealed, and an unsympathetic court promptly
fined him—just -£14,000 !
To ensure greater correctness in ascertain-
ing a ship’s longitude at sea than is obtainable by
either log or chronometer, it has been proposed
that the chief maritime nations should take con-
certed action to have noon and midnight signals,
Greenwich time, flashed across the oceans from all
Marconi stations. »• *
Adelaide Boys’ Institute has invented a
new kind of Sunday evening service for children.
It is held on the Institute lawn, in a crowded
part of the city, and contains more dumb : beli
selections than addresses or texts. On specially
hot nights it finishes with a free half-hour for the
boys in a swimming-bath.

THE PIANOLA
ey mm.
V
r=D
p %
j
What
It
I
Means
To
You.
If you are the owner of a piano which ia seldom or never used, little
benefit is derived from it by either you or your family. By the purchase
of a Pianola every member of your family can play the piano, although he
or she may never have played one note in their life.
The striking of the notes of the selection, in proper time and place, is
no concern of the player. This is correctly done by the Pianola.
The player’s only task is to decide how slow or how fast the notes shall
be played, or how loud or soft he wants them to sound.
Three little levers serve to impart the player’s wishes to the piano.
Should the selection not be familiar, a very simple set of markings is given
on the music-roll which unwinds in front of the player, directing the proper
movement of the levers.
If you will write us, asking for Catalogue B, we will mail you one
free, explaining more fully the Pianola.
The price of the Pianola is £6O prompt cash, or £65 on moderate
monthly payments.^

Jersey’s Yarn.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Being the sort of lie which is superseding the crude
snake-yam in the Australian bush.)
It was a lonely house in a lonely part.
“Yes,” said the woman who came to the door,
“I can give you something to eat. I’m glad you
called. I’ve got my husband here very ill; he
might not last the night. I don’t like the look of
him, and there’s no one but me in the house.”
In the kitchen she produced some victuals and
made tea. While I was eating she talked a bit
about her husband.
“ He's pretty low ” —jerking her thumb towards
another room—“he can’t leave his bed, and he
doesn’t seem all right in his head.”
Later on—“I think while you’re here I’ll saddle
the horse and go for the doctor. He is about four
miles off.”
I offered to go, but she assured me I’d never
find the place in the dark; she knew the way
and would be there and back in no time.
“I don’t think he’ll want anything,” she said,
“he calls sometimes, and when I go in he has
dozed off again. If he asks for a drink you can
give it to him ; it will be all right.”
She got a hat and jacket. I put the saddle on
for her and when she had gone off in the starlight
I went into the house again. Dog-tired and worn
out with a long day’s tramp, I mechanically turned
down the lamp that was on the table and rolled
on to a sofa in the corner. I did think of peeping
in to see what the patient was like first, but then
it might disturb him. So inside of two minutes I
was almost asleep when, creak, went the passage
door and the sick man came into the room in
his nightshirt—the man who couldn’t leave his
bed.
By the time he’d got to the middle of the room
I had jerked myself to a sitting-up posture facing
him. My idea was that delirium had given him
a burst of unnatural strength enabling him to get
up and that I would have to see about putting him
back again.
He looked a bit haggard, but beyond that there
was nothing.out of the way about his appearance.
He stopped the other side of the table.
“ It’s all right,” he said. “ I came out for some
water ” —he had a cup in his hand. “ Where’s the
missus?”
“Gone for the doctor.”
“ Did she say how long she’d be ? ”
All the while he eyed me so closely, as if taking
my measure, that I concluded he was simply curious
just to have a look at me. He’d heard us talking
together, and she, thinking he was asleep, had
not bothered to tell him anything about me. But
he eeriainly did not seem so ill as she had made
out; He got water out of a dipper on the table,
and after another hard look my way, said “ Good-
night,” and went back to his room again.
I had walked 30 miles that day, and soon
ceasing to think any mote about him, went
soundly off to sleep. I don’t know how long I’d
been asleep, but my next disturbance was the
noise of revolver-shots in the house.
“Hullo!” thought I, “he’s shot himself!”
and jumped up and made for the passage.
Couldn’t see anything, but somebody called out:
“ Back veu go, or I’ll shoot! ” I could do nothing,
so I backed, and the man—the invalid man with
the haggard face—came stamping down the
passage into the kitchen after me. . ,
He was fully dressed this time, and armed with
a revolver. There was not much of the invalid
about him, however mad he might be.
“Now, young feller’’—pulling his pistol up—
“you just sit down there and listen to me. * ou
think there’s been a murder done here, perhaps.
Perhaps there has, perhaps not, perhaps two ot
’em, but whether there has or no you don t know
anything about it. I wasn’t ill at all only
shamming. My wife told you she was going for
the doctor, but I know who she was going for very
well I’ve been on his track along time and just
set this to catch ’em, and to-night, while you were
asleep, I dressed and waited up for em. JNow,
what you’re going to do is to take your solemn
oath not to breathe a word to any living soul of
what you’ve heard to-night, or else 111 blow your
brains out. Go in that room there, and you 11 see
a Bible ; fetch it out and swear on it.
All this he said with his revolver up. I got the
book, and we had a formal swearing.
“ Now I’ve got your oath I’ll trust it, he said.
“Do you want to stop on here till the morning .
Or mebbe you’d rather get out of the place. You
can take some tea and some bread with you—the
track on the right goes straight to Longford. _
I took this hint, and, thanking him, told him 1
didn’t care to stop around there any longer, and
humped my traps to go along the track to the
r to doubt he’d shot both the man and the
woman; but I couldn’t help that now my own
miserable skin was safe for the present, anyhow,
and I couldn’t do any good stopping there longer.
Next morning I was sitting on the bank ot a
creek not a great distance off. The sun was up
and warm ; the grass thick and /velvety ; the
water deliciously cool, and in the air the s/ingmg
of locusts, and the distant sound of splitters at
work somewhere. ,
I was just sitting thinking about things—about
the murder and that, and wondering if it were
possible that it was mainly a lunatic s tale, after
all, when up rode a mounted trooper behind me,
and, without getting off his horse, asked where 1
had camped the night before. .
I pointed in the direction of the house, and said
“ Over there.”
“ At the house ? "
I nodded. .
“Did you hear any shooting while you were
there?”
“No.”
“You’re sure?”
“ Yes,” said I, lopking at him steadily; there
was no shooting while I was there.”
“ Jolly good job you said so, young feller. I d
have blown your brains out if you hadn’t.” _
It was the man of the house again—disguised
a fact I’d found out for myself just in time.
• • • *
Jersey was silent, scraping his pipe out.
“Well, what came after that?” asked Long
Bill and Anderson together.
“ That’s all I know,” said Jersey, mournfully.
