My Lover.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Thou awful and mysterious sojourner
Upon an earth of shadows and unrest,
Wakening in me a strange disquietude,—
What and whence art thou ?
In the obscure past,
Upon an earth, weird, and to us unknown,
That spirit lived and moved, gyved in a form
That no conjecture can discover; mine
Too lived and moved. We have inherited
That which our ancient fathers had from eld !
Whence is the fabric that composes “thee”?
So like this “ me,” and yet so different!
How have thy bodily elements been knit
And folded round the spirit thou dost bear ?
Our elements may be co-mingled now ;
Yet our attraction has repulsion too,
And between these we pause and hesitate.
Lives there a mood, swift and imperious,
Of some old lover in thee, one that swayed
A world of men and women and then died ?
With such an influence thou comest to me !
Dwells there within thee but a smallest part
Of one that warred with part of that in me
And slew, or else was slain ?—for times there be
When love is veiled with a most ominous mood.
A glimpse of terror from thy presence breaks;
What creature’s dust may in thy cheek repose,
Or monstrous shape fashion a part of thee ?
Some brute that trampling crushed the tender
herb,
Some lurid rock that earth smokingly hurled,
And ages ground to dust, absorbed in thee ?
That passes, ghost-like ; and a sweeter mood
Speaks of the resurrection of fair things
That live in thee as mortals live in joy.
The scattered dust of Queens may lodge in thee ;
The dust of creatures lowly, blind and frail;
The vapors that have dyed the violet,
Or that in evening calm the sun has dyed.
Thou hast the allurement of the luminous sea,
Moon-gladdened, infinite ; the exquisite
And musical tenderness of airs
Trembling with bird-song and the murmuring bee ;
The persuasiveness of love-compelling flowers :
Thy life that was lingers about thee still !
The breath thou breathest may have touched the
lips
Of our first parents, whatever shape they bore !
Thou art no new thing ; only a new form
Of ancient and most venerable parts
Reknit in living substance. Therefore, I,
Feeling myself kin in antiquity,
Look curiously on thee if I may trace
The immemorial parts of ages flown,
Mysterious, awful, whirled like sands and rain
Limitless ages between earth and sky,
Dispersed, and all these variable parts
Reanimate. Another heir of Time
Stands before thee, and gazing through those
eyes
That beam as windows in the shrouding mists
Of past and future, speculates with awe
What once thou wast who now art what thou
art!
M.L.
AJAU.
Our New Selection.
A FAMOUS EXPLORER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Located near the range, with its peaks and
precipices, its scrub, hanging rock and head
waters, Ruddville became known to all sorts of
people. City folk, tourists, happened along at
holiday-time and caused a stir. Often, when the
one at the township was in use, they hired our
buggy. Often they didn’t—it all depended how
Dad regarded them.
* • • •
It was the day remembered by everyone—the
day the O’Briens and the Bradys fell out and
fought in the lane and Mrs. O’Brien bit off
Brady’s finger—tfie day Mrs. Mathers and Mrs
Andy Mullens left for Brisbane to see the ex-
hibition and took their luggage in a cradle and
two tea-tins.
Distinguished visitors —a Minister of the Crown
and several M’s. P.—- were coming to Ruddville.
“ Dinny ” Donoghue, our reputed useless
member was the cause of it. He arranged with
Dad to meet the party, supply saddle-horses, and
escort it to the Falls and Parker’s Point, twelve
miles along the range. Attractive scenery
abounded at those places, and the party was to
spend a day or so viewing it. Nothing could have
pleased Dad more. He was in his element. He
would have found horses fora hundred had it been
necessary.
The party arrived. No band—no address —no
presentation—no distinguished gathering, except
the Regans and Kelly and Thompson’s men lean-
ing over the fence.
The dogs barked a welcome, and Dad intro-
duced the visitors to Mother and Sarah, who tried
desperately to appear composed. Then he treated
them to refreshments. Lots of people round our
way reckoned a Minister wouldn’t touch spirits.
But they couldn’t have entertained any at their
places. This one touched it—the whole party
did except Mr. De Hartigan. But he wasn t a
member of Parliament; he was only with the
party.
“No, no,” De Hartigan said, stalking about
the room composedly inspecting the enlarged
portraits hanging on the wall —“never touch it
when I’m in the bush.” , ,
Dad looked round at him and stared. Dad had
never known anyone to refuse before.
Some of the visitors smiled. Ihe Minister,
mother thought, frowned. We heard, afterwards,
that he didn’t approve of De Hartigan s presence.
De Hartigan was an heroic person. A man o
about fifty, and five-feet-ten ; a lofty forehead,
discontented-looking features, and wore , the
appearance of a man waiting at the butchers on
a very hot day to be served with meat. A per-
son, too, full of assurance, with an overweening
predilection for heavv words, and afflicted with a
delusion that his frame and constitution were ot
cast iron ; the latter he impressed on people oy
trving to break their hands instead of shaking.
Worst of all, he insisted being regarded as an
Before starting to the Range, Dad invited the
party to “ have a look round.”
They inspected the machinery, the dairy, and
the cows. The Minister was delighted. The
nngs he saw seemed to make him regret his own
uinble avocation. He said he envied Dad, and
spoke s of the freedom and independence of a
aimers life, and loudly repined because there
weren t millions of men with Dad’s industry and
lnte .i e ? ce on ie l anc k (He said the same of
'"ring of settlers at Black Duck three days
later.) J
- .i De Hartigan was not so impressed by the
surroundings. Queensland dairymen, he reckoned,
began at the wrong end. Hartigan was a plain-
spoken man. He said it would pay most of them,
before commencing operations, to sit down and
find out how much they knew about a cow.
Look at that waster of a heifer, there,” he
remarked, pointing, by way of illustration, to
oiiky, a nine-year-old cow; ‘‘ a Hereford, right
enough ; but see the udder she has—all flesh. She
wouldn’t keep a cat in milk.”
Silky was the best milker we had ; a shorthorn
that Dad paid thirteen guineas for.
Dad tried to explain these things to De Harti-
gan, but the latter turned a torrent of rhetoric on
Dad and confused him.
“Well, she was sold as a shorthorn to me,”
Dad mumbled in conclusion.
“ Just as I say,” De Hartigan retorted; “and,
a fortiori, you were sold.”
The Minister showed displeasure. He scowled,
and Joe overheard him say things about De
Hartigan to Mr. Fitzgerald, member for Up-
North.
“Bloodwood,” De Hartigan observed, placing
both hands on a yard railing as though he would
pull it down.
“ Ironbark,” Dad said, correcting him. “ Split
on the ridge there,” pointing with his finger.
De Hartigan made grimaces.
“ Tut, man,” he said, eyeing the timber closely,
“ not a rail of it. Eucalyptus corymbosa.”
Dad was knocked out.
* * • *
Ready to start. Joe, who with Dad was to
accompany the expedition, led out the horses and
was allotting each his mount.
De Hartigan waved him off.
“ Oh, dear, no ! ” he said, “ / require no horse.”
“ It’s fifteen mile,” Dad put in, staring at him.
“ Make no difference if it were thirty, ” Hartigan
answered, buttoning his coat up, “ only exercise
to a man when he’s done his sixty and seventy in
a day, carrying a rifle and tomahawk, exploring
in the interior.”
Several of his friends tried to persuade him to
change his mind, but in vain.
The explorer expanded his chest.
“ You’ll have to stir your mokes up,” he said,
“to keep me in sight.” Then, taking a piece of
bramble in his hand for a walking-stick, he started
off through the paddocks, making strides that
provoked Dave and Sarah to merriment and the
Minister to expressions of resentment.
The mounted division followed.
It was a brilliant day, all sunlight and bright-
ness.
The two-mile hut was passed, Hartigan still
swinging along, well in the lead.
Dad reckoned he was an extraordinary man.
Joe, with finger and mouth, whistled like a
locomotive. De Hartigan glanced back over his
shoulder. Joe waved his hand to indicate
a change of course. Hartigan swung into line
without shortening his stride.
The party laughed—all except the Minister.
He was a man without freedom or gaiety.
Five miles were covered.
Hartigan’s action became erratic. He puffed,
sweated. Gradually he “cune back” to the
horses. Burr and grass-seed clung to his clothes.
Gullies, undergrowth and patches of blady growth
impeded his path, broke his pace. The smallest
stone, a stick, the slightest unevenness in the
earth caused him to trip.
Six miles.
Hartigan fell in the rear ; removed his coat and
carried it on his shoulder.
'Dad became concerned. He dropped back and
invited the explorer to hang on to the stirrup.
De Hartigan wouldn’t. He declined with a
shake of the head. He didn’t speak; he couldn’t.
Dad rode along beside his guest in silence.
Hartigan removed his collar and hat, and
carried them in his hand.
Five miles more to tramp. The horsemen were
out of sight.
Dad became agitated.
“Look here,” he said, coming to a standstill
and sliding off his mare ; “ you get up— Fll walk
the rest.”
Hartigan sank in the grass.
“It’s the first time” he gasped, when he re-
covered sufficient breath, “that I’ve done any
walking in this sort of country.” Then he
mounted the mare and encouraged Dad, who
blundered feebly along, with cheerful chunks of
information about the fauna and flora of the Gulf
country. .
The mounted division had arrived at the I alls,
and dismounted at an old hut there.
“Confounded shame !” said the Minister when
De Hartigan hove in sight, perched on Dad’s
mare, while Dad himself staggered along on foot.
None of the others spoke. They stared. A sort
of depression was over them.
“ By Jove ! ” De Hartigan called ou t as he came
U p_“only my friend here would insist upon
getting down to stretch his limbs I wouldn’t have
been far behind you.” „
Dad was too exhausted to say anything. He
drank a lot of water, then sat and wiped the
perspiration from his face. .
After resting, the party put in a tew hours
sight-seeing and shooting. Then they returned to
the hut, had tea, and smoked. .
When the moon rose they went off again and
mooched round for wallabies.
De Hartigan wouldn’t carry a gun. Said tie
didn’t want any. He walked beside Dad all the
time, watching for game for him. His eyes were
better than Dad’s, and he saw the things first.
“Steady! steady!” he would whisper when
anything presented itself, and Dad would stand
still and peer excitedly all round the compass.
Then De Hartigan, without rmpving his eyes from
the object, would reach out his hand and noise-
lessly take the gun from Dad and fire it off. He
would always hand it back again, though, and
wait till Dad rammed another charge into her.
Dad didn’t remonstrate, but was struck with a
brilliant idea. He stuffed a heavy double (marge
into the old gun, and prowled along beside his
exuberant guest witli a wicked grin on his face.
“There! there!” De Hartigan whispered
hoarsely, as the white front of a wallaby showed
itself right under their noses.
Dad held out the gun to him.
“ No, no, no,” Hartigan said, you haven t had
a 'T\nli <! adjimed qU Dad tremblingly raised the
weapon, fired, and fell flat on his back.
All were preparing to turn-in, making their
beds on the floor of the hut. , , c tx
“Haven’t you a rug, sir? Dad asked of De
Hartigan, much concerned.
Hartigan hadn’t. Nevei carried one in his life.
*Twas always his practice, he said, to sleep with-
out covering of any kind : same with all explorers.
All he required was something level to lie on—the
harder it was the sounder he could sleep.
A slab table stood in the centre of the hut, its
legs fixed in the earth. With calm indifference,
De Hartigan mounted that table, stretched him-
self full length upon it, folded his arms across his
chest, closed his eyes—and was asleep.
Those on the floor grinned and compared him
to Gladstone. But the Minister shook liis head
sceptically and tucked his rug about himself.
Half-an-hour and all were snoring—all but Joe.
He was interested in the explorer, and lay with
an eye on the table.
Hartigan didn’t sleep very long. He raised
himself cautiously, and by the light of the
moon and the flickering fire stared at the slumber-
ing forms beneath. Then he slipped quietly from
the table and crawled under Dad’s rug.
Dad must have thought it was mother; he put
his arms round De Hartigan and murmured
things about “calves” and “milk.”
At daybreak, Hartigan took to the table again,
and when the others opened their eyes he was sleep-
ing ...
t, , , . ,
Breakfast. Then some more sight-seeing and
shooting. _ _
After a light lunch, made off the remains of the
_ , i ’ t •
provender, they spoke of returning.
Joe packed-up and got the horses ready.
De Hartigan, his chest pushed out and head
tilted back, strode leisurely forward and mounted
Dad’s mare.
"Come along, M‘Master,” he said; “you and
I’ll be going on.”
They rode off.
Seeing Dad on foot, the Minister hesitated, and
stared about. He saw De Hartigan in advance,
waving his hand in debate with M'Master, and
grasped the position.
“It’s too d d bad ! ” he exclaimed, angrily,
“ too bad, indeed ! ”
He dismounted at once and resigned his horse
to Dad. But the member for Up-North and
Dinny Donoghue, who misrepresented our dis-
trict, wouldn’t hear of such a sacrifice. They
alighted also, both vigorously claiming the right
to “pad the hoof.” Finally, they agreed to tramp
and ride alternately, and Dad and the Minister
rode.
“Curse him!” Donoghue would hiss, staring
after the form of De Hartigan, every time his
turn came to walk. “May th’ horse break his
thick neck!”
Nearing the end of the journey, and about one
mile from Ruddville, De Hartigan happened, in-
cidentally, to look back. Perceiving Donoghue
laboring laggardly along on foot, he stopped.
“ Bless my soul,” he said when Donoghue came
up ; “why didn’t you sHout, man, for the horse?
Well, well, well, you’re a nice fellow ! Here, man.”
And he jumped down and hoisted the tired poli-
tician into the saddle before he could protest.
The explorer then extended himself and left the
party behind.
Approaching the house he saw mother, Sarah
and others watching from the verandah and felt
they were admiring his performance. He de-
termined to finish strong. The garden fence was
before him. Exulting in the consciousness of his
own agility, he took'off his hat, bounded lightly
in the air, ran at the palings, jumped, and fell
heavily on the geraniums.
* • • «
There’s another Ministerial party coming to
Ruddville soon, and Dad is wondering if that
fellow De Hartigan will be in it.

STEELE RUDD.
The pony of a Berwick (Vic.) undertaker
was recently found dead hanging from the fork of
a tree. Local verdict was that the pony had sui-
cided owing to slackness of trade.
N.S.W. Methodist president, Wools-Rut-
ledge, quotes with approval a conclusion which
is more religious than dogmatic, by “ lan
Maclaren”: —
The dying man is much less concerned than his father
would have been about his soul and what is going to
happen to him after death. He is more anxious about
what is going to happen to his wife and children.
A change for the better.
Stipendiary Donaldson, of Sydney, in-
veighed bitterly the other day against persons
who give offenders into custody and then fail to
turn up and testify at the trial. The practice is
certainly wrong, but it springs from more causes
than the indignant stipendiary seems aware of.
Often the prisoner instructs his friends to dis-
gorge the goods, when stolen goods are in
question ; but much more often the prisoner’s
friends, whatever the question is, simply terrify
the prosecutor, and he stays away for the good
of his health. It is the best thing he
can do in a city where one policeman has often
20 miles of streets and lanes to look after, and
where the push can often maul a victim for about
90 minutes before constabulary aid arrives—also
in a city where, even if the push is caught after
nearly butchering a peaceful citizen, a dull
stipendiary only fines it 40s. Also, prosecutors
often stay away because of the. objectionable and
violent manners of the police-court lawyer, and
the gorgon-like manner in which the average
stipendiary sits on the bench and allows that red-
nosed outcast to misbehave himself at his own
sweet will.

Disillusion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For some forty years, and over,
Poets had with me their way ;
And they made me think the Lover
Owned the Night and owned the Day;
And the Corpse beneath the Clover
Had a hopeful word to say.
And they made me think that Sorrow
Was a Shadow in the Sun ;
And they made me think To-Morrow
Was a gift to everyone :
And the days I used to borrow,
Till my credit now is done.
And they told me softly, sweetly,
That, when Life had lost its glee,
I could be consoled completely
By the Forest or the Sea;
And they wrote their rhymes so neatly
That they quite deluded me.
But when Sorrow is at sorest,
And the heart weeps silently,
Is there healing in the Forest ?
Is there solace in the Sea ?
And the God whom thou adorest
Has He any help for thee ?
Does it soothe the spent man dying
That the stars are shining bright
O’er the field where he is lying ?
And the moon, with all her light,
Does she help his bare soul flying
Through the vast and lonely Night ?
Give to me the grasp of true man,
Though his state be high or low,
Give to me the kiss of woman—
Let your Seas and Forests go :
There is nothing but the human
Touch can heal the human woe.
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
West Tasmania.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thebe is Tasmania and the Tasmanian West
Coast. The West Coast is a thing apart. To go
to the West Coast I was aroused by the too-early
porter, and after breakfast, by the light of acety-
lene, I let my toes freeze on the verandah till a
voice from under the hood of a cab called out,
“ All aboard ! ” My next impression is of sitting
in a dark, hard-seated compartment of a train,
with something else alive in the compartment
with me—man or woman, I couldn’t say. Then a
fellow came along with a lantern tucked under
his arm, examining all honest men’s tickets, after
which he left us in darkness again. Then we
started.
When we could see, I noticed that we were
locked and barred in, and I asked my companion
why the bars.
“ That’s to keep your brains in your head,” he
said, and forthwith explained that rock being ex-
pensive to cut, and the line being run by a private
co., they had just left room enough in the cuttings
for’ the train to get through, and the co. had
barred up the windows so as to safeguard the
brains of people with a too great enthusiasm for
scenery. We left a frost at Burnie, and it rained
higher up, and it snowed at the Junction, and it
rained all the rest of the way. But, in spite of all,
it was a splendid trip.
Two-thirds of the land of health and deep
slumber are rough mountainous expanses which
they have so far no use for. But the scenery of
those useless parts is magnificent, and it is on the
Emu Bay line you realise it more than elsewhere.
Maoriland scenery often awes you, but here every-
thing has a curvy, velvety softness most feminine
in grace. , .
The deep green of the myrtle is intermingled
with light-tinted sassafras, and silvery-sheened
wattle and graceful blackwood, and the bush is
sometimes sectioned by arabesques of button-grass
and back of it, distant hills, or black rock-topped
mountains meeting the grey sky. Sometimes you
look deep down to the iron-stained waters of a
creek, flowing at the foot of colonnades of moss-
covered tree butts, and dropping over fallen
saplings. The rocks are of wonderful tints. Some
are cream and some orange, and others rich red-
brown, and then the white quartz leaders run
through them. And the peculiarity is that the
scenery looks better under a grey sky and in the
falling rain. I saw it under a perfect sky and the
landscape was grey and dun; but when the
myrtle and sassafrass got their diamonds again it
seemed to laugh.
This is all because those trees have grown in the
land of everlasting rain. The rain-clouds all seem
to drift towards those mountains that Tasman
sighted and called after his ships. The sharp
peaks split the clouds and the rain falls. That is
why it rains more feet on the West coast than
inches in some parts of Australia. The average
is, I believe, about 168 inches per annum.
Zeehan is built on a bog, and gravel-paths lead
up to the doors. You must follow these paths
round to reach the person next door, and Queens-
town is much about the same. In Zeehan they
get silver and lead, and in Queenstown gold and
copper. Queenstown is the town of a blasted
valley. It is charred by fires, and is for ever
garbed in black and brown and dirty white ; that
is, when its sulphurous pall of pale fogs lift for it to
be seen. High-peaked, threatening mountains
hem and smother it in, each mountain covered
with black, charred stumps and brown peat; dirty
silica-like outcrops at their summits. Further
away skeleton trees hold up their gaunt, dead
arms, as if invoking a curse on the wombat-like
workers who tear out the sulphurous ore, which,
in smelting, killed them. Yes, and is killing the
workers too.
Nothing is too dreadful to happen in this home
of tragedy. In such a place Macbeth might have
met the witches, and those hags have boiled
their hell’s broth. Rain and fire and sulphur,
and sulphur and fire and rain never ending. You
taste and smell the sulphur always, and sometimes
it surrounds you in a mist. It settles on the rocks
and among tiie peat, and corrodes the iron roofs,
and it chokes up the human system. A nail left
in the water of the smelters a fortnight will be
coated with pure sulphur, and plumbers replacing
rain-pipes will find only the paint left. One day
when it leaves off raining the sulphur begins to
burn and the flame leaps along the peat and
travels across the bare rock, and then homes are
consumed, and for a time there is a little Day of
Judgment. This is an annual occurrence.
You realise when visiting such places how dearly
men earn wages. A doctor gives the smelter two
years to stand it. Fancy two years, at seven
shillings a day, for a lifetime of broken health and
a certain death of torture ! And what work do
they do? They look like demons as they feed
the furnaces, or tap the ore or the slag, or
run the slag-pots. But look in their faces and see
the dead and puffy, pasty skin, and dull eyes, and
the sweat coursing down in sooty streams, ana
you know they are human. _ The longer they work
at the game, the more certainly are they doomed
.—in Queenstown with sulphur, and in Zeehan
with lead. The feeders at the furnaces have the
worst of it, for they stand right among the flue-
dust, which is composed largely of arsenic and
lead. Those know, who have been leaded, what
this means; to others “being leaded” is just a
phrase. At some of these furnaces the heat is so
great that the leather of the boots scorches. # ,
One night I lay under the dump at Captain s
Flat and watched the burning slag stream down.
Such a sight might almost make one believe in
Hell, with molten fire being poured down to tor-
ture the wicked who have passed out. It was a
black, starless night, but as the slag-pots were
emptied above, and the contents came streaming
down like writhing serpents, or wolf-heads with
fangs closing inwards, they lit up the creek and
trees till it was light as day ; and then the molten
live heat would die away into the blackness of the
bank.
In Zeehan, one showed me how you can run
your finger through the molten ore as it comes
out of the tap of the syphon from the vat, and
then he told me of a sight lie had seen m Broken
Hill, before they had removed the smelters to
[Port Pine. .
“The floor of the dump is‘made,’” he said,
“ otherwise it would be too rough for the slag-pots
to run along. When the rough edge has to be
smoothed, a man is put on. He digs a trench
first, and the slag is poured in in its molten state.
One day, a fellow was put on to make the floor,
and while he cut the trench we wheeled the slag-
pots and left them standing by him. Then he
stood up and began to walk backwards, looking
at the trench as a man will look at his work.
Then I heard an awful yell, and I saw that he
had sat back in the slag-pot, and it had tipped up
and the stuff had run all over him. He only gave
one cry, and in 20 seconds he was dead. That is
one of the things I’ll never forget. When we
took off the cold slag it stripped the flesh to the
bone.” . , ..
Life in these mining towns is never dull.
Even the smelters, whose hard lives should breed
hatred of the place, never seem to be satisfied
away. They leave and go back again. They
have “ good times.”
One Saturday night I went to bed and woke
next morning with a hazy idea of a monotonous
sound as of the waves lapping the sea-beach, and
a memory of being wakened through the night.
Once I heard my door tried and a knock, and an
impatient voice which said, “Wha’s good er
knockin’! Open er door an’ drag him outer bed !
“ The door’s locked,” said somebody else.
“ Well, le’s burst the dum thing in. Say,
come an’ have a drink wi’ us ! ” Then “He mus
be asleep !” —and a fading away of sound down
the corridor. Beneath me there was an all-night
gambling-school, and further off there was an all-
night bar murmuring.
After breakfast I went to sit by the fire in a
remote parlor, and soon after a clay-soiled miner,
with his crib-bag, came in and sat down. Then
a couple of young fellows in their Sunday clothes
widened the fireside circle, and after that another
man, who had not yet changed his shift-clothes,
rambled unseeingly in and sat a dead weight into
a chair.
Our numbers increased, and then somebody
offered to throw someone else for drinks all round,
and after that somebody offered to tug-of-war
someone else on the floor for drinks all round, and
they tugged, while we dodged the travelling chairs
and tables, and a few stood on the piano; and
then the tug-of-war was interrupted by a man
falling in the fire, after which mostly everybody
left to tug in the yard.
But one man remained behind —a broad man,
who evidently had something to think out, or who
had been drunk the night before. And while he
sat dangling his unlit pipe in one hand, with his
eyes fixed on the fire, another man came in and
said: “ What’s amiss, old chap ? ”
Now, the thoughtful man did not understand
the solicitude of the other fellow, and he said
something nasty; but the other fellow held out
his hand magnanimously, and said, “If I’ve said
anything I’m sorry for, I’m glad of it.”
“ You yust holt your tunk and don’t insoolt me,
can’t yer?” said the thoughtful man, who evi-
dently hailed from the Baltic.
“Certainly,” said Number Two. “I’m a man
of peace, and if I’ve said anything disrespectable
I ’ll apologise. Not that I’m afraid of you, you
long-chinned son of a Skye terrier and Yamba-
coona bear. I suppose you ’re not the daring boy
who jumped on the back of a buffalo and plucked
hairs out of his horns, or who dragged up the
long piles of Chicago, and come from a country
where they fight Djin-Djins. Here, give me a
shake of your forefoot, and come and have a
drink ; but I don’t care whether I drink with
you or fight with you.”
And this is how the typical miners of those
two blisters on the landscape pass their time-
working hard, breathing poison, and drinking
hard, a little sport and a fight or two, and bar-
parlor barracking, which often leads to a fight.
You reach Queenstown through the gates of
Paradise. When you have passed through the
King River gorge, you think Queenstown is worth
visiting. The river is banked by bosoming myrtle-
spread hills and the sassafras, and there is also
the laurel and waratah and the wattle, which
light up the scene wonderfully when spring comes.
The river is for ever coming out of these velvety
bosoms, and each bend seems even more delightful
than the last. The line rises the further you get
up the stream, till you leave the gorge with the
river hundreds of feet beneath; and then you
follow along a gully that is equally beautiful till
you reach the summit of the range, after which
you come to the awful blackness of the Blasted
Valley, Queenstown. *
ROBERT BROTHERS.
Those who argue that the non-payment of
public school fees by the poorer classes of children
attaches no stigmato parentsor children, should ask
themselves why, if that be the case, the authorities
of Sydney University take such remarkable pains to
protect from the merest suspicion of being non-
paying, certain poorer class students of that insti-
tution. There are 11 bursaries in the University
devoted to students unable to pay their fees, and,
in order to keep the particular persons unidenti-
fiable, there is no record dealing with them avail-
able even to the Senate. The Chancellor is wholly
trusted in the matter, and his nominations ar
never known to either professors or stude:,r.-.
